The author of this book says: When I have completed for you, O my sons, this book, which is a part of medical science in its completeness, and have reached the utmost clarity and explanation in it, I saw fit to complete it for you with this treatise, which is a part of the practical work.
Because practical work is completely lacking in our country and in our time, so much so that its knowledge is almost extinct and its effect has ceased. And there remain only a few traces of it in the books of the ancients, which have been copied by hands and marred by error and confusion, until its meanings have become obscure and its benefit has been lost.
Therefore I saw fit to revive it and compose this treatise on it in a way that is explanatory, clear, and concise, and to include pictures of cauterizing irons and other surgical instruments, since this adds to the explanation and provides what is needed.
The reason why there is no skilled craftsman in our time is that the craft of medicine is lengthy, and its practitioner must first practice in the science of anatomy, as described by Galen. until he understands the functions, forms, temperaments, connections, and separations of the organs, and knows the bones, nerves, muscles, their number, and their origins, the arteries and veins, and the places of their origins.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are many in name but few in deed, especially in the craft of surgery, and we have mentioned some of this in the introduction to this book.
For he who is not knowledgeable in what we have mentioned concerning anatomy will inevitably fall into error and kill people thereby, as I have often seen in those who imagined themselves knowledgeable in this science and claimed it without knowledge or expertise.
For I saw an ignorant physician who incised a porcine tumor in a woman's neck, and severed some of the neck's arteries, so the woman bled until she fell dead before him.
And I saw another physician who proceeded to remove a stone from a man who had been wounded in the bladder; the stone was large, and he acted rashly, removing it with a piece of the bladder tissue. And the man died after about three days, and I had been called to remove it, and I saw from the size of the stone and the condition of the patient that I could have done it.
And I saw another physician who earned his living practicing medicine with some of the brothel keepers of our city.
Then a black boy came to him with a fracture in his leg near the heel, together with a wound.  The physician, in his ignorance, hastily bound the fracture over the wound with bandages and splints very tightly, and left no room for the wound to breathe.
Then he gave way to his desires, and left him for several days, and ordered him not to untie the bandage until his leg and foot swelled and he was near death.
Then I was called to him, and I hastened to untie the bandage; then he found relief and was freed from his pains, except that the corruption had become established in the limb and I could not prevent it.
Then the corruption continued to spread in the limb until he died.
And I saw another physician cauterize a cancerous tumor, which ulcerated after several days until the suffering of its owner became severe.  This is because if a cancer is purely of a black humor, then it should not be touched with iron at all.
Unless it is in a limb that can be entirely excised.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual work is divided into two parts: work accompanied by safety, and work accompanied by harm in most cases.
And I have warned, in every place that it comes in this book, against work which involves risk and danger, and you should avoid and reject it.
Lest the ignorant find a way to criticize and attack, take for yourselves caution and circumspection, and for your patients gentleness and deliberation.
And use the best method leading to safety and a good outcome, and avoid dangerous diseases that are difficult to cure.
And keep yourselves clear from what you fear might cast doubt on your religion and your worldly affairs; for that is more lasting for your reputation and higher for your standing in this world and the next.
For Galen said in some of his precepts: Do not treat a bad disease, lest you be called bad physicians.
This chapter is divided into three sections.
The first section.
Concerning cautery with fire and cautery with a strong medicine, arranged systematically from head to foot, with illustrations of the instruments and cautery irons and everything needed for the work by hand.
And the second section.
Concerning incision, puncture, bloodletting, cupping, wounds, and the extraction of arrows and the like, all arranged systematically, with illustrations of the instruments.
And the third section.
Concerning setting [bones], reduction of dislocations, treatment of fractures and the like, arranged systematically from head to foot, with illustrations of the instruments.
The first section.
Concerning cauterization.
And before we mention its use, it is necessary to mention its benefits and harms, and in what temperament it is used.
So I say that the discussion of the benefit and harm of cauterization is lengthy discourse, a precise science, and a hidden secret, and a number of wise men have spoken about it.
And they differed in it, and I have shortened their words for fear of lengthening.
So I say that cauterization generally benefits every bad temperament that is with a material thing and without a material thing, except for two temperaments: the hot temperament without material and the dry temperament without material.
As for the dry temperament with material, they differed in it. Some said that cauterization is beneficial in it, and others said the opposite, that cauterization is not suitable for a disease that is from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
And it is impossible to cure a hot dry disease with a hot dry medicine. And he who says the opposite says that cauterization with fire may benefit from a hot dry disease that occurs in the bodies of people. Because when you add the body of man and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the body of man cold.
And I say what he says, because experience has revealed that to me many times, except that one should not imagine that matter except one who has practiced and trained in the matter of cauterization a thorough training. And he has observed the difference in the temperaments of people and the state of diseases in themselves, their causes, their symptoms, and their duration.
But as for the other temperaments, there is no fear from them, especially cold and moist diseases, for all physicians have agreed upon them and have not differed in the benefit of cauterization in them.
And know, O my sons, that among the secrets of cauterization and its superiority over cauterization with a burning medicine is that fire is a simple element whose action does not exceed the member that is cauterized, nor does it harm another member connected to it except a little harm.
And cauterization with a burning medicine may exceed its effect to what is beyond the members, and perhaps it causes in the member a disease difficult to treat, and perhaps it kills, and fire, for its nobility and the nobility of its essence, does not do that except if one exaggerates.
And this has become clear to us through experience, long service, attention to the craft, and understanding of the realities of the matter.
For this reason I have dispensed with lengthy speech, and were it not that it is not befitting my book, I would have presented to you concerning fire a hidden secret and the manner of its action in bodies and its expulsion of diseases with philosophical, demonstrative speech too subtle for your understanding.
And know, O my sons, that they have differed concerning the time suitable for cauterization, and they have considered the best time to be springtime.
And I say that cauterization is suitable at every time, because the harm occurring from the time is consumed by the benefit which is brought about by cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for strong, necessary, urgent pains which do not tolerate delay, for what is feared from them is that they may be followed by a calamity which is greater than the slight harm resulting from the time.
And let it not occur to you, O my sons, what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine, that cauterization which cures a certain disease will never recur, and that you should make it a preventative.
And the matter is not as they suppose, because cauterization is only in the position of a medicine which changes the temperament and dries the humors which are the cause of the pains.
Except that cauterization is preferable to medicine in the speed of its success, the strength of its action, and the intensity of its power.
And it is possible that the disease may return after a time, according to the temperament of the patient, the strength of his disease, and what is prepared in his body from the gathering of humors in it, and his negligence in acquiring them from foods and similar causes.
Except that the disease in which cauterization is used is a mild disease and in a member with few humors and moistures. Like cauterizing a tooth for pain and the like; it is possible that that pain may not return, and that is in the least.
And as for the common people's saying also that cauterization is the last of medicine, it is a true saying, not to what they go to, for they believe that no treatment is beneficial with medicine or otherwise after cauterization has occurred, and the matter is otherwise.
And the meaning of cauterization being the last of medicine is only that when we have used types of treatment in a disease and those medicines have not been effective, then we use cauterization as the last thing, and it is effective. From here it is said that cauterization is the last of medicine. Not in the sense to which the common people and many ignorant physicians have gone.
The ancients mentioned that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they said that because of the moderation of gold and the nobility of its essence, and they said that the place of cauterization does not suppurate.  This is not always the case.
Because I have tried that and found that it only does that in some bodies and not others, and cauterization with it is better and more preferable than with iron, as they said, except that if you heat the cautery iron in the fire [made of] gold, you cannot determine when it is heated to the degree you want because of the redness of the gold.
And because it quickly becomes cold, and if you increase the heat, it melts in the fire and is poured out, causing the craftsman trouble. Therefore, cauterization with iron is, for us, quicker and closer to correctness in practice, God willing.
I have arranged this chapter on cauterization into sections that I have organized from head to foot, to make it easier for the student to find what he wants, God willing.
The first chapter.
Cauterization of the head, one cauterization.
This cauterization benefits from the excess of moisture and cold in the brain, which are the cause of headache, frequent colds from the head to the region of the eyes and ears, excessive sleep, and toothaches and throat pains. And in general, for every disease that arises from cold, such as paralysis, epilepsy, and aphasia, and similar diseases.
The method of this cauterization is that you first order the patient to vomit with a purgative medicine that cleanses the head for three or four nights, according to the strength, age, and habit of the patient.
Then you order him to shave his head with a razor, then you seat him before you cross-legged, having placed his hands on his chest.
Then you place the base of your palm on the base of his nose between his eyes; where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-shaped cautery, which is of this form.
Let the bone be uncovered to the extent of the head of a clove or the size of a carob seed; then raise your hand, otherwise replace your hand with the same or another iron if it has cooled. Until you see of the bone what I have mentioned to you; then take some salt, dissolve it in water, soak a cotton swab in it, apply it to the area, and leave it for three days.
Then apply a cotton swab soaked in butter to it and leave it there until the scab from the cautery is gone; then treat it with the four-ingredient ointment until it heals, God willing.
And they have said that the wound, the longer it remains open, the more pus it discharges, and that is better and more beneficial.
And some have mentioned cauterizing the skin to the bone, holding the cautery until some of the thickness of the bone is burned, then removing what has been burned from the bone afterwards, and then treating it.
And others said that one should exaggerate with the cautery until it strongly affects the bone, until it falls from the bone in the form of a carat or a small disc, and they claimed that this vents the vapors of the head from that area, leaving the wound open for a long time.
Then it is treated until it heals.  I do not see these two types of cautery at all except in some people and in a haphazard way; in my opinion, leaving it alone is better and safer.  If there is healing, for the head weakens whenever its natural connection is disrupted, as we have seen in other organs.
Especially if the patient's head is naturally weak. The first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion, and I use it; act accordingly and you will be safe, God willing.
Chapter Two.
Also on cauterizing the head.
If a chronic pain occurs in the head and persists in the patient, and applications, gargles, snuffs, ointments, and dressings have been used, especially if a single cauterization, as we have described, has been performed without benefit, then look. If the patient's head is naturally strong and not weak, and he feels intense cold, cauterize him again a little above the previous cauterization. Then cauterize each horn of his head until the thickness of the skin is gone and the amount of bone we described is exposed. And cauterize him at the back of his head in the place known as the axe, and lighten your hand in this, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will find this very painful, unlike the pain of all other head cauterizations. I will mention this cauterization in its place.
The cautery used for the horns of the head and the back of the head should be gentler than the cautery used for the middle of the head.  This is its image.
Chapter Three.
On cauterizing non-chronic migraine.
If pain occurs in a fissure of the head, accompanied by headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then purge the patient with head-purifying medicines and use all the treatments I mentioned in the divisions of diseases. If this does not work, then cauterization is done in two ways. Either cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine or with iron.
As for cauterization with a burning medicine, take a single clove of garlic, peel it, and cut its ends on both sides. Then, incise the place of pain in the temple with a wide scalpel until there is a space under the skin large enough for the clove.
Then insert it under the skin until it disappears, then tie it tightly with bandages, and leave it for about fifteen hours. Then untie it, remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, then dress it.
Apply cotton soaked in butter until the area suppurates, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.  If you wish, you can do this with some of the burning medicines which I mentioned in the eighteenth chapter on burning medicines.
As for cauterization with iron, it is in this manner: heat the cautery iron, which is pictured thus:
It is called the nail cautery because its head is like the head of a nail, somewhat perforated, and with a small projection in the middle.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while you rotate the iron little by little, and the amount you burn should be about half the thickness of the skin. Lift your hand so as not to burn the artery underneath and cause bleeding, then soak cotton in salt water and apply it to the area and leave it for three days. Then dress the cotton with butter, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize this migraine with the knife-like end of the cautery, and this preserves you from cutting the artery in this chronic migraine in particular.
Chapter Four.
Incision of chronic migraine.
If you treat the migraine with what we have mentioned of the aforementioned treatment and what we mentioned in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment does not work, and you see from the illness what is not remedied by what we mentioned of the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire. Then you should heat the knife-like cautery until it whitens, after you have marked the painful area with a line half a fingerbreadth long or so, and you should let go your hand once, while you are pulling it, until you sever the artery and reach toward the bone.
But you should be cautious about touching the jaw which moves when chewing, lest you burn the muscle or the nerve which moves it, and thus cause spasms.
And be cautious and watchful of bleeding from the artery which you cut, for there is danger in cutting it. Especially for one who is ignorant of what he does and is not a practiced expert; and abandoning the work is preferable.
And the treatment of bleeding that occurs from the artery will be mentioned in its place in the book, God willing; if you see from the condition something which this cauterization does not remedy.
And you saw the patient's body able to tolerate it, cauterize in the middle of the head as we described and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, use the cauterization which we mentioned in the chapter on the incision of the artery with the double-bladed cautery; for it is a better and more effective cauterization than this.
Chapter Five.
On cauterizing the pains of the ears.
If a pain occurs in the ear due to cold, and it is treated with purgatives and all the treatments we mentioned in the classification, and the pain does not go away, then heat the cautery called the point, which is of this shape.
Then, after heating it, prick with it around the whole ear, as it goes around, or around both if the pain is in both. And keep the cauterization a little away from the root of the ear, after marking the place with ink. The cauterization should be about ten points in each ear or thereabouts.  Then treat the places until they heal, God willing.
Chapter Six.
On cauterizing the abscess.
The abscess which is treated by cauterization is of the type which arises from phlegm, as I mentioned in the classification of diseases.
And one should avoid cauterizing the type which arises from dryness and nerve tension.
When you have treated this type of abscess with poultices, snuffs, and gargles and your treatment has been ineffective, then it is necessary that the patient be cauterized with three cauterizations. One at the root of the ear, the second a little below his temple, and the third at the junction of the lips.
And make your cauterization on the opposite side from the diseased side, for relaxation occurs on the side which appears healthy.
The form of the cauterization is that you cauterize with a cauterization opposite the upper tip of the ear, a little below the horn of the head, and another in the temple, and its length should be the length of the thumb.
Lower your hand with the cautery until you burn about half the thickness of the skin. This is the image of the cautery; it is a type of scalpel, the image of which has preceded, but it is a little gentler than it, as you see. The scalpel should be a little thicker than usual. Then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
Cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
If the apoplexy becomes chronic and you treat it with what we have mentioned and your treatment is not effective and the patient does not have a fever, cauterize him in four places. One cauterization on each temple, one in the middle of the head as we mentioned, and one at the back of the head as previously described.
The prescription for the cautery is as previously mentioned.  It may also be cauterized on the stomach, which will be more effective, then treat as before.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterizing forgetfulness.
If it is from phlegm, the patient should first be given strong purgatives and brain-cleansing pills.
Then his whole head is shaved and a mustard plaster, as described in the chapter on plasters, is applied to the back of his head repeatedly, for this is a kind of cautery.
Do this in the very order I mentioned there; if he is cured by this, well and good; otherwise, perform three cauterizations on the back of his head, arranged in a row from the top of the head to the bottom of the neck.
Make the space between each cauterization the width of a finger. Then treat the cauterization as described above. If you wish to increase [the number of cauterizations] and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle.
If you wish to increase [the number of cauterizations], cauterize on the temples, then treat it until it heals. The cautery should be olive-shaped, as previously described.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterizing paralysis and general bodily relaxation.
It is necessary that you first proceed to purify the head with purgatives and what we have mentioned. Then shave the patient's head, then cauterize: one cauterization in the middle of the head, one on each temple, and one on the back of the head.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck. If, in a case of bodily relaxation, you need more than that, and the patient can tolerate it and the illness is strong and persistent, then cauterize with four cauterizations on the vertebrae of his back. And continue the cauterization until you burn most of the skin, then lift your hand, and then treat him as previously mentioned until he heals, God willing.  The cautery should be olive-shaped.
Chapter Ten.
Cauterization for Epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is caused by phlegm should be cauterized.  His brain should first be purged with strong purgatives and with all the other treatments we mentioned in the classification, if the patient is an adult and can tolerate taking medicines.
But if he is a child who cannot tolerate medicines, then use gargles and masticatories that cleanse the brain many days beforehand, along with improving his diet.
Then shave his head and cauterize him with one cautery in the middle of the head, as previously described, and another cautery at the back of his head. And one cautery on each temple. If the patient is strong and can tolerate it, cauterize him with the cauteries mentioned for the treatment of paralysis and body relaxation, on the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back; the cautery irons should be olive-shaped, as previously described.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery iron gentle in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is corrupt humors and thick phlegm, cauterize with the cauteries we mentioned for the paralytic.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess inclined toward black bile and the patient's body is moist, purge him with what cleanses the brain, as previously described in the classification.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tightly woven linen cake in the shape of a circle, then place it in the middle of his head while the seated patient holds it in place from all sides, then take one pound of old sheep tallow.
Then heat it over a fire to a moderate temperature, as much as the finger can bear when inserted, then pour it into the middle of his head inside the circle and leave it until it cools. Do this for the patient once a week, along with his other good treatment, until he is healed, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small punctures, without holding the cautery in your hand but rather by sprinkling it, for this type of cautery moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a cotton ball soaked in tallow or chicken fat to it.
Chapter Twelve.
On cauterizing a runny eye.
If you ascertain the beginning of a runny eye with the signs I mentioned in the classification, then act quickly, purge the patient of what cleanses his head and protect him from all humors, and sweat him in the bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and cauterize him in the middle of the head, then cauterize him twice on the temples if the onset of the runny eye is in both eyes, or on one side if it is in one eye.
Then cut with the cautery all the veins and arteries which are under the skin, and let the cauterizations be lengthwise across the temples, and guard against bleeding.
If you see any of it, cut it off immediately with whatever treatment is possible for you, and we will come to the wisdom in cauterizing and cutting arteries and guarding against bleeding.
And he may be cauterized in the nape of the neck under the two bones with two strong cauterizations.
Chapter Thirteen.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic weeping sores.
If the weeping sores of the eye are chronic and persistent, and are from the veins and arteries on the surface of the head, from outside the head, and you are certain that it is from cold, thick, phlegmatic superfluities, then cauterize it with the cauterization that I described in the beginning for the descending water. One cauterization in the middle of the head, and two cauterizations on the temples, and two cauterizations in the nape of the neck under the two bones. And if you need more, then cauterize one cauterization on each side of the corner of the eye at the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter Fourteen.
Concerning the cauterization of a foul-smelling nose.
If you treat it with what we mentioned in the division and the treatment is not effective, then immediately purge the patient with colocynth for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle with the olive-shaped cautery. Then cauterize with the nail-shaped cautery two cauterizations above the eyebrows, slightly under the hair, and be careful of the artery, do not cut it. And this is the image of the nail-shaped cautery.
Chapter Fifteen.
Concerning the cauterization of a relaxed eyelid.
If an eyelid relaxes due to illness or moisture, cauterize the eyelid with one cauterization using this crescent-shaped cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize slightly above the eyebrows, two cauterizations on each side, keeping a distance from the temples, and let the length of each cauterization be the length of the eyebrow.
Do not exaggerate with the cauterization, but only to the extent that a third of the skin is burned, and the shape of the cautery is like this.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If its margins are inverted inwards so that the eye is pushed in, then cauterization is of two types. Either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a burning medicine. As for cauterization with fire, you should order the patient beforehand to leave his eyelashes alone, if he is one who plucks them, until they grow long and even.
If it is pushed in while they are growing, then tie his eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until they grow. When they have grown and become even, place the patient's head in your lap, then mark the eyelid with ink in the shape of an upside down ‘āsin’.
The beginning of the mark should be near the margins. Then place under the eyelid a cotton wool pledget soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a lizard. Then heat a cautery iron; this is its shape.
Then cauterize following the marked shape little by little, many times, until the surface of the skin, which is in the shape of the upside down ‘āsin’, is completely burnt, especially its surface. A sign that your work is successful is to see the eyelid curled and the eyelashes raised from the conjunctiva of the eye.
Then raise your hand and leave it for three days. Then apply a cotton pledget with butter to it until the eschar separates.  Then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If some hair returns after a while and the eyelid relaxes,
repeat the cauterization in that place as you did before. If the hair is in the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and the hair does not prick the eye. As for cauterization with a corrosive medicine, you should instruct the patient to leave the margins until they lengthen and become even.
Then make from paper the shape of an upside-down ‘āsin’. Then take of the usual soap and of unslaked lime, of each one a drachm or thereabouts, and grind them together very finely. Do this quickly lest it dry. Then spread some of it on the paper you made in the shape of the upside-down ‘āsin’ and place it on one or both eyelids. Place a cotton pledget soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger on top of the medicine and press it gently, moving it whenever the patient feels the sting of the medicine, for he will feel a burning sensation. As long as he feels the burning, leave the medicine in place and move it with your finger. When the burning ceases, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
Observe, and if you see the eyelid raised as it is raised by burning or cutting, then it is finished. Otherwise, apply more medicine to the place where the medicine has not taken effect and has not blackened, until you complete your work and the eyelid is raised.  Then apply cotton with butter to it until the scab from the burning is removed.
Then treat it with emollient ointment or another until it heals. When working, you should be extremely careful that none of the medicine falls into the eye. If the eyelid relaxes after a few days and something like hair is suspected in the eye,
reapply the medicine to that part of the eyelid that has relaxed, in the same way as before, then treat it until it heals. This is the shape of the upside-down ‘āsin’.
Know that people's eyes differ in size, so your cauterization should be adjusted accordingly. The correct method is not hidden from one who is experienced in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cauterizing the polyp that appears in the corner of the eye.
If you treat the polyp with what we mentioned in the classification of diseases and your treatment is not effective, then it should be cauterized in this manner.
Order the patient to place his head in your lap, and have a servant hold his head in your hands so that he does not move or shake his head.
Then, place a cotton ball soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a frog on his eyes, then heat the cautery iron, which is depicted as follows.
It should be hollow like the quill of an eagle's feather at one end, which is the end used for cauterization; and if you wish, it can be perforated at the other end, or if you wish it can be solid like an awl, but the hollow one is better for your work, God willing.
Then, squeeze the polyp if it is open and remove its contents and dry it; or if it is not open, open it and remove its pus. Then, at that time, apply the very hot cautery iron to it, holding it with your hand until you reach the bone.
And move your hand slightly away during cauterization from the eye towards the nose, lest your hand slip or the patient move, and the cautery iron fall on the conjunctiva and damage it. If you reach the bone with the first cauterization, well and good; otherwise, reapply the cautery iron a second time if needed.
Leave it for three days, then apply cotton soaked in butter and treat it with drying ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, apply a strong, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and clean it as will be described in its chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing a polyp, mentioned by some of the ancients:  You should aim at the location of the polyp, incise it, then place a thin cone in the incision itself; this is its image.
Pour into it the weight of a dirham of molten lead, and hold your hand firmly on the cone, and the patient must not move at all, lest the molten lead flow into his eye.  It is necessary to place cotton soaked in egg white or water on the patient's eye.
For the lead will burn the polyp and heal it wonderfully. If the polyp heals with the cauterization and treatment mentioned, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to use the perforation of the nose and return the polyp to the nasal passage, as will be described in its appropriate place.
Chapter Eighteen.
On cauterizing a cleft lip.
A cleft often occurs in the lip, called a harelip, especially in the lips of children; it frequently occurs. If you treat this cleft with what we have mentioned in the section and the treatment is not effective, then heat a small, knife-like cautery of this form.
Its cavity should be as thin as a knife, then apply it hot quickly to the cleft itself until the cauterization reaches the depth of the cleft, then treat it with the quirotu until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
On cauterizing a fistula occurring in the mouth.
If a swelling appears at the root of the gums, or in the palate, or at the roots of the molars, then it suppurates and bursts, and a fistula results from the flow of pus, and you treat it but the treatment is not effective, then you should heat a cautery to the extent that it fits into the fistula.
Then insert it hot into the opening of the fistula, and hold your hand until the heated iron reaches its bottom and end. Do this once or twice, then treat it afterwards with what we have mentioned of treatments until it heals, God willing.
If the discharge stops and it heals, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to examine the place and remove the decayed bone, according to what will come in its chapter, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
Concerning the loosening of the molars and gums.
If the gums become loose from moisture and the molars become mobile, and you treat them with medications and they do not work, then place the patient's head in your lap, then heat the cautery iron, the image of which comes after this, after you place the cannula on the tooth.
Then insert the hot cautery iron quickly and hold your hand a little until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth, then raise your hand, then repeat the cautery several times as you wish, then let the patient fill his mouth with salt water.
And he holds it for an hour and spits it out; then the mobile tooth will be fixed, and the loose gum will tighten, and the foul moisture will dry.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In cauterizing the toothache.
If the toothache is from cold or there are worms in it and medication has not worked, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with fat or cauterization with fire.
As for cauterization with fat, you take cow's fat and boil it in an iron spoon or a shell, then take cotton, wrap it around the end of a probe, then dip it in the boiled fat and quickly place it on the aching tooth and hold it until it cools.
Then repeat it several times until the strength of the heat reaches the root of the tooth.  And if you wish, you can dip wool or cotton in cold fat and place it on the aching tooth and put a heated iron on top of it until the heat reaches the bottom of the tooth.
As for cauterization with fire, take a copper or iron tube, and let it be somewhat thick so that the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth. Then heat the cautery iron, the image of which is shown, and place it on the tooth itself, and hold your hand until the cautery iron cools. Do this repeatedly.
Then the pain will go away, either that same day or another day. After the cauterization, the patient should fill his mouth with good fat and hold it for an hour, then spit it out.  This is the image of the cautery iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you wish, and according to what is possible. This is the image of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-two.
On cauterizing fistulas.
If fistulas are caused by phlegm and cold humors and do not respond to ripening medications, and you desire quick ripening, then heat a hollow cautery iron, the image of which is shown here, which is perforated at both ends.
So that smoke exits from the other end during cauterization, place it carefully on the tumor itself once or twice, if necessary, until you reach the depth of the tumor. If the tumor is small, make the cautery iron proportionate to the tumor.
Then leave it for three days and apply cotton soaked in butter to it until what was burned by the fire is removed.  Then treat it with ointment and tents until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
On cauterization for hoarseness and shortness of breath.
If humors overwhelm the windpipe, especially if this is accompanied by a cold temperament, then the patient should first be purged with purgative medicines. Then cauterize with a cautery iron in the hollow of the neck at the root of the throat in the lower location.
Beware of reaching the throat with the cauterization and do not burn more than half of the skin. Then cauterize another time at the joint of the neck at the end of its last vertebra, forcefully. And let the cautery iron be nail-shaped in the manner previously described.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On cauterization for lung disease and cough.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold humors, and the patient has no fever or consumption, and the disease is chronic, then cauterize two times above the clavicles in the low, soft, empty places, and another time in the middle of the chest between the breasts.
And let the cautery be nail-shaped, in the manner previously described. And if you wish, let your cauterization be by puncturing with the cautery called the “point,” the image of which has been previously shown in puncturing earache, and let the punctures be from thirty points or thereabouts.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he recovers. You may also make a cautery with three branches in this shape, and hasten the cauterization with it because you cauterize three times in one go with it. This is the image of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus is dislocated because of slippery humors, or if it does not remain fixed when it is reduced from its dislocation, so that it becomes habitual for it to be reduced and then dislocate at the slightest movement it is subjected to, as we have witnessed, then it is necessary to reduce the joint first. Then the patient lies on his back or on his healthy side, and then the skin inside the armpit is raised upwards with the fingers of your left hand if the joint is dislocated inwards.
Then you heat the cautery iron with two heads, which is of this form.
Then you cauterize the skin with it until it penetrates to the other side, and the form of the cauterization is four cauterizations.  It may be cauterized with a cautery iron with three heads; then the form of the cauterization is six cauterizations. The heads should be thin as a reed.
And this is the image of the cautery iron with three heads.
And this number may be increased by one, so that the cauterizations are eight. Then, pounded balls with salt are applied to the cauterization. Then, the patient is kept quiet and does not move the limb for a time until it strengthens. If the dislocation is upwards, which is rarely the case.
Cauterize above the shoulder with one good, nail-like cauterization, or numerous cauterizations in a dotted pattern; for the joint will be strengthened, the moisture will leave, and the patient will heal.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In cauterizing the stomach.
If coldness and excessive moisture occur in the stomach, deranging its temperament, and if many catarrhal discharges occur in it, and it is treated with various remedies to no avail, then the patient should lie on his back, extending his legs and arms, and then three cauterizations should be performed. One cauterization should be made below the pit of the stomach, the size of a finger, with a nail-like cautery; and two cauterizations lower down, on either side of the single cauterization, so that the cauterizations form a triangle, and sufficiently far apart that they do not coalesce if they suppurate. The depth of the cautery should be about two-thirds the thickness of the skin.  This will be the shape of the cauterizations.
In this manner and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with one large cauterization in the middle of the stomach, in this manner.
And the cautery iron with which you perform this single cauterization should be in the shape of a circle, in this manner.
And the stomach may be cauterized by puncturing it for someone who shrinks from this cauterization. This is done by marking points on the stomach with ink to the extent desired, then cauterizing them with a pointed cautery, then treating it with the previously described treatment until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the cold cauterization of the liver.
If there arises from the liver a pain due to coldness and dampness or due to a slight wind, so that it has departed from its natural temperament excessively, and the patient is treated with what we mentioned in the classification, and that does not work. Then the patient should lie on his back, and three cauterizations should be marked with ink in this shape and this exact amount on the liver below the diaphragm, where the human elbow ends.
And after each cauterization there will be a cauterization of the thickness of a finger, and the cauterization will be along the body straight, and do not press down firmly with the cautery.
Let the amount burned of the thickness of the skin be half of it, not more, and let the patient stand on his feet; if he is not standing, let him lie down with his legs extended and his arms raised. And this is the image of the cautery.
And you may be able to cauterize these cauterizations with the knife-cautery if you have gentleness and skill in the craft, and be careful lest you overdo the cauterization and burn all the skin, burning the abdomen and reaching the intestines, for the skin there is thin, so know that.
Chapter twenty-eight.
On cauterizing a swelling of the liver.
If an abscess appears in the liver, and you wish to know if that swelling is in the flesh of the liver or in its peritoneum, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient will feel heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the peritoneum of the liver, there will be intense sharpness with the pain, and if you see that the physicians have been unable to treat it, then the patient should lie on his back, and then you should mark the swollen area with ink.
Then heat the cautery in the fire; it is the cautery that resembles a hoe, and this is its form. And cauterize it with one cauterization until you burn all the skin and the cauterization reaches the peritoneum so that all the matter comes out, then treat it with the treatment of abscesses until it heals.
And this type of cauterization should not be used except by one whose experience in the art of medicine is long and who has repeatedly treated these diseases; then he may undertake such work, but leaving it is better in my opinion.
Chapter twenty-nine.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients mentioned cauterization with the roots of rhubarb for a cold fistula in this manner: that you take one long dry root of rhubarb, the longest you can find, and let it be about the thickness of a finger, then dip it in oil and hold it in the fire. Then cauterize with one cauterization between the connection of the clavicle and the neck, and two small cauterizations a little below the jugulars, slightly inclined toward the side under the chin, and two large cauterizations above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two cauterizations also between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly inclined backward, and another cauterization in the middle of the chest, and another above the stomach, and three cauterizations from behind: one between the shoulders, and two on the sides of the loins, lower than the cauterization which is between the shoulders.
And you should not deepen your hand with the cautery, but it should be superficially on the skin like a fumigation.  Some of the ancients mentioned that some people used a cauterizing iron like a file, heating it and inserting it between the ribs until it reaches the tumor itself.
And the pus is removed as we mentioned in the tumor of the liver. In this method of cauterization, there is great danger; either the patient dies immediately, or a fistula forms in the place which will not heal.
Chapter Thirty.
On cauterizing the spleen.
If you treat the disease of the spleen with what we have mentioned in the classification and your treatment is not effective, then cauterization is done in three ways, all of which are correct. One is to cauterize it with three or four cauterizations lined up along the length of the spleen.
In the shape of the liver cauterizations whose form we have presented, and between each cauterization and another there should be the width of a finger or slightly more. The nature of the cautery should be the same as that which we mentioned in the cauterization of the liver.  Do not deepen your hand with the cautery, and the patient's position should be lying on his back.
The second method is in the armpit.  You raise the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left armpit ends, and you raise the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left armpit ends. Your raising of the skin should be across the width of the body so that the cauterizations fall along the length of the body.
Then you insert two cautery irons, very carefully, until they penetrate the skin on the other side, then you remove the cautery iron.  The cauterizations will then be four. And if you wish, you may cauterize with the other cautery iron with three points.
Then you treat the site of the cauterization after you leave it to drain pus for many days; this is more effective than all the treatments that have preceded it.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On cauterization for dropsy.
Cauterization is only beneficial in particular for ascitic dropsy. If you treat the dropsical patient with the types of treatment we mentioned in the classification, and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize him with four cauterizations around the navel and one cauterization on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver, a cauterization on the spleen, and two cauterizations on his back between the vertebrae, one opposite his chest and another opposite his stomach. The depth of the cauterization should be approximately the thickness of the skin. Then you leave the cauterization open to drain pus for a long time.
And do not deprive the patient of treatment after cauterization with what is necessary to combine the two purposes, so that healing hastens to him.  The shape of the cautery iron with which the back is cauterized is nail-shaped, as its shape was described previously, and that with which the abdomen is cauterized is olive-shaped.
Chapter thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If they swell in dropsy and fill with yellow water, then it is necessary to cauterize on the back of the foot in the hollow between the little toe and the ring finger.
And place your hand with the cautery iron and do not bend it, then raise your hand and do not return it at all, for the yellow water will ooze out, and the cautery iron is of this shape.
Then cauterize on the legs, two cauterizations on each leg. The cauterization should be with the sharp end of the cautery iron, and the cauterization should be along the length of the leg, one below the knee and another below it towards the middle of the leg, and on each thigh two cauterizations.
And leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time, so that the water may ooze out, then treat it with all that we have mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On cauterization for diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is from cold and dampness, to the point that it weakens the retaining and digestive powers in the stomach and intestines, and this has been treated with various treatments but has not healed, and you see the patient able to tolerate cauterization and strong, then cauterize him with a large cauterization on his stomach. As was mentioned previously in cauterizing the stomach with the circular cautery iron, and four cauterizations around the navel with a gentle, nail-shaped cautery iron, and a large cauterization on the coccyx with cotton, or two cauterizations.
If you see the dampness is abundant and the patient can tolerate it, then cauterize him on the pubis and on each flank, and perhaps add two small cauterizations on the stomach near the large one, for it is a successful treatment whose benefit is not missed, God willing.
Chapter thirty-four.
On cauterizing hemorrhoids of the anus.
If there are chronic, numerous, or single hemorrhoids in the anus, and they are from thick, cold humors or putrid moisture, and they have been treated with what we mentioned in the section, and the treatment has not been effective, then cauterize the patient with three cauterizations on the lower part of the sacrum below the coccyx a little, forming a triangle.
And a cauterization below the navel by the length of two fingers; and if you judge that his stomach has become cold and his food is not digested, and you see his face swollen, then cauterize him on the stomach with a large cauterization as previously mentioned, and a cauterization on his liver. And another on his spleen with a nail-shaped cautery, and leave the cautery open for a time, then treat him until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On cauterizing warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, heat the cautery which resembles a needle, then insert it hot into the same cut wart and persevere until the cautery reaches the mouth of the vein from which the blood flows; do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one as we have described, then treat them with appropriate ointments until they heal.  And if you cauterize a large area with cotton, it will be more effective.
Chapter Thirty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula which is in the anus and its surroundings.
If the patient does not consent to the incision and the procedure we described in its place and shrinks from it, then perhaps he will heal by cauterization. If a fistula occurs and the pus and corrupt humors flow from it for a long time, then the first thing you should do is measure it with a thin probe.
Then ascertain the depth of its channel with a probe. Then heat the cautery iron which resembles a needle, and insert it, hot, into the fistula itself, along the course of the fistula and to the depth that the probe reached, and repeat the cauterization until all those corrupt substances are burned, once or twice or three times, according to your need.
And be careful not to burn a nerve if there is one, or a large vein, and if the fistula opens into the substance of the bladder or into the substance of the intestine.
Therefore, be careful of all these areas.  You should only do this if the fistula is in a fleshy area and you have assessed that it is not penetrating. Then treat the area until it heals. If the area closes and the flow stops and remains so for a time, then know that it has healed completely.
And if the flow does not stop, then know that it is penetrating, or that in its channel there is a corrupt bone, or something similar, which will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On cauterizing the kidneys.
If pain occurs in the kidneys due to cold or a strong wind, and this causes a decrease in the patient's sexual activity, then it should be cauterized on the loins, over the kidneys themselves.  Cauterize each kidney with the nail-shaped cautery mentioned previously. And perhaps you should cauterize it a third time in the same place. So three cauterizations are applied in a row, and this will be more effective.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On cauterizing the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation occur in the bladder due to cold and moisture, so that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize it: one cauterization below the navel, on the bladder where the pubic hair begins, and one cauterization to the right of the navel, and another to the left.
The distance after cauterization on each side should be the width of a thumb. And cauterize one or two times in the lower back, if needed. The cautery should be nail-shaped, as previously mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On cauterizing the womb.
If a substance forms in the womb due to cold and moisture, thus preventing conception, corrupting its substance, and hindering menstruation or causing pain during its arrival, then three cauterizations should be made around the navel, as mentioned in cauterizing the bladder. One or two cauterizations on cotton in the lower back, and the cautery should be nail-shaped.
Chapter Forty.
On cauterizing a dislocated hip.
Humoral fluids may accumulate in the hip joint, causing it to dislocate.  The sign is that one leg will appear longer than the other when they are compared, and the site of the dislocation will feel empty.  Therefore, the patient should be cauterized in a semi-circular fashion directly over the hip joint itself.
After marking with ink around the joint in a circular fashion so that the joint itself lies in the center of the circle, use the cautery shown in the illustration for cauterizing the stomach. If you do not have this cautery, use three cauterizations with a large olive-shaped cautery.
Make the cauterization deep enough to penetrate the entire thickness of the skin, then treat [the wound] until it heals, God willing.
Chapter forty-one.
On cauterizing sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint due to cold and dampness, and you have treated the patient with what we mentioned in the classification, but the treatment has not been effective and the condition has become chronic, then it is necessary to evacuate the thick humors from the patient using foul-smelling beans or prickly pear fruit, or something similar.
Then shave it and apply cautery in two ways: either cautery with burning medicines or cautery with fire. Cautery with fire can be done in many ways; one of them is to cauterize the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations in this manner.
Make them of a suitable depth, and the distance between each cauterization should be the width of a finger.  The cautery iron should be olive-shaped.  Alternatively, you may cauterize one in the middle, on the head of the hip itself, making four cauterizations.  Or, if you wish, cauterize it with the circle previously mentioned in cauterizing a dislocated hip, as a single cauterization.
So that the circle encompasses the entire hip and burns through the entire thickness of the skin. This is the method of cauterization for sciatica if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize two areas on the thigh where the patient indicates pain, and one above the malleolus, four fingers towards the lateral side, slightly. The cautery iron should be knife-shaped, and the depth of the cauterization should only be the thickness of the skin.
And if he indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize where he indicates with a pointed cautery iron, three or four times, or more if necessary. And if he indicates pain below the knee towards the leg, cauterize there with one knife-shaped cauterization.
And be careful in all your cauterizations not to burn a nerve or a large artery, lest you cause the patient a serious or lasting harm.
And I have seen one and another who was cauterized above the malleolus, and the cauterization was excessive, so that the leg became gangrenous until the gangrene reached the foot and perforated it entirely, and the whole leg became corrupted, and then diarrhea and death followed. If the pain is in both sides, cauterize both in the same manner.
Some learned physicians have mentioned a cauterization of the hip of this kind:  A cup-like instrument is made of iron, its diameter half a span, and its mouth the thickness of a date pit, or a little less; and inside this cup is another cup, and a third.
The distance between each cup is the width of a thumb's knuckle; the cups are open on both sides, and their height is about a knuckle or two knuckles.  A handle of well-secured iron is attached to them.  This is its image.
Then it is heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, then it is placed on the hip joint, the patient leaning on the sound side, and three round cauterizations are performed at once. Then he is left for three days, and the wound is dressed with butter, and the wound is left open for days.
Many then treat it with ointment until it heals. The author of this book says: We have rarely used this type of cauterization because of its ugliness and the horror of its appearance, and because few are found who can endure it; however, it is a good cauterization for those who endure it and whose ailment is in the correct location.
As for cauterization with burning drugs, it is done by making two cup-like things, similar to the rings through which the hinges of a door pass, of copper or iron.
Their rims are raised about two finger-widths or so, and the distance between them is the width of a finger. They are open at the bottom and open at the top, and are held together in this manner.
Then you place it on the hip joint, the patient lying on his sound side, and you hold your hand firmly, then you pour between the circles some sharp water, which is warmed a little by the fire, and you hold it for the time of an hourglass, and the patient endures its stinging and burning, for he feels a burning like fire.
Until the stinging subsides, then you lift the instrument with your hand and wipe the sharp water from the entire hip with fresh water, then you leave it for three days and apply butter to it until the blackness of the burn disappears, and you leave it for days so that pus flows from it, for that is more effective.
Then you treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, you make a mold for it that holds the water, in the same way as you did for the hip.  This is the description of the sharp water, and I have already mentioned it in the chapter on the preparation of drugs.
Take one part of alkali salt and one part of unslaked lime, crush them, and place them in a new pot which has a single small hole in its bottom, large enough for a probe to pass through.  Place another glazed pot under the bottom of the pot.
And pour on the alkali and lime enough fresh water to cover them by a finger's breadth, after you have pressed them down firmly with your hand. Leave the pot until the sharp water drips into the glazed pot below. Then collect all that water, and pour it over more fresh lime and alkali.
Then it will be very strong and sharp, useful in many medical procedures and for cauterizing various members, because it acts just like fire itself. Among the medicaments that also cauterize the hip are thapsia, honey of spurge, and lime with soap, well mixed.
Galen recounted a story from an ancient man about a treatment for hip pain and sciatica, and he greatly emphasized it, claiming that no other treatment is needed and that it cures with a single application.
To the point that someone might enter the bath carried and leave cured.  This is done by taking green borage; if green is not available, take fresh dry borage, finely grind it with some fat, and apply it to the hip where the pain is, or to the leg or thigh.
Then bind it on and leave it for three hours, or however long the patient feels the burning subside. Then bring him to the bath. When his body is damp, put him in the bath. Then, God willing, the pain will go away and he will be cured. If he is not cured, repeat the application after ten days.
Then, God willing, he will be cured. And know that one should not cauterize with these medicaments except after purging the body. Dioscorides mentioned that if sciatica is cauterized with goat hair, it is beneficial.
The cauterization is done in this way: take wool, soak it in old oil, and place it on the hollow place between the thumb and the forearm, closer to the forearm. Then take dry goat hair.
Ignite it with fire until it becomes a coal, then place it on the wool and leave it until it goes out. Then take another and continue doing this until sensation reaches from the middle of the arm to the hip, and the pain subsides, God willing. This type of cauterization is called cauterization with goat hair.
Chapter forty-two.
On cauterizing back pain.
Pain in the back may arise from many causes, either from a fall, a blow, excessive vomiting, and the like. It may be from the effusion of a cold, moist substance; cauterization is only for this type.
That which is from the effusion of a cold substance: after the patient has vomited with the hellebore and the like, he should be cauterized on his back where the pain is, three rows across the width of the table itself, after marking the location with ink.  In each row, five cauterizations or more, according to what you see the patient can tolerate and his strength.
The cauterization should be with a pointed cautery; and if you wish, cauterize it three or four times with a medium-sized nail cautery, in this manner.
Chapter forty-three.
On cauterizing the beginning of a hump.
This ailment frequently affects young children.  Its initial sign in a child is shortness of breath upon standing and movement, and you will find at the end of his spinal vertebrae a bead that has protruded, standing out from the other beads.
If you see this and wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery iron of this shape. Let the cauterization take hold on each side of the vertebra equally. And if you wish, cauterize around the vertebra with a pointed cautery iron in two or three rows, and let the points be close to one another. Then treat the area until it heals as we have mentioned.
And take care not to use cautery in a hump that is due to a nerve spasm.
Chapter forty-four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pains.
If joint pains are due to cold humors that settle in any member of the body, and if the pains occur in the legs, then it is customary for physicians to call this gout, especially.
If gout caused by cold is treated with the various methods we mentioned in the classification, and the pains do not go away, then cauterization will remove them.  This is done by cauterizing around the joints of the legs with many cauterizations after vomiting. The cautery should be olive-shaped and medium-sized, in this manner.
And if you need to cauterize the surface of the leg, do so with a pointed cautery. If the pains ascend to the knees or to the other joints, and this often happens, cauterize each knee with three or four cauterizations on each side with this same olive-shaped cautery.
If you need more cauterization, cauterize, but do not deepen your application, rather it should be only about the thickness of the skin. If the pains ascend to the hips or to the back, use what we mentioned concerning cauterization in its chapter.
If the pains are only in the hands, cauterize around the forearms as if circling two rows. If the pains remain in the fingers, cauterize each knuckle with one cauterization and the metacarpus. If the pains ascend after several days to the elbows or to the shoulders,
cauterize them on each side. The patient should not be without good treatment and the taking of medicines, for if he improves his diet and evacuates phlegm, he will be cured with this cauterization, God willing.
Chapter forty-five.
On cauterizing hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin and some intestine and fat descends to the testicle, and this is recent and early, then you should order the patient to abstain from food that day and to use laxatives which will move the stool from his abdomen.
Then he should lie on his back before you, and you should tell him to restrain himself until the fat or intestine protrudes, then you should return it with your finger.  Then mark with ink below the hernia on the pubic bone a sign resembling a semicircle, its ends towards the upper part of the body.
Then heat a cautery of this shape.
Until it becomes white and throws off sparks. Then return the intestine or fat to the abdomen. Then let an assistant place his hand on the place lest the intestine protrude.  And you have parted the patient's legs and placed a cushion under him, and another assistant ties his legs.
And another on his chest holds his hands. Then lower the cautery onto the same mark, and your hand with the cautery should be upright and straight, and hold it until it reaches the bone, or repeat it again if it did not reach the bone the first time.
And guard against the protrusion of the intestine while you cauterize, lest you burn it, which would cause the patient either death or a great calamity. And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your work will not have succeeded. And the cautery should be gentle for children according to their size, and for adults according to their size.
Then treat the cauterized area after three days with butter until the scabbing from the fire disappears. Then treat it with other ointments until it heals. And let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound closes.
And you should make his diet during his treatment that which softens his bowels, lest the intestine protrude during defecation. Then, if he wants to get up after forty days, use a tight bandage and keep it on for another forty days. And let him reduce exertion, overeating and drinking, and loud shouting.
For if this procedure is used in this way, he will be completely healed, God willing. And I will mention the treatment of hernias by incision in its chapter, God willing. As for the hernia that occurs in the rest of the abdomen and is beginning, if you want it not to increase, cauterize a round mark on it, corresponding to its size. And let what you burn of the skin be about two-thirds of it. Then treat it as we have mentioned, for it will not increase, God willing. And some of the ancients mentioned cauterizing the hernia with a triangular cautery in this manner.
After you have drawn on the hernia with ink, making the horizontal line at the upper side of the hernia and the other line downwards, and placing your hand in the middle, apply one nail-shaped cautery.  The first cautery is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter forty-six.
In cauterizing the fistula.
If pain occurs in some member from a fall or a blow, and that pain lasts a long time, and medication does not alleviate it, then it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery. The cauterization of each member should be according to its size, smallness, weakness, and strength.
And if the pain and fistula persist, then if it heals from the first cauterization, well and good; otherwise, repeat the cauterization. For it is the nature of these pains to move from one member to its neighbor, so you should follow them with cautery until the patient is cured.
Chapter forty-seven.
In cauterizing leprosy.
As for lepers, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially the type of leprosy that comes from the corruption of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize, consider: if the leprosy is incipient and you have treated it with what we mentioned in the classification, and it has not subsided nor stopped, and you fear that corruption will overwhelm the whole constitution of the patient, then cauterize him on the head with five cauterizations. One in the middle of the head, as is customary; the second below it towards the forehead at the hairline; two on the temples; and one at the back on the nape of the neck.  And exert yourself a little with the cauterization until it slightly affects the bone.
And scales will be shed from it, so that the thick vapors may more easily escape.  Cauterize also the spleen in the same manner as before. But if the leprosy has persisted in the patient and become clearly manifest, then it is necessary to cauterize with these cauterizations which we mentioned on the head. And a cauterization on the tip of the nose, two cauterizations on the cheeks, two cauterizations on the vertebrae of the neck, six on the vertebrae of the back, one large cauterization on the coccyx at the base of the spine, and another above it in the same place, and two on the hips, one on each hip. And two on the knees, one on each knee; and two on the shoulders; and two on the elbows; and two on the breasts; and cauterize each joint of the fingers of his hands and feet with one cauterization; and each heel of his feet and forearms.
And be careful of the nerve which is on the back of the heels, lest you burn them.  And it may be that a cauterization is performed on the pubic bone, and another on the stomach, and another on the liver. And know that the more cauterizations you perform, the more beneficial and effective it will be.
And know that the patient does not feel the pain of cauterization as much as a healthy person, because his body is numb. And the cauteries should be large and small according to the organs and joints, as described in the characteristics of the cauteries.
Then treat the cauterization with barley flour mixed with honey and other treatments until it heals, God willing.
Chapter forty-eight.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness: When a limb becomes numb and is treated with medicines, ointments, and dressings, but does not heal, cauterize the numb limb with cauteries according to the size of the limb, whether large or small. Let your cauterization be deep enough to penetrate the thickness of the skin a little.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  Sometimes, some numbness that affects the hand and foot in the vertebrae of the back at the exit of the nerve that moves that limb may be cauterized, and the numbness will go away.
And let no one undertake this except one who is knowledgeable about the anatomy of the limbs and the exits of the nerves that move the body.
Chapter forty-nine.
Concerning the cauterization of vitiligo.
If vitiligo is longstanding and no medical treatment is effective, cauterize it slightly, to the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour mixed with rose oil, lamb's tongue, and pigeon's or partridge's blood—one part of each. Mix everything together, apply it to a cloth, and bind it to the area until it heals.
Chapter fifty.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
If the cancer is incipient and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it grows. Some wise men have mentioned cauterizing it intensely in the middle, but I do not see this as advisable, because I expect that it will ulcerate, and I have witnessed this many times.
Therefore, the correct method is to cauterize around it in a circle, as we said, or with many small cauterizations.
Chapter fifty-one.
Concerning the cauterization of hemorrhoids.
If a hemorrhoid develops and is slow to mature, either because of the excessive strength of the humors, or because of the patient's age if he is old and anemic, or because of time, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the hemorrhoid, cauterize around it with many small, punctate cauterizations.
Then leave it, for it will hasten maturation. If you wish to stop it with cauterization, take the cautery iron whose image is this.
And insert it into the middle of the hemorrhoid until it penetrates the skin. Let the cauterization be from what is below, so that the pus may flow easily. Then treat it appropriately until it heals.
Chapter fifty-two.
On cauterizing the canker.
A canker is a corruption that spreads in a member, eating it as fire eats dry wood. If you see a canker in a place that can tolerate cauterization with fire, take many small and large, nail-shaped cauteries, according to what is suitable for that place where the canker is.
Then cauterize it from every side until all the corruption is eradicated and nothing at all remains. Then leave it for three days and apply to the cauterized areas powdered sulfur with oil until all the eschar and all the corruption is removed.
Then treat it with ointments that grow flesh. If you see after three days that the flesh grows healthy growth with no corruption, otherwise repeat the cauterization on what remains of the corrupted areas. And a canker may be treated with a sharp medicine.
For it acts in place of fire, more quickly beneficial. And I have mentioned its treatment with a sharp medicine in the classification, so take it from there when you need it.
Chapter fifty-three.
On cauterizing inverted and non-inverted nails.
This ailment often occurs in the soles of the feet; it is a rough thing that pains the person.  Cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a sharp liquid.
As for cauterization with fire, you heat a hollow cautery iron resembling an eagle's feather, made of iron to the extent that it surrounds the nail on all sides, and it should have a thin edge. Then you apply it hot to the nail, and then you rotate the iron around the nail until the iron reaches the depth of the nail.
And you leave it for three days, during which pus will form, then you apply a dressing of pounded wild lettuce with salt, and leave the dressing on it overnight. Then it will be extracted from its roots, and then treat the wound site with a flesh-regenerating ointment until it heals, if the nails are not inverted.
This often occurs on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet.  You should take a tube of copper, iron, or eagle's feather and place it over the nail or wart, then pour a little of the sharp liquid into the tube.
Hold your hand as you rotate it, gently pressing with your hand so that the edge of the tube affects the root of the nail, and the sharp liquid finds its way to the root of the nail. The patient should endure the stinging of the sharp liquid for a short time.
Then leave it, for the nail will be extracted by its roots. Do this one by one until you have treated all of them on the body, then treat the areas after they are removed with ointments that regenerate flesh.
Chapter fifty-four.
On cauterizing scabs.
If a scab develops from a cold in the nerve, or from a fever blister or something else, then it should be cauterized four or five times, on the vertebrae of the back, with a cauterization between each vertebra, and a cauterization on the chest. And a cauterization on his stomach with an olive-shaped cautery iron, for the scab will subside and hasten the maturation of the cold illness.
Chapter fifty-five.
On cauterizing pustules that occur on the body.
Ugly pustules may erupt on the body, caused by thick, corrupt, cold substances.  At their first appearance, each pustule should be gently cauterized with a thin cautery iron whose tip has been heated in the fire, or with a long cautery iron from a zarwan plant, or with a lentil-shaped cautery iron.
And boils may be cauterized at their first eruption in this manner, so that the active cause does not increase, and it dissipates, and the patient recovers from them; however, this should be done after the patient has been bled.
Chapter fifty-six.
On cauterizing bleeding that occurs when an artery is cut.
Often, bleeding from a severed artery occurs from an external wound, or when an abscess is incised, or when a member is cauterized, and so forth,  and it is difficult to stop.
If this happens to someone, quickly place your index finger on the mouth of the artery and press it firmly to contain the blood under your finger so that nothing comes out. Then, place several small and large olive-shaped cauteries in the fire and heat them until they are very hot.
Then take one, either small or large, depending on the wound and the place where the artery has been opened.  Quickly remove your finger and apply the cautery to the same vessel and hold the cautery until the bleeding stops.
If, when you remove your finger from the mouth of the artery, the bleeding resumes and the cautery is extinguished, quickly take another cautery from those in the fire that have been prepared, and continue to do this, one after another, until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn a nerve, lest another affliction befall the patient. Know that if an artery bleeds, it cannot be stopped, especially if it is a large artery, except in one of four ways: either by cauterization, as we have said; or by amputation, if it has not already been severed; for if it is severed, its ends contract and the bleeding stops; or by tightly tying it with threads; or by applying medications that stop bleeding, and binding it firmly with bandages.
As for anyone who attempts to stop it with a ligature, or by tight bandaging, or by applying cauterizing agents, and the like, it will never be stopped in this way except rarely. If this happens to someone and a physician or medication is not available, he should immediately place the index finger on the mouth of the wound itself, as we have described.
He should press it very hard until the bleeding ceases and flows from above the wound and onto the artery. The finger should not be removed, and cold water, very cold, should constantly be applied to it until the blood clots, thickens, and stops.  During this time, consider what else is needed in the way of cautery or medicine.
Chapter Two.
Concerning incisions, punctures, bloodletting, wounds, and the like.
Khalaf said: In the first chapter we mentioned every ailment suitable for cauterization with fire and caustic medication, its causes and reasons, its instruments, the shapes of cautery irons, and we made that into sections, from the head to the foot.
And I follow in this chapter the same path, so that what the student requires may be easier for him.
Before I begin with that, you should know, my sons, that this chapter contains more pitfalls than the first chapter on cautery.
And because of that, warning should be stronger in this, for in the work of this chapter, there frequently occurs the evacuation of blood by which life is sustained, when opening a vein or making an incision on a tumor, or puncturing an abscess, or treating a wound, or extracting an arrow, or incising a stone, and the like, all of which are accompanied by danger and fear, and in most of them death occurs.
And I advise you against falling into what is doubtful for you; for in this craft, various kinds of people may come to you with various kinds of illnesses, and among them are those who are weary of their illness and to whom death is easy.
Because of the severity of what he finds in his illness, and the length of his affliction, and in the illness there is evidence of death; and some give you their money and enrich you with it in hope of health, and his illness is fatal, so you should never help at all whoever comes to you who is of this description.
