Let your caution be stronger than your desire and eagerness, and do not proceed to anything of that except after certain knowledge that is confirmed with you as to what the favorable outcome will be, and in all the treatment of your patients, employ the priority of knowledge and warning as to what leads to safety.
For in that you have help in acquiring praise, glory, mention, and thanks. May God inspire you, O my sons, with His guidance, and may He not forbid you from truth and success; indeed, that is in His hand; there is no god but He.
And I have arranged this chapter in sections, as preceded in the chapter on cauterization, from the horn to the foot, so that what you want from it may be easier for you, God willing.
Chapter One.
In the treatment of the water that collects in the heads of children.
This ailment often affects infants at birth if the midwife presses the infant's head without gentleness, and it may also occur from a hidden cause that is not known.
And I have not seen this ailment in anyone other than infants, and all those I have seen of them have quickly died; therefore, I have seen fit to leave treatment of it. And I have seen an infant whose head was full of water, and the head grew larger every day. Until the infant could not sit up by himself because of the size of his head, and the moisture increased until he perished.
And this moisture either collects between the skin and the bone, or it collects under the bone on the peritoneum.  The procedure is that if the moisture is between the skin and the bone, and the swelling is small, then it is necessary to make a single incision across the middle of the head, and the length of the incision should be about two fingerbreadths, so that the moisture flows out. This is the image of the scalpel.
If the moisture is greater and the swelling is larger, then make two intersecting incisions in this manner:.
And if the moisture is under the bone—and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side, and the water sinks down if you press it with your hand inwards, and this is not hidden from you—then it is necessary to make three incisions in the middle of the head in this manner.
And after the incision, all the moisture comes out, then you bind the incisions with cloths and bandages, then you bathe it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day, then you untie the bandage and treat the wound with lint and ointments.  And do not leave off binding the head moderately, and feed the patient with every dry food with little moisture.
Until the member strengthens and heals. Another description of the incision is that you look where the bone of the swelling and the collection of water appear, because it may be in the back of the head more, or in the front, or on the right, or on the left.
Then you aim with the incision where the swelling and the fullness of water appear to you, so you incise it as much as you can, and be careful not to cut a vein, lest bleeding occur and the patient die from that bleeding along with the evacuation of the moisture.
Chapter Two.
Cutting the two arteries behind the ears known as the "al-ḥasīsayn".
When someone is afflicted with acute catarrh in the eyes or chest, and this persists and medication does not work, then the most effective treatment for this is cutting these two arteries.
So, if you want to cut them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to reveal the artery, then tie the patient's neck with the edge of his garment, then look where the pulse beats and their location, the two low-lying places behind the ears, and they rarely hide except in some people.
Then mark them with ink, then cut them with a lancet, cutting to the bone. This should be done across the head, or if you wish, insert the lancet under the artery and cut upwards. The length of the cut should be about two joined fingers.
If the artery is cut, the blood will gush out in a pulsating manner, leaping forward and continuously flowing. If the artery is not palpable, you should measure three fingers from the ear, then mark it with ink and incise to the bone.  What should be...
...sent forth from the blood six ounces on average, and perhaps more or less, all this according to the strength of the patient and the fullness of his arteries that you see. Then look at the wound; if some peritoneum remains on the bone, cut it away.
lest a hot inflammation occur. Then bind the wound with a thread of linen rags and treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
Incising the two arteries in the temples.
If a person suffers from chronic migraine or acute attacks caused by sharp humors and heat in the muscles of the temples, or severe chronic headache and the like, and he has been treated with various medical treatments without success, then we have tried in these diseases to incise the arteries of the temples or cauterize them, as we have described.
The procedure for incising them is that the patient's hair in the temples should be shaved, and the artery visible in the temple should be targeted, for it will be apparent to you from its pulsation, and it rarely hides except in a few people.
Or if it is very cold, then if it is hidden from you, let the patient tighten his neck with the excess of his garment, then you rub the area with a cloth or compress the area with hot water until the artery appears clearly and evidently. Then take the scalpel, whose image is this. Then you incise the skin gently with it until you reach the artery, then you throw a hook into it and pull it upwards until you bring it out of the skin and free it from the membranes underneath it from every side; if the artery is thin, then twist it with the tip of the hook. Then cut off a section of it to the extent that its ends are separated and constricted, and do not cause bleeding, for if it is not severed and not cut, the blood will not flow at all, then blood is evacuated from six ounces to three.
If the artery is large, then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong doubled thread, and let the thread be either of silk or of lute strings, lest corruption reach it before the wound heals, and bleeding occurs.
Then cut what is between the two ligatures; do this at that time or after another time. And if you wish to cauterize it with a knife-like cautery to the bone until its ends are severed, it will serve the same purpose or better, as we have said.
Unless the patient has a fever or a hot temperament, because cauterization is what helps in the consumption of humors, and it will be more effective. After tying off the two arteries, the area should be packed with worn cotton and firm dressings applied, and after healing, it should be treated with drying medicines that grow flesh and with bandaging until it heals, God willing.
If bleeding from the artery occurs during your work, quickly cut it, either by cauterization or by filling the area with vitriol and squeezing your hand until the blood stops.  If you do not have any of that, place your finger until the blood clots.
And wash the area with very cold water until the sharpness subsides, and bind it as necessary.  What is easier than ligating the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery after marking the two points with ink.
And apply it very hot until it reaches the bone and the vein is severed in two places so that its ends are separated, for it will not reunite with this cauterization at all.  This is the image of the cautery.
The blades should be sharp, resembling small awls, except that they should be much less sharp than a knife, because if they are as sharp as a knife, they will cool more quickly and the flesh will not be severed quickly.  If they have some thickness, the heat of the fire will be retained in them.
And the flesh is severed quickly. This method is better than any other method, and lighter and easier. The distance between the blades should be the width of a finger.
Chapter Four.
In the treatment of the constant flow of hot tears into the eyes.
If the tears are constant and their flow is from the vessels on the cranium from the outside, and nothing from medical treatment with medicines has been beneficial, and you see the patient's face has reddened. And he feels in his forehead a crawling like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are sunken and moist, their lids corroded and their eyelids clinging together from the severity of the tears.  The ancients in treating it resorted to this procedure, and they call it treatment with the scalpel; it is that you order the patient to have the hair shaved from his forehead.
Then you make three parallel incisions in the forehead along the length of the forehead. The length of each incision should be about two fingers, one incision parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second a little distance from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third on the other side.
And be careful not to cut the two arteries on the sides, and keep your hand away from the connection of the jaws.  Let the distance between each incision be about three fingers' breadth.  And let you have prepared pieces of sponge or many dry cloths to wipe away the blood.
Then insert the double-edged scalpel, whose image is this. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, and with it flay all the skin that is between the two incisions, together with the periosteum on the bone. Then do the same also from the middle incision to the third incision, then remove this scalpel. And insert into the first incision also another instrument called a knife, sharp on one side and smooth, not sharp, on the other side. And this is its image:
And place its sharp side upwards towards the flesh adhering to the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone, and push it until it reaches the middle incision, and cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes, without the cut reaching the surface of the skin.
Then do this in the middle incision to the other incision. And after a moderate amount of blood has flowed, squeeze the places of the clotted blood, then place in each incision a pledget of cotton, and place over it a compress moistened with a drink and oil, or vinegar and oil.
Lest a hot swelling occur. And on the third day, loosen the bandage and use much irrigation with lukewarm water, then treat it with basil ointment with rose oil and all that you treat wounds with, until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
In the treatment of tears and discharges to the eyes from the interior of the head.
When someone experiences frequent, sharp, severe, constant discharges, and you see his eyes weak, small, and his vision weakened, and his eyelids ulcerated and the eyelashes falling out. And there is severe, painful pain in the depth of the head and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep veins. The best treatment is this treatment: You should order the patient to have his forehead shaved, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is severe, painful pain in the depth of the head and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep veins. The best treatment is this treatment: You should order the patient to have his forehead shaved, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or slightly higher, laterally, beginning the incision from the left temple to the right temple.  The incision should reach the bone, and keep your hand away from the temporal muscles that move during chewing, until the bone is exposed and all the blood is dried with a sponge.
Then separate the edges of the incision with old cotton or a linen thread. Then tie it from above with bandages, and soak the bandages with syrup and oil, lest a hot inflammation occur.  When you untie them and see that the hot inflammation has subsided, you should scrape the bone until the growth of flesh begins.
Then treat it with a drying preparation that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, preparing an ointment from them, and using it for flesh growth in such wounds.
Chapter Six.
In the treatment of what falls into the ear.
Whatever falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone, or something like stone such as iron and glass; or a vegetable seed like chickpea and kernel and the like; or a fluid thing like water and vinegar and the like.
Or an animal.  When a stone or a kind of stone falls into the ear which does not grow in the ear, expose the ear to the sun. Then, if you see the stone, instill some violet oil or cerate into it, then try to remove it by moving the head or sneezing with a handkerchief. And block the nostrils at the time of sneezing after you place a ring of rags or wool around the ear and extend the ear upwards. Often it comes out with this treatment. If it does not come out otherwise, then try to remove it with a soft probe, the image of which is this.
If it comes out with the probe, otherwise try to remove it with a gentle, slightly curved, blind probe. If it does not come out with that, then make a tube of copper and insert the end of the tube into the hole of the ear gently.
And seal what is around the tube with pitch softened with oil, lest there be a path for the wind other than the tube. Then draw it with your breath strongly, and often it comes out with that. If it does not come out in the way we described, then take a little of the Nabataean mastic or of the prepared mastic which is used to catch birds, and place it on the end of a probe.
After you wrap it tightly with cotton, then insert it gently into the hole of the ear after you dry the ear from moisture. If it does not come out with all that we described, then hasten to incision before hot swelling or tension occurs.
The description of the incision is that you first bleed the patient in the cephalic vein and let out blood according to his strength. Then you sit the patient between your hands and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear near its lobe in its lowest part.
And the incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it with whatever instruments you can. Then quickly suture the incision and treat it until it heals.
And if the thing that has fallen into the ear is one of the seeds that grow and swell, then try to remove it as we mentioned. If it does not respond to removal, then take a thin, fine scalpel of this shape.
And try with it to cut that type of seed that has fallen into the ear.  You do this only if you are certain that the seed has become moistened by the vapor of the ear so that it becomes many small pieces, then remove them with a blind probe, or with a fine spatula, or by suction.
As we have mentioned, then it is easy to remove it. As for water entering the ear, the patient should first use sneezing with hellebore, having filled his ear with some old cotton while lying on the ear in which the water is. If it comes out with that,
otherwise, take many small, smooth, flat stones the length of the fingers, warm them slightly by the fire, and the patient inserts one into the opening of his ear. He should then press down with his foot on that side and strike the stone in the ear with another stone.
He continues to do this with stone after stone until all the water comes out. The water may also come out by taking a piece of papyrus or a feather and inserting one end into the ear and bringing the other end to the fire until most of it burns, then another feather is used.
Do this repeatedly until all the water comes out or draw it out with a tube as previously described for the stone. As for removing an animal that has entered, look: if it is small in size, like a flea or similar, treat it as I mentioned in the section, but...
if its body is large and perceptible to the touch, try removing it with forceps and hooks. This is easier than anything else that lodges in the ear. As for removing worms generated in the ear, if you treat it as we mentioned in the section on instillations and your treatment is not effective,
you should look into the ear in the sun. If you see anything of the worms, remove them with forceps or fine hooks. If you do not see anything, take a tube of this shape.
narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, and insert the thin end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate. Then suck strongly. Do this repeatedly until all the worms come out. If they do not come out, seal around the tube with wax as I described for the stone.
If they do not come out by what we have mentioned, use the instillations that I have mentioned, which the ancients have tried in killing worms. You will find this in the section on instillations.  The pouring of oils and medicines into the ear should be done with this instrument, and this is its image:
As you see, make it of silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, and wide at the top. If you wish, the nozzle inside the tube can be made of tightly fitting copper.  If you wish, you can take a reed and tightly wrap cotton around its end.
Then pour the oil or juice or whatever medicine you want into the tube while it is in the ear. Then insert the reed with cotton from above and squeeze it moderately with your hand until the oil is forced into the ear and is felt.
The patient feels it entering.  What you pour into the ear should be warmed slightly in the fire, and be careful that what is dropped in is neither very cold nor very hot, for the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
In the treatment of the obstruction affecting the ear.
Some children may be born with their ear canals not perforated.
It may also happen that some people experience an obstruction in the ear canal from a wound or from growing flesh. This obstruction may be deep within the ear canal, unseen by the eye.
It may also appear slightly outside the opening and be visible. That which appears deep within the ear and is not visible is, in most cases, difficult to cure.  That which is visible, the patient's ear should be placed in the sun. And you should look at it. If you see the obstruction is visible, open it with a delicate scalpel, which has this shape:
Its tip should be slightly broad, the rest of the scalpel smooth on both sides so as not to injure the ear. If the obstruction is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate hook and cut it gently until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep within the ear, take a smooth, delicate probe, warm it slightly in the fire, then insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction, and the patient feels a heaviness in hearing, remove it gently.
Take care not to injure the membrane of the ear. Then insert into the ear a tent of a size appropriate for the opening, having coated it in Egyptian ointment. Do this for several days until you are sure the wound will not close up. Or, take a tent, moisten it in water and sprinkle powdered vitriol on it.
Use this. If you see the ear has swollen with a hot inflammation, you should remove the tent and replace it with another tent coated in a preparation made with rose oil to calm the hot inflammation, then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear. Use the other treatments for stopping bleeding mentioned in the chapter on styptics.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts which appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry.  You should grasp them with forceps or a hook and cut them from their base with a scalpel, then apply powdered verdigris to the area.
If it bleeds, cauterize it with a mild lentil-shaped cautery. Cauterization is better after cutting.  It often returns if any of its roots remain; if cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and it will not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of the cold affecting the eyelids.
Something resembling a cold in its intensity and hardness often appears in the eyelids.
Therefore it is called a cold, which is a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids.  The treatment is to look: if the coldness is prominent on the surface of the eyelid and moves to every side, then it is easy; make a transverse incision on it.
Then gently peel it from every side until it is freed, then hang it with a hook and cut it. If you cannot cut it except after penetrating the eyelid with the cut, that does not harm the patient. If the incision is large, close it with sutures and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, there is no harm from it, for ointment will heal and join it. If the coldness is inclined towards the inside of the eyelid, towards the inner surface, then turn the eyelid over and hang the coldness with a hook without needing an incision, and cut it from every side.
If it penetrates the eyelid with the cut, that does not harm. Then wash the eye after cutting the coldness with salt water and treat the area with what joins until the patient heals.
Chapter Ten.
In the treatment of the stye which appears in the eyelid.
A stye is a lump that forms in the layers of the upper eyelid, and this mostly occurs in boys. It weighs down their eyes and they experience colds in the mornings, and they are unable to look at sunlight. Because the tears rush to them.
Therefore you see them always leaning on their faces and sleeping on them, and their eyelids under the eyebrows are moist, with swelling upon them, and the stye is clearly visible.  When you press the area with your fingers, you feel the stye between them. The method of treatment is that the patient should place his head in your lap.
Then you take a linen cloth and prepare a wick from it, and make from that wick a circle the size of what surrounds the stye from every side, then you place it on it and press with your fingers from every side to gather the stye in the middle of the circle.
Then you make an incision in the middle of that moisture with a lancet, a transverse incision, and the incision should not be larger than the incision that is made in bloodletting. As for the depth, you should cut all the skin until you reach the stye.
Then, in most cases, the stye protrudes from the incision in the form of a white piece of fat. You pull it out with a cloth that you have wrapped between your fingers, while you turn your hand right and left until it is healed. Beware of increasing the incision lest the lancet reach the eye and injure it.
If the stye does not appear to you at the first incision, you should increase the incision a little gently until the stye appears, then pull it out as we said, then dip a cloth in vinegar and water and place it on the area and bandage it. Some people crush salt.
And they place it inside the incision to dissolve what remains of that moisture, then treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs in the area, treat it with soothing compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On types of eyelid inflammation.
If extra eyelashes grow on the eyelid, outside the natural course, under the natural eyelashes and brows, then they harm the eye and cause various diseases such as constant tearing, eyelid relaxation, and whiteness and thickness, until it becomes a cause of blindness in the eye.
Eyelid inflammation is of four types: either cauterization with fire, or with a strong medicine as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, or the inflammation may be treated by excision and suturing or with a reed, as I will mention.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand. If it turns, well and good; otherwise, insert a needle with thread from below the eyelid, passing the needle with thread from above, near the hair itself, and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid.
Turn it with a probe, then incise within the eyelid, below the extra hairs, with a sharp scalpel, from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus. Then remove the thread and place a small pledget of cotton or cloth under the eyelid. Then mark the eyelid with ink.
Like the shape of a laurel leaf, except that the shape should be according to how much you want to raise the eyelid, because this varies among people; some need a suitable amount of eyelid to be cut according to how much the eyelid has relaxed.
And some need less to be cut, all that according to the relaxation of the eyelid. Then incise with the scalpel along the two lines that you marked, beginning from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus, and let each incision be near the natural hair, about the thickness of a probe.
Then insert the hook into one corner of the skin and peel it all off. Then, using a needle and fine woolen thread, stitch the edges together. Wipe away the blood and stick the remaining threads to the eyebrows with some adhesive substance.
If you wish to do that, otherwise it doesn't matter. Then leave the stitches and threads for about three or four days, then treat it. If you wish, you may leave the wound without stitching and treat it with what dries and contracts, for the eyelid rises when the wound closes and heals; stitching is better.
This method of blepharoplasty was mentioned by the ancients, although it is burdensome for the patient. It is a good method and there is no danger in it.  Another method of blepharoplasty is also to mark on the eyelid a shape like that of a laurel leaf, as we described. Then raise the eyelid with three hooks, either separated or grouped in this manner:
Then cut off the excess eyelid with small scissors, in this manner, in even pieces.
If you cannot hold the hooks and they do not remain steady for you, then take a needle with thread and insert it into the middle of the shape, and insert another thread near the larger canthus and a third thread near the smaller canthus.  Gather the threads together evenly with your fingers, then raise them with a steady hand.
Raise the marked skin entirely as we described, then join the edges of the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals. If a hot inflammation appears when you cut or incise, then calm that inflammation with qirūṭī and similar soothing ointments.
It may also happen that the lower eyelid inverts its margins; then you should use what we mentioned of cutting and suturing and cauterization with fire, and with a caustic medicine. Cauterization with fire or with a caustic medicine is more effective and gentler on the patient than cutting and suturing, and you should perform cutting and suturing in both.
Cauterization with a reed is done in this way: you invert the eyelid and make an incision on the inside as described, then you make two thin reeds or pieces of wood, their length equal to the length of the eyelid and their width less than the width of a scalpel.
And you carve at their ends on both sides where the threads are attached, then gently bring them together over what remains of the eyelid and tightly pull the reeds from both sides and leave it for a few days. Then the stretched skin dies, turns black, and decays.
Until it falls off by itself. If it is slow, then cut it with scissors, then treat it until it heals. When it has healed, the eyelid will be raised and the margins of the eye will not be inverted. This is an image of the two reeds; make them in this shape and this exact size in length and width.
Chapter Twelve.
On raising an inverted eyelash from the eye with a needle.
If it is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a thin needle and thread it with a fine, smooth silk thread, then bring the two ends together and tie them in a very delicate knot, and let the length of the thread be about a span.
Then attach another thin thread to the needle, shorter than the first, and tie its ends if you wish. Then let the patient place his head in your lap, and let it be near the sun so that the work may be clearly seen, for it is delicate work. Then insert the needle with the thread into the root of the superfluous hair.
And pass it through the eyelid quickly until you bring it out above the natural hair, then pull the thread upwards on the needle and insert into the needle that hair, whether it be one, two or three, no more.
Then pull it with your hand until the hair comes out with the needle in the eyelid along with the natural hair. If you pull the needle and the hair does not come out with it, pull the needle downwards with the thread that you attached to it until the needle comes out from the upper opening.
Then return the hair into it and pull them both. Do this until it rises in the upper opening and you see it clearly. Then remove the needle and thread and take them out, and bind the eyelid and leave it bound for two or three days until the hair settles in its place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add to it a long hair from the natural hair so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer, then raise it.
Chapter Thirteen.
In the treatment of the stye which occurs in the upper eyelid.
That which this stye is exposed to is called a harelip, and this stye is either natural or accidental. The accidental one is from the healing of a wound or a cut or cautery or the like.
And the way to treat it is to incise that adhesion and separate its lips and place between them a wick of linen and bind it until it heals, and it is not appropriate to use in its treatment things which dry and constrict.
For if you do that, the stye will return more severely than it was, but rather use in it things which relax, like fenugreek and lint with water in which althaea and flaxseed have been boiled, and diachylon ointment melted with some fats, and smear the wick with it and treat it with this.
And the aim of its treatment is to try by every means that it does not adhere in the way it was initially, and this treatment which we have mentioned is only some improvement of the stye, not that the appearance of the place returns completely to what it was.
Chapter Fourteen.
In the treatment of the stye which is in the lower eyelid.
This stye which forms in the lower eyelid is what is truly called a stye, and it is both natural and accidental.  The accidental one results from a wound, incision, cautery, or the like.
And the method of working in it is to take a needle with doubled thread and insert it into the flesh, passing it from the left canthus to the right canthus until the thread is at both ends of the flesh, then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a wide scalpel.
If the shape of the eyelid returns to what is appropriate, otherwise, take a probe and place it on the site of the incision and turn the eyelid with it and make two incisions on the inner side of the eyelid, and the ends of the incisions are from the corners of the cut which you cut until they meet and form an angle from them. Until, when they meet, its shape becomes similar to this shape:
Which is the Greek letter lambda, then remove that flesh to the extent that its sharp side is lower than what is below the eyelid, then gather the separated parts with two sutures, sew it with a wool thread, then treat it with what we mentioned of softening and relaxing medications until it heals.
And if the fistula appeared from an incision, suture, or cauterization, it is necessary to make a simple incision without the hair of the eyelids as well, as mentioned before, then separate the lips with a probe as we mentioned.
In short, in the treatment of a fistula, whether it is from above or below, you should work according to what is available to you from the appearance of the fistula, for it may differ greatly in appearance, and the skillful surgeon devises a solution in any way possible to him.
Until the shape returns to its natural form or approximates it, and the disease itself will indicate to you what treatment and instrument are suitable for it in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
This adhesion may occur to many people, such that the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye, preventing it from easy movement and hindering its natural functions.  Then it is necessary to insert the tip of a probe under the eyelid.
And raise it upwards or stretch it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a gentle scalpel, not with the sharpness of cutting scalpels, but rather slightly dull, lest the patient be disturbed during the procedure and the scalpel slips, injuring the eye with its sharpness; and your cutting should be as if you are peeling off that adhesion.
And wait until the eyelid returns to its natural state and the adhesion is completely healed, then pour into the eye salt water to wash it, or instill into it some solution of vitriol dissolved in water, then separate the eyelid and the eye with a linen wick.
And place over the eye wool moistened with egg white, and after the third day use astringent eye drops until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cutting the pterygium and the fleshy excrescence of the conjunctiva.
The pterygium is of two kinds: either nervous, which resembles a firm, thin membrane, or non-nervous, which resembles a thick, white moisture; if touched with iron or attempted to be removed with a hook, it is torn.
And a hook does not hold in it. Both kinds begin from the greater canthus, until they gradually spread, until they cover the pupil and prevent light and the movement of the eye. The method of its removal is that the patient should place his head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes, and you raise the eyelid with your hand, then you grasp the pterygium with a slightly curved hook and stretch it upwards, then you take a needle and thread it with a horsehair or oxhair and strong thread, and bend the point of the needle slightly and insert it into the middle of the pterygium.
And you pass it through with the needle and tie the pterygium with the thread and stretch it upwards and dissect with the hair the side of the pterygium which is towards the pupil, as if you are spreading it with the hair to its end, then you cut the remainder at the root of the canthus with a delicate scalpel or small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the canthus.
Lest there should result from its cutting a constant flow of tears.  The pterygium may be stretched with the hook alone or with the thread as we said, then the smooth scalpel dissects the end, whose image is this:
And be careful that the scalpel does not touch the cornea, lest it cause a tear in it and injure the pupil. Then, after cutting, put something of powdered salt or of vitriol into the eye and leave it until the next day, then treat it as appropriate until it heals.
If the pterygium is not fibrous and you are unable to insert a needle into it or fix a hook in it, this type of pterygium is often treated with medications and heals as described in the classification. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
And take a delicate, smooth scalpel with a weak edge, like this image: And dissect the pterygium from above gently with it. If you see that it is loosening and breaking up and the dissection has affected it, then instill into the eye from your instrument vitriol or red vitriol or powdered salt and bandage the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the procedure until it is all gone, unless a hot inflammation intervenes in the eye; then leave it and treat the hot inflammation until it heals, then resume the dissection until it heals. As for the fleshy excrescence of the meatus…
If the excrescence injures the eye excessively, then hook that excrescence with a hook and cut some of it, but do not overdo the cutting lest it cause a flow of tears, then instill into the meatus red vitriol or vitriol until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the excision of fleshy excrescences and what grows of excessive flesh in the eye.
In the eyes of some people, a red fleshy growth may develop, accumulating until it covers the pupil, or approaches it, or overflows onto the eyelids, and sometimes the eyelids may be everted, and it resembles the rose of the pomegranate.
The procedure is that the patient should place his head in your lap, then open his eyes, and you should pick up all that red flesh with hooks suitable for that purpose.
Or grasp it with forceps or tweezers, then cut the flesh bit by bit until you have removed all of it by cutting, and be careful of the eye lest you injure it during the procedure. Your cutting may be done either with the scalpel which we described in the excision of the pterygium, or with small scissors. Like those with which one plucks hairs, as their image will be shown in the chapter following this chapter.
When you have finished cutting and all the growth is gone, fill the eye with crushed salt, or instill into it some red vitriol or a similar caustic medicine, and apply externally to the eye a pledget of cotton with egg white to prevent hot inflammation.
If any of the growth remains, and the bleeding is excessive, and you fear hot inflammation, then leave the eye and treat it with what will calm the inflammation, then return to the procedure until it is healed.  And do likewise with any excess flesh which appears in the eye of this type.
However, you should avoid treating any eye weak by nature or by accident with iron instruments, and do not undertake any of these procedures until you see that the member is able to withstand such treatment.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the removal of pterygia from the eye.
Pterygia are red vessels which grow over the eye, hindering its function and weakening the eye over time.  First, you should observe: if the eye in which the pterygia are present is strong and has no other disease besides the pterygia,
then removal is performed as follows: you should have the patient place his head in your lap, then hook those vessels with one or two hooks, according to your skill.  The hooks should be gently curved, in this manner:
or there may be two double hooks in one piece, in this manner:
Then, gently remove those vessels with fine scissors, wiping away the blood from time to time until you see that those vessels have been removed from the eye and the bleeding has stopped.  Be careful not to injure the eye with the points of the scissors, and let your work be done at midday facing the sun.
Be very careful in your work, lest you cut anything other than those vessels. When you have finished, instill green or red vitriol into the eye to cauterize what remains of the pterygium.  If you cannot remove it all at that time, bandage the eye with something that will soothe the pain.
Leave it for several days until the pain subsides and the inflammation ceases, then repeat the procedure in the same manner until it heals. This is the image of the appropriate scissors.
Leave it [and let the patient's head rest on the pupil's lap, while another wipes away the tears and examines the front and back of the eye.  And you] should, in principle, open the eye with fixed or triangular speculums held by a pupil.
Sharp, and let the patient's head rest on the pupil's lap, while another wipes away the tears and examines the front and back of the eye.  And you may choose whether to begin removing it from the top or bottom of the eye; it is preferable to sever two filaments on the upper side, one at the end of the cornea and the other at its beginning.
Grasp it between the middle and index fingers, then hook it from below opposite them; then grasp it between the little and ring fingers, and lift your hand gently until you see that the entire pterygium has been elevated. Cut it from the smallest side if you wish, with scissors, or if you wish, with a delicate scalpel.
And insert the thread onto a needle and dissect the entire pterygium until it is elevated above the conjunctiva. Do not cut the filament, for the tears and blood will obscure your vision.
Then cut with scissors what extends beyond the cornea from above and below, so that nothing remains attached except to the cornea. If you can elevate it from the cornea by dissection with the thread, that is best; and this may be possible in some people.
And if you cut it, let its site heal for several days, until the pain subsides and the inflammation abates, then resume the procedure.
Chapter Nineteen.
On replacing the nose.
Physicians call a fistula, when treating it with cautery or with the sharp, burning medicine previously described, and it does not heal, there is no recourse but to incise the swelling when it is ripe and evacuate all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed.  If the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, remove it with an instrument of this shape:
The rough head, so-called, is made of Indian iron and its head is round like a button, finely engraved with the pattern of a file or rasp. You place it on the affected part of the bone, then rotate it between your fingers, while applying slight pressure with your hand, until you know that the corruption has been removed.
Do this several times, then dress the wound with drying, astringent medicines. If the wound closes and the flesh grows over it and the discharge ceases, and forty days pass without recurrence, inflammation, or further incident,
know that it has healed. Otherwise, there is no recourse but to return the fistula to the nasal cavity in this manner:  Expose the bone again with an iron instrument or a sharp medicine. When the bone is exposed, take a probe of this shape:
The tip of the instrument is triangular, and its shaft is conical, tapering to the point, as you see. Then place it on the bone itself, near the ethmoid bone and slightly away from the eye. Then, rotate your hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
by feeling air escaping from it when he holds his hand over his nose. Then dress the wound with the astringent, drying medicines as I have taught you. When the flesh grows and the place hardens, the matter that used to flow outward will return to the nose, which will be easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
In returning the protrusion of the eye.
If the eye protrudes completely, and no harm or deficiency occurs in the sight, then you should ease the patient, then bleed him in the cephalic vein, then place a cupping glass on the nape of the neck without restriction, and gently suck.
Then apply a poultice made of acacia, aloe, frankincense, and alkanet to the eye. Then bind the eye from above to the compress with many small bandages. The method of making the small bandages is to take many soft, burnt cloths and fold each cloth into four layers, making many of them.
Then place them one on top of the other, as much as the eye needs, then bind them tightly with a strong cloth, and repeat the cupping with suction without restriction. Then remove the cupping glass and leave the eye bandaged for a day and a night.
If the bandage loosens during that time, it should be tightened well. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we said. If you see that the eye has returned, then do nothing. Otherwise, repeat the poultice, bandages, tightening, and cupping until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On cutting the uvula.
If a hernia occurs in the uvula layer, then it protrudes and emerges outside the eyelids like a grape, and the person's appearance is disfigured because of it, and you want to cut it, then the procedure is as I describe.
It is to insert a needle into the base of the uvula from below upwards, then insert another needle with doubled thread from the side of the nape of the neck and pass it through, leaving the first needle as it is.
Then cut the doubled thread where it bends and tie some of the uvula around the needle, and pull it gently, then remove the needle and place on the eye wool soaked in egg white and leave it until the threads fall out with the uvula, then treat the eye with whatever strengthens it.
Until the wound heals.  You may also pull the uvula from every direction with a circle of cloth until the eye protrudes gently, then incise with a thin scalpel reaching to the depth of the eye so that the white moisture flows out, and the eye descends and shrinks in place, then pull it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In the treatment of uvulitis.
This ailment, called uvulitis, is a moisture that gathers in the eye resembling descending water, but it is not. The method of treatment is that you seat the patient on a chair upright, then take his head in your hands from both sides.
And move it until you see the moisture descend to the lower part with your eye, then it stabilizes and does not move, and the light emerges, and the patient sees things as he used to see them. And if it does not descend to the lower part, we know that it is water. If the descent of the moisture is not achieved by what we mentioned,
otherwise, seat the patient before you, then take a thin scalpel and incise with it slightly above the cornea at the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the limbus until the material comes out.
When it comes out, instill in the eye warm water in which honey has been mixed, or water in which fenugreek and honey have been boiled, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have mentioned the types of water in the classification and which of them is suitable for cupping, with a detailed and explained statement, so take it from there correctly. Then you should seat the patient before you, sitting squarely facing the light near the sun.
And you bind his healthy eye and tie it tightly, then you lift the eyelid of his eye with your left hand if the eye with the water is the left eye, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then you place the tip of the cupping instrument near the cornea, with the thickness of a probe, in the same white of the eye on the side of the smaller caruncle, then you push the cupping instrument forcefully while rotating it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel with the cupping instrument that it has reached something empty.
And it should be that the extent to which the cupping instrument goes into the depth is the extent of the distance from the pupil to the end of the blackness, which is the cornea, for the copper is seen in the same vision of the observer, by the clarity of the corneal membrane, then you move the cupping instrument upwards to the place where the water is.
Then you press it downwards again and again; if the water descends immediately, then the patient will see what his sight has opened to immediately, with the cupping instrument in his eye, then it settles a little; if the water rises, then lower it again without removing the cupping instrument.
If it settles and does not rise, then remove the cupping instrument gently while twisting it with your hand little by little, then dissolve in the water some pure Andarani salt and wash the eye with it from the inside, then apply to the outside of the whole eye a compress or wet wool.
With rose oil and egg white, and bind with it the healthy eye; but as for us and the people of our time, we place on it crushed cumin with egg white. If the cupping instrument does not enter the eye because of its hardness—for some people have very hard eyes— Then you should take the scalpel called the *barīd*, whose image is this:
Then pierce with it only the adherent part and do not exaggerate in the piercing; it is only that you make a delicate place for the cupping instrument, then insert the cupping instrument as we mentioned. When your work is complete, prepare for the patient a firm bed in which he sleeps on his back in a dark room.
And he should abstain from all movements and from coughing, and make his food whatever softens his constitution, and he should not move his head to the right or left at all. The bandage should remain in place until the third day, then untie it in that dark room and test his sight.
Show him things, then reapply the bandage until the seventh day.  It is not appropriate to do this during treatment or after cupping at your hour, but you should avoid it before, lest the water quickly ascend with intense looking.
If a hot swelling appears, then you should untie the eye before the seventh day and treat that with whatever calms the swelling, until when it subsides, then you release his sight and place a veil on his face that trains his sight from under it for some days while he is in that dark room, then...
He leaves the house gradually and manages his causes. And know that the learner cannot dispense with observation many times in cupping, then he proceeds to work. And it has reached me from some Iraqis that he mentioned that he makes in Iraq a cupping instrument that is perforated, by which he sucks out the water.
And I have not seen anyone in our country make that, nor read it in any book of the ancients. It may be that it is recent.  And this is an image of types of cupping instruments for the ignorant to stand upon:
It is made of copper, especially, and its tip is of this very thinness, triangular-tipped and sharp.
Chapter twenty-four.
In the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
Various kinds of superfluous flesh may grow in the nose; some resembling a scorpion with many legs, some cancerous flesh, hardened and dark in color, and some soft flesh, not dark in color.
If any of this flesh is soft, not malignant nor cancerous, then the patient should sit before you facing the sun, you should open his nostril, and insert the snare into that flesh, then draw it outwards, and then cut what you have grasped of it with a fine, sharp scalpel on one side.
Until you know that all the flesh has gone. If any remains that you could not cut, remove it gently with one of the fine instruments, so that nothing remains. If the bleeding is excessive or a hot swelling appears,
treat it accordingly. If it is one of the malignant tumors, act quickly to excise it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone. Then place vinegar and water or a drink in the nose after the cutting, if the nose opens and moisture passes from it to the throat.
Know that he is healed. If the moisture does not flow as it should, know that inside there is flesh growing on the weakened bones which the instrument did not reach with the cutting. Then you should take a moderately thick linen thread.
and tie many knots in it, making the space between each knot the width of a finger or less. The patient should skillfully insert one end of the thread into his nose with a probe or whatever is possible after making it like a button, and draw it through until it reaches the pharynx.
and it will come out through his throat. Children in school often do this, and it is easy for anyone who wants to. Then bring together the two ends of the thread, the one that came out of the mouth and the other that remained in the nose.
Then use the knots in the thread to saw the flesh, doing so until you know that the flesh has been severed by the knots of the thread. Then remove the thread and put in the nose, after wiping away the blood, a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment. Do this for three days or more.
until the ointment consumes all the remaining flesh. Then, as a final step, place a lead tube in the nose for several days until it heals. If it needs drying treatment, use that.
This is an image of the instrument with which oils and medications are dropped into the nose:
It is made of silver or copper, resembling a small, open, oiled candlestick, and its channel is likewise.
And if you wish, make the tube closed like a reed, and the surface of the instrument flat.
It has a handle at its end, as you see, to hold it when you heat oil or whatever you wish of juices or flowing substances in it.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
Concerning warts growing on the tip of the nose.
A wart often grows on the tip of the nose, and it increases and grows larger with the days until its appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut it off at its first appearance and to excise it completely, then apply to the place either cautery or a burning medicine which takes the place of cautery. If you fail to cut it off until it becomes large, then look: if it is hardened, solid, dark in color, and insensitive,
do not use the iron on it, for it is a cancerous tumor; and I have often seen that cutting this tumor resulted in a great calamity for its owner. But if the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that it can be completely cut, then let the patient empty his bowels and cut it without hesitation or delay, and treat the place with what dries and contracts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On suturing the nose, lip, and ear. If their connection is separated by a wound or something similar, know that when a separation of connection occurs in one of these cartilages, the treatment rarely works except in some people.
Then, if something like that happens to someone, see if the wound is fresh with its blood; gather the edges of the wound with sutures, then treat it until it heals. And if the separation of the connection has separated its edges and each part has become sound, then you should scrape each part to the thickness of its outer skin until it bleeds.
Then gather the edges with sutures and tighten them, and sprinkle on them mastic and frankincense, powdered, and place on top of the sutures a plaster of palm-tree ointment or another of the uniting ointments, and leave it bandaged for two or three days, then untie it.
And change the medication and leave it until the threads separate themselves, then treat it with ointment until it heals.  The method of suturing is to bring together the separated connection, either with needles as we described in suturing the abdomen, or with thread as I showed you there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On removing the knots which appear in the lips.
Many people develop small, hard tumors inside their lips; some resemble a carbuncle, others are smaller or larger. You should evert the lip and incise over each nodule and extract it with a hook.
And cut it from each side, then pack the area after cutting with powdered alum until the bleeding stops, then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the area with something astringent until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On cutting away excess gum.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums, which the ancients called epulis. You should hook it with a hook or grasp it with forceps and cut it at its base, and let the blood flow, then apply powdered alum to the area.
Or one of the astringent, drying powders. If that flesh returns after treatment—for it often does return—cut it again and cauterize it; for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On extracting teeth with iron.
Rough, unsightly crusts may accumulate on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums.  These may become black, yellow, or green, even causing damage to the gums and making the teeth unsightly.
You should seat the patient before you, his head in your lap, and clean the tooth or teeth where you see crusts and things resembling sand until nothing remains.  Do the same with blackness, greenness, yellowness, and anything else until you have cleansed them.
If the substance is removed by the first cleaning, good. Otherwise, repeat the cleaning on another day, and then a second and third time, until you achieve your goal. Know that a molar requires many different types of instruments, various shapes, according to what suits your work.
For the instrument used to clean a molar from the inside is not the same as that used to clean it from the outside, nor that used to clean between the molars, which is of another form.  These are several forms of instruments that you should have, all prepared.
Chapter Thirty.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
You should treat the molar for its pain by every means, and delay its extraction, for there is no replacement if it is extracted, because it is a noble substance, except when its extraction is unavoidable.
If the patient decides on its extraction, you should make sure that the aching tooth is correctly identified. For often the patient is deceived by the pain and thinks it is in the healthy tooth, and extracts it, then the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is extracted.  We have seen this done repeatedly by barbers.
Then, if you have correctly identified the aching tooth, you should then incise around the tooth with a strong scalpel to loosen the gum on all sides, then move it slightly with your fingers or with gentle forceps, little by little, until you loosen it.
Then, firmly grasp it with the large forceps, and hold the patient's head between your knees so that it is steady and does not move. Then pull the tooth straight out, so as not to break it. If it does not come out, then take one of those instruments and insert it gently under it from each side, and try moving it as you did before.
And if the tooth is perforated or decayed, you should fill the hole with a piece of cloth and pack it tightly with the end of a thin probe, so that it does not crumble when you pull it with the forceps. You should also thoroughly incise around the gum on every side.
And take care not to break it, for if some of it remains, it will cause the patient greater harm than his initial pain. And beware of doing what the ignorant barbers do, in their haste and recklessness in extracting it without using what we have described.
For they often inflict great harm on people, the least of which is that the tooth breaks and all or part of its roots remain. Or it may extract some of the jawbone, as we have often witnessed. Then, after extraction, rinse with a drink or vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs at the site—and this often happens—then crush some alum and pack it into the site.
Otherwise, cauterize it if the alum does not help you. The image of the delicate forceps with which you first move the tooth: they should have long ends, short handles and be thick, so that they do not bend when you grasp the tooth with them. And this is the image of the large forceps.
As you see, they have thick handles so that when you grasp them, they do not yield or bend; they have short ends, and are made of Indian iron or well-tempered steel, with sharpened ends, and teeth at the tip which interlock, so that they grip firmly and tightly.
The ends may also be made in the shape of a file, and will then also be strong in their grip.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On extracting the roots of molars and removing fractured jaw bones.
If, when extracting a tooth, a root remains broken off, you should place cotton soaked in butter on the area for a day or two until the area relaxes, then insert a hook or forceps. whose ends resemble the beak of the bird called the balargah.  This is an image of the forceps:
They should be made like a file on the inside, or like a scalpel. If you are unable to remove it with these forceps, you should excavate the root and expose all the flesh with a scalpel, then insert an instrument resembling a small lever, which is depicted here:
short and slightly thick at the end, and not pointed, lest it break. If the root is removed by this, well and good; otherwise, use these other instruments, which are depicted here: the first has a triangular end and is somewhat thick.
Another fine triangular image:
We may also use this instrument with two branches, which is also depicted here:
and other instruments and tools previously mentioned in the extraction of teeth. We may also use this instrument which resembles a large hook, which is depicted here:
triangular, with a curved end, slightly thick so that it does not break, and not pointed. And know that the instruments for teeth are numerous, and so are all the other instruments; they are almost innumerable, and the skillful and expert craftsman may invent for himself instruments according to what the work and the diseases themselves indicate to him.
For there are diseases for which the ancients did not mention instruments, due to the variety of their types.  If a bone of the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth is broken or decays, then investigate its condition in its place with one of these instruments and forceps which were mentioned in the extraction of roots.
And we use a curette, its image is this:
It should be somewhat thick to control the bone so that it does not escape until the bone comes out, and we treat the area with suitable medications. If the bone has a slight decay, then remove its decay and blackness until it is clean, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
Concerning the extraction of molars growing upon others.
If molars grow other than in their natural course, this is an unsightly appearance, especially if it occurs in women and slaves.  It should be examined; if the molar has grown behind another tooth,
and its extraction or pushing back is not possible, then extract it.  And if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, whose image is this:
It resembles a small beak and should be made of sharp Indian iron, very sharp at the tip.  Your cutting should be done over many days because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that you do not shake other teeth. But if it is prominent and firmly rooted, then file it down with an Indian file; its image is this.
It should be entirely of Indian [steel], and its point should be very finely engraved, like the file with which needles are made.  File the tooth down little by little over many days gently, so that you do not shake the tooth and cause it to fall out.  Then smooth it and remove it with some forceps.
And if a tooth is broken, and it hurts the tongue when speaking, then it should also be filed until the roughness of the break is removed, and it is smooth and even, and does not hurt the tongue nor impair speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On binding loose teeth with silver or gold threads.
If the front teeth become loose and move due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on anything to eat for fear that they will fall out, and you have treated them with astringent medicines but the treatment has not been effective.
The solution is to bind them with a gold or silver thread, gold being preferable because silver tarnishes and decays after a few days, whereas gold remains unchanged forever and is not subject to this. The thread should be of moderate thinness and thickness, according to what fits between the teeth.
The method of binding is to take the thread and insert its loop between the healthy teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until you reach a healthy tooth on the other side by weaving.
Then you return the weaving to the side where you began and pull gently and skillfully so that they do not move at all.  Your pulling of the thread should be at the roots of the teeth lest it come loose. Then you cut off the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them together, twist them with a small instrument, and hide them between the healthy and the loose tooth.
So that it does not injure the tongue. Then leave it thus tightened for as long as it remains. If it becomes loose or breaks, tighten it with another thread, and he will enjoy it thus his whole life. And this is an image of the teeth and the manner of the binding in two healthy teeth and two loose teeth as you see.
And it may be that one or two teeth, after falling out, are returned to their place and bound as we have described, and remain. This is done by a skilled dentist. He may also carve a piece of ox bone and shape it like a tooth and place it in the place from which the tooth has gone. And he tightens it as we said, and it remains, and he enjoys it.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
Concerning the cutting of the ligament which appears under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament which appears under the tongue may be either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from a wound which has healed.
And the procedure is that you open the mouth of the patient and place his head in your lap and raise his tongue, then cut that nervous ligament transversely until the tongue is released from its restraint. If there is some stiffness and complexity, and this is from the healing of a wound, then place a hook in it and split it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knots are undone.
And beware that the incision is not deep in the flesh, lest you cut an artery there and bleeding results. Then, let him rinse his mouth after the cutting with rose water or with vinegar and cold water, then place under the tongue a pledget of linen which the patient holds in place each night.
Lest it reunite. If bleeding occurs, place powdered alum on the place. If the bleeding overcomes you, cauterize the place with a lentil-shaped cautery suitable for this. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-five.
In removing a frog that has formed under the tongue.
A swelling resembling a small frog sometimes occurs under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function, and it may even grow large enough to fill the mouth. The procedure is to open the patient's mouth towards the sun and examine the swelling.
If you see it dark-colored or black and hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, do not interfere with it, for it is cancer. But if it is inclined to white and moist, pierce it with a probe and incise it with a fine scalpel, freeing it from every side.
If blood overwhelms you during your work, apply powdered alum until the bleeding stops, then resume your work until you have completely removed it. Then have the patient rinse the mouth with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with other appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
In the treatment of tonsil tumors.
And what grows in the throat from other tumors.  Glands may appear inside the throat resembling those that appear externally, called tonsils. If you treat them with what I mentioned in the section and they do not heal,
then look: if the tumor is hard, dark in color, and insensitive, do not approach it with iron. And if it is red in color and its base is thick, do not approach it with iron either, for fear of bleeding, but leave it until it matures, for either you will lance it or it will break open by itself.
And if it is white in color, round, and its base is thin, this is what should be cut.  The procedure is that you should look before operating whether its hot swelling has completely subsided or diminished somewhat.
Then seat the patient facing the sun, his head in your lap, and open his mouth. Take an assistant, and he will press down his tongue with an instrument of this form:
It is made of silver or copper, thin like a knife. When you press the tongue with it and the tumor is revealed to you and you see it, take a hook and pierce it into the tonsil and pull it outward as much as possible without pulling along any of the membranes.
Then cut it with an instrument of this form, resembling scissors except that its ends are curved, each end being very sharp, next to the other. It is made of Indian iron or Damascene steel:
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a scalpel of this form:
Sharp on one side and not sharp on the other. After you cut one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner. Then, after cutting, gargle the patient with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate peel or myrtle leaves or similar astringents have been boiled until the bleeding stops. Then treat him until he recovers. Other tumors besides the tonsils may grow in the throat; cut them as I have described for the tonsils.
I treated a woman who had a tumor that had grown inside her throat, resembling a polyp, somewhat insensitive, which almost blocked her throat. The woman was breathing through a narrow passage and was prevented from eating and drinking water.
She was on the verge of death if she had remained for a day or two. Two branches extended from the tumor, emerging from her nostrils. I acted quickly, inserted a hook into one of them, and pulled; a suitable piece was extracted.
Then I cut it where I reached it through the nostril.  Then I did the same with what protruded from the other nostril. Then I opened her mouth, pressed down her tongue, inserted the hook into the tumor itself, and cut some of it; only a little blood flowed.
The woman's throat opened, and she immediately began to drink water, then she ate. We repeatedly cut away parts of the tumor over a long time; the tumor repeatedly replaced what was cut, until this went on for a long time for both of us.
Then I devised a plan and cauterized the tumor inside the throat with a cautery; it stopped growing. Then she traveled away, and I do not know what God did with her afterwards.
Chapter thirty-seven.
On cutting a uvular tumor.
That which is called an `anaba` if it descends as a growth to the uvula and swells and is white and elongated; if it is thicker at the bottom and round, it is called an `anaba`. If it is treated with what we mentioned in the section and the treatment is not effective,
and you see that the hot tumor has subsided from it and it is thin, then it should be cut, and whatever is gathered and round and does not have length, and is bloody or the color of a boil, or black, or insensitive, then it should be avoided cutting.
For there is danger to the patient. Therefore, if you see it in the manner I described, of its whiteness and length, then seat the patient facing the sun and compress his tongue with the instrument whose description precedes, then pierce the `anaba` with the hook.
And pull it downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments which I mentioned in cutting the tonsils. And it should not be cut except that which exceeds the natural state without excess, for if you cut more of it, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after cutting, use what we described in cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient is afraid of cutting it, then it is necessary to use a method of cauterizing it without fear or caution. The way to cauterize it is with a sharp medicine, and that is to place the patient's head in your lap.
Then compress his tongue with the instrument we mentioned, then take some of the sharp water which I mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and mix it with unquenched lime, and make it neither thick nor thin, and fill the concavity of this instrument with it.  And this is its image:
Its end, in which you place the medicine, has a concavity like the concavity of a spoon, and you place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself, and the patient lying on his side so that the saliva flows from his mouth from inside his throat, lest anything descend from it into his throat and harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine while you squeeze it on the uvula for about half an hour until you see it has blackened and the sting of the medicine has subsided. And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe and wet the cotton in the medicine.
And insert the probe with the cotton into the tube from above until the cotton adheres to the uvula. Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired cauterization of the uvula, then leave it. It will wither and fall off after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, reapply it.
After cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in butter and use it to dry the medicine around it, then rinse with cold water, and treat from the outside with lotions and from the inside with gargles until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and further from fear.
The uvula may also be treated with something gentler than cauterization and cutting; it is treated with fumigation in this manner: take fumitory, hyssop, savory, rue, wormwood, chamomile, and cassia, and similar herbs. Gather them all, or some of them, in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
Boil them, with the pot plastered with potter's clay, and there should be a hole in the middle of the lid into which a hollow instrument is fitted; this is made of silver or copper.
Insert the end with the rose head into the patient's mouth until the steam rises to the uvula through the tube, until the uvula is well steamed. Then repeat several times until it withers. And do not perform this treatment at the first onset of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling.  Rather, it should be done when the hot swelling subsides. If you do not have this instrument, take a reed and fit an eggshell to the end so that the patient's mouth does not burn.
Because the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth, and this is a good treatment and safe.
Chapter thirty-eight.
On removing thorns and other things that lodge in the throat.
Often a bone, fishbone, or something else lodges in the throat.  It is necessary to remove what is visible to the eye, after compressing the tongue with the instrument, towards the sun.
So that you may discern what is in the throat.  And as for what does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary to induce vomiting in the patient before his food is digested in his stomach, for perhaps the lodged object will come out with the vomit; or the patient may swallow a piece of turnip or a root of lettuce.
Or he may swallow a piece of dry bread, or you may take a piece of soft sea sponge, tie it to a string, and then he swallows it; and when it reaches the place of the thorn, pull the string quickly; do this repeatedly.
Often the thorn or bone will stick to it and be removed. If it does not come out by what we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument of this shape:
It should be slightly thicker than an awl, and at its tip a hook. The patient inserts it gently into his throat, raising his head upwards, and taking care not to touch his larynx lest he cause coughing and push the bone or thorn; or the physician inserts it with his hand.
And it is better for the patient to insert it himself, for he knows the location of the lodged object, and pushes downwards or pulls upwards with the instrument, all this as he is able, until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On removing leeches lodged in the throat.
If you treat the leech with what we mentioned in the section on medicinal treatment and it does not work, then look into the patient's throat in the sunlight after you have depressed his tongue with the instrument which I described to you.
If you see the leech, then draw it out with a small hook or with a fine, firm probe.  If you cannot manage it otherwise, take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, then insert into the hollow of the tube a piece of iron heated in the fire.
Do this repeatedly, and the patient should abstain from water all that day. Then he should take a jug full of cold water and open his mouth into it and rinse his mouth with it, but should not swallow a drop, and move the water from time to time with his hand.
For the leech will fall away if it feels the water. If it does not come out as described, fumigate the throat with urine and with asafoetida with the instrument which I described in the fumigation of the tonsils; do this repeatedly, for it will fall out.
The method of fumigation is that you take a pot containing live coals heated in the fire, the pot being covered with a lid in the middle of which is a hole. You fit into that hole the end of the instrument, then you throw in the fumigant. The patient puts his mouth to the end of the tube and closes his mouth so that the fumigant does not escape, until he knows that the fumigant has reached his throat.
Then the leech will fall away. If it does not fall, then repeat the fumigation. The patient should endure thirst and eat salt and garlic and drink no water; then it must come out by this method. And this is the image of an instrument by which the leech is drawn from the throat if it is seen.
It resembles forceps, as you see, except that it has this curve which enters the throat, and its ends resemble the beak of a bird; they have the roughness of a file, so if they grip something, they do not let go.
And another instrument, with which the tongue is compressed; it is a wide ring of iron, as large as will fit in the mouth above the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth standing on its side, so the mouth remains open without the will of the patient, and he cannot remove it at all until it is taken away.
The description of the instrument with which the mouth is opened in this very way.
Chapter Forty.
Herein is made mention of the cauterization and incision of tumors.
Tumors are of many kinds and varied, according to what will be mentioned one by one in this book, and they differ in their cauterization and incision in two aspects: one, from the type of tumor itself and what it contains of humors.
And the second type is from the locations in which they occur in the body, for a tumor occurring in the head is not like a tumor occurring in the rectum, and a tumor occurring in a fleshy location is not like a tumor occurring in a joint; for each of them there is a ruling on treatment.
And of tumors, there are some that should not be cauterized except after the ripening and completion of the pus within them, and some that should be cauterized while they are raw and have not completely ripened, such as tumors that are close to the joints.
For if a tumor occurs near a joint and its condition persists until it corrupts what is around it, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, and thus become a cause of the crippling of that limb, or the tumor may be near a principal organ.
Because if you delay its cauterization until it ripens, you will harm that principal organ; or it may be near the rectum, so cauterize it raw.  It is necessary to cauterize a raw tumor, not completely ripened, which is near the rectum, lest it corrupt the depth and penetrate into the interior of the rectum, and become a fistula.
And it becomes to a degree that it will not heal.  And you should know the time for cauterizing tumors that have completely ripened, which is when the pain of the tumor subsides, the fever disappears, the redness and throbbing decrease, and the head of the tumor becomes pitted and all other signs appear.
And it is preferable to perform the cauterization in the lowest part of the tumor, if that is possible, so that the flow of pus may be easier downwards; or in the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor. Let the cauterization be lengthwise if the tumors are in the hands or feet.
And the locations of muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries; in short, in all flat locations that do not bend.  But those that bend, let the cauterization follow the shape of that location.  But if the tumor is in fleshy locations, it is best to leave it uncauterized.
Until its ripening is complete, as we have said; for if you cauterize it before that, the flow of pus from it will be prolonged, and it will be very harmful and dirty, and perhaps its lips and depth will harden.  And some tumors may be cauterized across the body, when necessary, or according to the needs of the member.
And it is necessary to use a single cauterization for small tumors, and for large tumors, a wide cauterization or many incisions, according to the size of the tumor.  There may be some tumors that it is necessary to shave the skin and cut it, if it has become like a rag.
