And it has reached a point where it has died, like what happens in many boils and abscesses.  And some of them are incised with a three-sided incision, and some of them are cut in the shape of an ivy leaf, like the inguinal tumor.
And some of them use a circular incision and a crescent-shaped incision and similar incisions.  And what does not have a head, like smooth, flat tumors, it is necessary to cauterize it with a simple cauterization only. And it is necessary, if the tumor is large and has gathered for a long time and you cauterize it, not to rush so that all the pus comes out at that time.
But remove some of it, then dress the tumor until another day, then remove some of the pus also.  Do this repeatedly gradually until you remove all of it.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old man, for the vital spirit often dissolves with the sudden exit of the pus, and perhaps the patient dies and you do not realize it. Therefore, beware of this matter with great caution.
After cauterizing these tumors, it is necessary to wipe the wound and look. If the wound of the tumor is small or the incision is one and simple, then use lint from linen or old cotton. And if the tumor is large and the incisions of the cauterization are many,
it is necessary to insert into each incision a tent so that some of them reach each other. And if some of the skin of the tumor has been cut or scraped, it is necessary to pack it with old cotton or linen lint without moisture and dress it until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar after you soak a linen cloth in them and apply it to the bleeding site repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, it is necessary to use the procedures, treatment, and styptics that we have described in many places in this book and in the division. If you are in winter and the location of the tumor is very sensitive, it is necessary to wet the bandages with warm wine and oil.
And place them on the site. And if you are in summer and the sites are fleshy, it is necessary to apply the bandages soaked in water and oil or in wine and oil, all of it cold, until the third day, as we said. Then it is necessary to unwrap the tumor and wipe it.
And use something similar in its treatment until it heals. This is what you need to know about the general treatment of tumors. As for the detailed method, I have mentioned how to treat each tumor, categorized and summarized.
Chapter forty-one.
Concerning the incision of tumors that appear in the scalp.
Small tumors appear in the scalp; they are a type of boil, contained within membranes, and they have conditions like a chicken’s crop.  Their types are many; some are fatty, and some contain a moisture resembling mud.
And some contain a moisture resembling pus and broth and the like, and some are hardened and solid.  All of them are not dangerous to incise and remove, unless a vein obstructs you during the incision.
The procedure for incising them is to first probe them with the instrument whose image comes after this, which is called the “muddass,” until you know what it contains. If what it contains is moisture, then incise it lengthwise with a simple incision in this manner:
Begin the incision from point B to point C.  When the moisture has emptied, then peel away the sac that contained that moisture and cut it all away, and do not leave any of it at all, for it often recurs if any of it remains.
Then dip cotton in the “marham surri” if it is present, otherwise in salt water, and fill the wound with it, and leave it until the next day, for it will consume what remains of the sac. Then apply the cotton with the “surri” again, a second and third time if needed.
Until you are certain that nothing of the sac remains. Then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. And if the swelling contains fatty material, then incise it in this manner:
With a firm incision, and place hooks in the lips of the wound and peel it from every side, and exert yourself in removing the peritoneum that contains it. If an artery obstructs you, then do as I described to you before, and similarly do in the swelling if it is hardened from the incision.
And the treatment is the same as I mentioned, and the incision on the hardened swelling is easier because it has little blood and moisture. And it may be that some of these swellings which are in the head in some people have no moisture with them at all, and that is because I incised a swelling in…
The head of an old woman, and I found the swelling like hard, rough, white stone, which could not be broken, and if someone threw it, it would cut him. And it was not like other swellings occurring in the head, except for these, like the swellings which appear in the heads of children.
And at the roots of the ears, then incise them all with a simple incision, and make their bottom from their lower parts so that the flow of the material is easier to the bottom, then treat them with what suits them of treatment.
Chapter forty-two.
Concerning the incision on the swellings which frequently appear in the neck. These swellings frequently appear in the neck and under the armpits and in the groins, and they may appear in the rest of the body. And that which appears in the neck of them may be one, or it may be many, and some of them develop from others.
And every swelling of them is within a special membrane, as is the case in the abscesses and swellings of the head as we have described. And the types of these swellings are many; some of them are calcified, and some of them contain fluids, and some of them are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
So what I saw of them in good condition to the touch, and their appearance was close to the color of the skin, moving to every direction, and it was not adhering to a nerve of the neck, nor to a vein, nor to an artery, nor was it deep-seated. Then it should be incised with a simple incision from above to below the body, in this manner, from line J to line B.
And it should be dissected from every side, and the lips of the skin should be stretched with a hook or with two hooks, or with many hooks if that is needed, as we said in the swellings of the head, and it should be removed little by little, and it should be on the neck so that a vein or nerve is not cut, and let the scalpel not be very sharp.
Lest your hand increase the cutting, or the patient is disturbed, and you cut what you do not need to cut.  If you cut a vein or an artery and it hinders you from work, then place in the wound powdered verdigris or some of the styptics which stop blood.
And tighten the wound and leave it until the intensity of the swelling subsides and the wound relaxes and begins to suppurate, for the bleeding then stops.  Then return to your work until you finish, then examine with your index finger if there remain other small worms, then cut them and clean them.
If in the root of the worm there is a large vein, then it should not be cut from its root, but it should be tied with a doubled, tight thread and left until it falls off by itself without harm. Then stuff the wound with old cotton which has been soaked in Egyptian ointment, then treat it.
And if you cut the whole worm, then you should bring together the lips of the wound and stitch it immediately after you know that no particle remains. If you see that a particle of the bone of the worm remains,
then you should aim at cutting to its root and base, and use stitching and what we have mentioned. And as for worms which contain fluids, lightly cauterize them where their ripeness appears, and make the cautery from below, as we have said.
Then after cauterization, use a dressing with Egyptian ointment or the like to consume what remains of the corruption, until when you see that the wound has been cleansed, treat it with ointments which generate flesh until it heals.
Chapter forty-three.
Concerning the incision of the throat for a tumor which occurs inside the throat.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it. This is the account of their words. As for those with angina, they should avoid incision of the throat, since they do not benefit from it.
Because all the veins and the lung become diseased, but those with a hot tumor in the mouth, or throat, or tonsils, if there is no illness in the trachea, then it is necessary to use incision of the throat to escape the harm which results from suffocation.
Therefore, if we wish to do this, we should incise the throat below three or four rings of the trachea, a small transverse incision between two rings, to the extent that the incision is in the membrane, not in the cartilage. This location is suitable for the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are distant from it. If the surgeon is cowardly, then he should extend the skin of the throat with a hook, then incise the skin until he reaches the trachea, avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should incise the membrane which we have described.  One can determine the incision of the trachea from the phlegm which comes out of it, along with whatever is punctured, and from the cessation of sound. The wound is left open for a time. When the time during which strangulation was feared has passed, the edges of the wound are brought together from the skin.
And its outline alone, without the cartilage. Then use medicines which cause the growth of flesh until it heals. The author of this book said, the explanation of all this speech which we have related is only if they see that the patient's throat is obstructed by one of these tumors which have been mentioned.
And the patient is near death and his life is about to end, they proceed to incise the throat so that the patient may breathe somewhat through the wound and be saved from death. They are ordered to leave the wound open until the severity of the disease subsides, and its severity is about three days or so.
Then they are ordered to suture the wound and treat it until it heals. What I witnessed myself is that a maidservant took a knife and ran it across her throat, cutting part of her windpipe. I was called to treat her, and I found her weakening as one who is slaughtered weakens.  I uncovered the wound.
I found the blood which came out of the wound to be slight, and I was certain that she had not cut a vein nor an artery, and air was coming out of the wound. So I proceeded, and I sutured the wound and treated it until it healed. Nothing happened to the maidservant except for hoarseness in the voice, no more.
And after days she returned to her best condition. From this, we say that incising the throat is not dangerous.
Chapter forty-four.
Concerning the incision on the tumor which appears in the throat from the outside and is called the throat's elephantiasis.
This tumor which is called the throat's elephantiasis is a large tumor of the color of the body. It is frequent in women and is of two types.
It is either natural or accidental. As for the natural, there is no remedy for it; as for the accidental, it is of two kinds: one resembling fatty swellings, and the other resembling the tumor which arises from the complication of the artery; and there is danger in incising it. Therefore, one should never use the iron on it unless it is small; if you probe and examine it with a probe and find that it resembles a fatty swelling and is not connected to anything from the vessels, then incise it as you incise fatty swellings. And remove it with whatever contains it from the sac, if it is in a sac; otherwise, remove all of it completely, then treat the place with what is necessary of treatment.
Chapter forty-five.
Concerning the incision of various swellings.
Swellings are numerous in types, and I have mentioned all their types in the classification.  It is necessary to explain here the difference between a swelling and an abscess, since it is difficult.  So I say that with an abscess there is heat and fever and frightening pains until the boiling of the matter subsides. And the putrefaction is completed; then the fever and intensity subside.  A swelling, however, has neither heat nor fever nor pain, and it is contained in a serous sac, which is a special covering for it. It is the color of the body and its beginning is like a small boil, and it becomes like a blister, larger or smaller.
And it is of two kinds: either fatty, or containing moisture. The colors of the moisture are numerous, as I mentioned in the classification.  Therefore, if you proceed to the treatment of a swelling, you should probe and examine it first with the instrument called the *muddass*, whose image is given in the chapter following this.
Assess the size of the swelling, then remove the probe and look at what comes out after it. If a flowing moisture of any color comes out, then incise it with a simple incision as I have mentioned in the case of other swellings. If no moisture comes out after the probe, then know that it is fatty.
Then make a firm incision on it in this manner as I have taught you, and hook it with hooks.  Peel the skin from every side gently, and preserve the sac if you can, so that you may remove it intact with the swelling.
If the sac is ruptured during the operation and you cannot remove it intact—and this often happens—then remove it piece by piece until nothing remains of it. For if any part of it, however small or large, remains, the swelling will usually return.
If it overcomes you and a little remains, cauterize the wound at its completion with some sharp corrosive caustics, and tighten the wound and place over it something that soothes hot swelling and treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. If the swelling is large, then suture its lips and treat it with that which heals.
If you encounter a bleeding or non-bleeding vein and bleeding occurs, immediately cauterize the area with powdered vitriol and leave it constricted for two or three days until the wound suppurates and the flow of blood stops. Then return to removing what remains of the swelling.

Chapter forty-seven.
On the treatment of men's breasts.
The breasts of some people may swell at the age of puberty until they resemble women's breasts, remaining swollen and unsightly.  Whoever dislikes this should make a crescent-shaped incision on the breast in this manner.
From line B to line C. Then remove all the fat, then fill the wound with a cauterizing medicine, then bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and treat it until it heals. If the breast inclines downwards and becomes flaccid because of its size, as happens to women,
it is necessary to make two crescent-shaped incisions in its upper sides, each connecting to the other at their ends so that the larger line encircles the smaller one, in this manner: From line B to line C, then remove the skin between the incisions and remove the fat, and use what we have mentioned of sutures and dressings and whatever treatment is needed until it heals.
If you are unable to cut away what needs to be cut because of the patient's anxiety or because of bleeding, then you should pack the wound with cotton soaked in caustic ointment and leave it until it consumes the remaining fat, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter forty-eight.
On the treatment of tumors that appear under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are of the kind of hard, cystic swellings; some contain fluids.  Those that contain fluids should be incised with a crescent-shaped incision in this manner.
From line B to line C.  If all the fluid in the tumor is evacuated, then pack it with old cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
And treat it with the appropriate ointment until it heals. If the tumor is of the kind of nodular swellings, incise it as we mentioned in the incision for swellings. If the wound extends and pus forms, cauterize it as we previously mentioned.
Chapter forty-nine.
On incising a tumor that appears near an artery or vein, called an aneurysm.
If an artery is injured and the skin above it heals, a tumor often results; similarly, a vein may also swell and become tumorous.
The signs by which one knows whether the tumor and swelling are from an artery or a vein are as follows: if the tumor is from an artery, it will be oblong and deep-seated in the body; and if you press the tumor with your finger, you will feel a kind of crepitation.
That which is from a vein, the tumor is round and on the surface of the body. Incision of these tumors is dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many other places of the body, and when they are very large;  therefore, one should avoid treating them with the iron.
And those which are also in the extremities or in the head should be avoided.  As for those which are a swelling of the mouth of an artery, make a longitudinal incision in the skin, then open the incision with hooks, then dissect the artery and free it from the membranes.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle underneath and pass it to the other side, and tie the artery with a doubled thread in two places, as I have described to you in the ligation of the two arteries in the temples; then incise with a scalpel the area between the ligatures until all the blood in it is removed.
Then the tumor will resolve, and you will use treatment that produces pus until the ligatures fall off; then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the tumor is from a vein incision, you should grasp with your hand as much of the tumor as possible along with the skin, then insert a needle below the area you grasped with your hand and pass it through, with a doubled thread, until you bring it out on the other side. Then tie the tumor tightly with it, as I have mentioned to you in tying the uvula from both sides with the ends of the thread. If you fear that the threads will slip, then insert another needle with another thread under the entire tumor at the crossing point of the first needle.
And tighten your threads in four places, then incise the tumor in its middle until what is inside comes out; then cut off the excess skin and leave what was tied. Then apply a compress that you have soaked in a potion and oil. Then use treatment with cautery and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
On the tumor that occurs from the twisting of a nerve.
Just as a tumor occurs in the artery and vein, so a tumor occurs in the nerve if a blow happens to it or it happens from excessive exertion or the like. This is mostly in the regions of the wrists and heels.
And in every place where the joints move, it is a swelling that is deep-seated, its color resembling the rest of the body. In most instances, it is without pain, and if pressed firmly, the patient feels a kind of numbness. The swelling is not concentrated deep within the body.
Rather, it is under the skin, and it moves in every direction, but does not extend forward or backward. As for that which is in the joints, it should not be approached with iron, for it may cause gangrene. And as for that which is in the head or forehead...
then incise the skin with a scalpel. If the tumor is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it from its base. If it is large, hook it with a hook and peel it off, then remove it and close the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals.
Chapter fifty-one.
On the excision of warts which appear in the abdomen.
Often, some people develop warts on their abdomens and other parts of their bodies, called "fungal" because of their resemblance to mushrooms. Their base is thin and their head is thick, their lips may be altered, and some are small while others become very large.
And I saw a man who had two warts on his abdomen which were most like mushrooms, white and thin at the base, their lips altered and cracked, and moisture constantly flowing from them.  I cut them off and weighed one at about eighteen ounces and the other at about six ounces.
The procedure for cutting them is as follows:  If the patient is moist, and the wart is white, moist, and thin at the base, cut it with a broad scalpel, and have cauteries ready in the fire, for often much blood flows when they are cut.
If the bleeding overwhelms you, cauterize it immediately. If you see that the patient is cowardly and frightened by cutting with iron, take a strong lead thread and tie it tightly around the wart of this description, and leave it for two days, then increase the tightness of the lead, continuing to tighten the lead.
Tighten it repeatedly until it affects the wart, so that it separates and falls off by itself without trouble. If the wart is thick at the base, there is some fear and uncertainty in cutting it, especially those in the abdomen; however, it may be possible to cut off half or some of it.
Then cauterize it so that it does not return, and then treat it until the wound heals. Beware of cutting a wart that is dark in color, insensitive, and foul in appearance, for it is a cancerous tumor. We will discuss cancer later.
Chapter Fifty-Two.
On the treatment of umbilical hernias.
An umbilical hernia results from many causes: either from a rupture of the peritoneum of the abdomen, from which fat or intestine protrudes, as occurs in other hernias; or from blood emitted from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or from wind that is retained there.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and the protrusion of fat, then the tumor will be similar in color to the body, soft, painless, and of varying appearance. If it is due to the protrusion of the intestine, its appearance will be more varied than what we have described. If you press it with your fingers, it disappears and then returns; sometimes it is accompanied by gurgling, and it increases significantly with bathing and strenuous exertion. If it is due to moisture, it will be soft.
It does not disappear when pressed with your hand, nor does it increase or decrease. If it is due to blood, in addition to these signs, the tumor will appear dark. If it is due to newly formed flesh, the tumor will be firm, hard, and remain the same size.
If it is due to wind, it will be soft to the touch. The treatment is as follows: if the umbilical hernia is due to blood from an artery or vein, or to wind, then you should abstain from treating it, for there is danger and uncertainty in this, as I have informed you in the chapter where I described the tumors that occur from arteries and veins.
If the umbilical hernia is due to the intestine or fat, then you should instruct the patient to restrain himself and stand upright and straight, then apply a bandage around the entire navel. Then instruct him to lie on his back between your hands, and then incise with a wide scalpel around the navel at the place you marked with ink. Then pull the center of the tumor upwards with a large hook, and then tie the incised area with strong thread or silk sinew tightly.
The knot of the ligature should be a loop. Then open the middle of the tightened tumor above the ligature and insert your index finger. Search for the intestine; if you find that the ligature has grasped it, loosen the loop and push the intestine back into the abdomen. If you find fat, pull it out with a hook.
Cut off its excess. If an artery or vein obstructs you, ligate it carefully and return to your work. Take two needles and thread them with strong thread, and insert the needles into the incision you made around the tumor, crossing them, having passed them through. Then tighten the tumor in four places over the needles.
If you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the constricted flesh decays and falls off by itself, or cut it off when it becomes soft and decayed, then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals. If the umbilical hernia is from flesh that has grown there,
or from moisture, then you should constrict the tumor as we said, and remove the blood or moisture that you find there, then treat it with what will close the area.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of cancer and how to treat it with medication, and warned against treating it with iron, lest it ulcerate, and we have mentioned cancer that develops in the uterus and warned against treating it.
And the ancients mentioned that if the cancer is in a place where it can all be excised, like the cancer that is in the breast or in the thigh and similar accessible members, and its removal is possible in its entirety, especially if it is small and beginning; but if it has progressed and become large, then it should not be approached.
For I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve that.  The procedure, if it is accessible as we have said, is to first purge the patient with black hellebore several times, then to bleed him if there is evident fullness in the veins.
Then you position the patient in a position that allows you to work, then you apply hooks suitable for it to the cancer, then you scrape it from every side together with the skin, thoroughly, until nothing of its roots remains, and let the blood flow and do not stop it quickly.
Rather, squeeze the areas and drain all the thick blood with your hand or with whatever instruments you can. If during the procedure, profuse bleeding from a severed artery or vein obstructs you, cauterize the vein until the bleeding stops, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter fifty-four.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have explained in the classification the types of dropsy, how the accumulation of water occurs, and the signs of each type and its treatment with medications.  That which is treated with iron is only the ascitic type, and the tympanitic type and the fleshy type are not at all approached with iron.
For that is lethal, if you have treated this type of ascitic cancer with medication and your treatment has not been effective, then see if the patient is extremely weak or has another illness besides the cancer, such as…
a cough or diarrhea or the like, then beware of treating him with iron, for it is deception. If you see the patient strong, without illness other than the cancer alone, and he is neither a child nor an old man, then the procedure is to stand the patient upright before you. and a servant behind him squeezing his belly with his hands and pushing the water downwards towards the pubis, then take a lancet like this:.
limited on both sides, long, with a pointed end like a lancet, except that it has a slight curve so that it does not penetrate the intestine during the procedure and injure it. Then look, if the cancer has developed from the side of the intestines.
then it is necessary that you make the incision three fingers' breadth below the navel in line with it above the pubis. If the cancer has developed from liver disease, then let your incision be three fingers' breadth to the left of the navel. If its development is from spleen disease, then let the incision be on the right side by three fingers' breadth.
Also, the incision should not be on the side on which the patient wants to lie down, lest the fluids flow to that weak area. Then pierce the whole skin with the instrument, then insert the instrument into that incision and raise your hand.
with the lancet between the skin and the peritoneum as if you are flaying it, and the amount you flay should be about the size of a fingernail or so. Then pierce the peritoneum until the lancet reaches an empty place, which is the place of the water, then remove it and insert into the hole the instrument which is of this form.
It is like a tube of a thin reed made of silver or copper or smooth, polished lead. It has a small hole at its lower end and three holes in its sides, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And one may sharpen its end in the shape of a pen nib, in this form:
At its upper end is a ring.  If the instrument reaches the water, it will immediately descend on the instrument, and you will evacuate a moderate amount of water at once. For if you evacuate more than is necessary at once, the patient may die from the dissolution of his animal spirit.
Or he may suffer a faintness approaching death. But evacuate according to his strength and what indicates to you the patient's condition from the strength of his pulse and the good color of his complexion. Then remove the instrument, and the water will be retained, for it will be retained immediately.
Because of the skin which holds the hole on the peritoneum which I told you to suture in that manner. Then you may reinsert the instrument another day if you see that the patient can bear it.
And you will also remove a small amount of water.  Do this for several days, and you must be careful and guard against error, until only a little water remains. If you fear for the patient and leave a large amount of water, treat him by burying him in hot sand.
And profuse sweating in the bath and sun, and enduring thirst, and treat him with drying medicines until he recovers. And cauterize the stomach, liver, and spleen after removing the water as I described before.
Chapter fifty-five.
In the treatment of children who are born with their urinary passages unperforated, or with a narrow or misplaced opening.
Some boys may be born with their urethra imperforate; it is necessary to immediately perforate it at birth.
With a very fine scalpel, in this manner:
Then place a thin lead nail in the opening, tie it and keep it in place for three or four days. When he wants to urinate, remove it and then replace it. If you do not use the lead, it is possible that the urine flowing over the area will not allow it to close.
As for the one whose opening is narrow, it is treated with lead, as we have said, for many days until it widens.  Those whose opening is misplaced, such as those born with the opening at the end of the penis, cannot urinate forward.

Be careful during the procedure to avoid bleeding, as this often occurs. Stop the bleeding and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Six.
On the ulcer that occurs in the foreskin and penis, and on blackness, corruption, and adhesion of the foreskin to the penis.
This ulcer often occurs in the urethra; it is a fleshy, tough growth, and it can be malignant or non-malignant.  The non-malignant type should be tied off with a fine ligature.
Cut it until you remove it completely, then apply cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, then treat it afterwards with nut ointment until it heals. But if the ulcer is malignant and dark in color, cauterization should be used after it is cut and excised.
If the ulcer is in the foreskin of an uncircumcised man, and part of the ulcer is inside the foreskin and part outside, then you should remove the ulcer inside first, until it heals; then treat the outside.
Because if you treat them both at the same time, you risk perforation of the foreskin.  Blackness and corruption can also occur in the female genitalia and the foreskin; you should excise everything that is black and tending towards or already corrupted.
Then apply honey mixed with crushed and sifted pomegranate peel and acacia, and then treat it with other treatments until it heals. If bleeding occurs, use cauterization with a crescent-shaped cautery iron in this manner:
Cauterization is beneficial for both conditions, namely bleeding and a corrupted wound. If the glans is eroded and destroyed entirely at some point, a lead tube should be inserted into the urethra for the patient to urinate through.  As for the adhesion of the foreskin to the glans…
This adhesion occurs in those whose foreskin is intact and who have not been circumcised.  Adhesion may also occur due to a wound or swelling. You should separate it with a blunt scalpel until the constriction is released and the glans is freed on all sides.
If it is difficult to distinguish them completely, you should remove some of the glans and some of the foreskin. The foreskin is thin and may easily perforate because of its thinness. Then separate the foreskin from the glans with a thin linen cloth moistened in cold water, so that it does not adhere again.
Then treat it with an astringent drink until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Seven.
On the cleansing of boys and the treatment of what may befall them of error.
Circumcision is nothing other than the separation of a connection like other wounds, except that since it is done by our will and is used especially in boys, it is necessary to describe the best method for it.
And the easiest way leading to safety.  I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because it was not used in their laws, but rather it is something we have acquired.
by experience.  And that is that I found the majority of surgeons and barbers using purification with the knife and with the scissors, and they use the forceps and tying with thread and cutting with the fingernail.  I have tried all these methods.
I found nothing better than purification with scissors and tying with thread, because purification with the knife often harms the skin, because the skin of the foreskin is two layers, so perhaps the upper skin is cut and the lower layer remains.
So one is forced to make another cut and renewed pain, and purification with forceps does not prevent cutting the end of the urethra, because it may enter its hole. As for purification with the fingernail, the skin may slip and your work will be spoiled, or the boy's skin may be naturally short...
Therefore, they are often born like that and do not need purification. I have seen this. As for purification with scissors and tying with thread, experience has revealed its superiority to me, because the cut of the scissors is proportionate, because the blade above is like the blade below.
So when you squeeze your hand with the proportion of the two blades, you cut with one measurement and in one time. Thus the thread becomes like a wall for the skin of the urethra from all sides, with no mistake at all. The first step in the procedure is to reassure the boy.
Especially if he is one who understands a little, that you are only tying the thread in his urethra and leaving it until another day, then rejoice and please him in every way you can and what he accepts with his mind, then stand him before you, standing upright.
And let him not be sitting, and hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot, so that the boy's eye does not fall upon it at all, nor on any of the instruments. Then insert your hand into his urethra, inflate the skin, and lift it up until the head of the urethra comes out.
Then cleanse it of whatever dirt gathers there, then tie the marked place with a doubled thread, then tie a second one a little below it. Then hold the lower tie firmly with your thumb and index finger and cut between the two ties. Then quickly lift the skin upwards and bring out the head of the urethra, then let the blood flow a little; it is better and lessens the swelling of the urethra.  Then dry it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some burnt ash of dried gourd; it is the best I have tried, or finely ground barley; that is also excellent.
Then quickly lift the skin upwards and bring out the head of the urethra, then let the blood flow a little; it is better and lessens the swelling of the urethra.  Then dry it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some burnt ash of dried gourd; it is the best I have tried, or finely ground barley; that is also excellent. Then apply to the glans from above a pledget of white egg cooked in rosewater, beaten with fresh, good rose oil, and leave it on until the next day. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
And this is the image of the scissors which are suitable for circumcision:
It should be thin, sharp, without any curve, well-sharpened, and the length of the two blades should be equal to the length of the handles. As for the mistake which occurs in circumcision, it is sometimes that the inner foreskin is completely or partially inverted during the cutting; it should be extended at once with your fingernail before the place swells.
And cut it evenly. If you are unable to grasp it with your fingernail, then pull it with a hook and cut it. If three days pass and what is under the head of the urethra remains swollen and inflamed, then leave it until the acute inflammation subsides and remove it gently.
And cut it according to what is convenient for you, and be careful of the glans penis; for if something is cut from the glans penis, it does not harm, so treat it with what heals the wound from the cauteries which we described in the chapter on cauteries.
And if more than the necessary amount of skin is cut and it retracts upwards, that also does not harm much, so treat it as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter fifty-eight.
On the treatment of urine retention in the bladder.
Urine retention in the bladder occurs due to its obstruction by a stone, or clotted blood, or pus, or a growth of flesh, and the like.  If you treat that with what we mentioned of the types of treatment described in the classification.
And if the urine does not flow, and you see that its retention is due to a stone which has lodged in the neck of the bladder, then it is necessary that the patient sit on his knees, then one should mount on his back and extend his abdomen towards his back, and the patient should hold himself as much as he can.
Then the neck of the bladder is forced to push the stone backwards, and the urine flows. If it does not flow by what we mentioned, and the matter becomes severe for the patient, then it is necessary to use its extraction with the instrument called a catheter, whose image is this:
It is made of silver and is thin, smooth, hollow like a bird's quill in thinness, long about a span and a half, with a gentle funnel at its head. The way to draw the urine with it is to take a doubled thread and tie to its end wool or cotton firmly.
And insert the end of the thread into the bottom of the catheter and cut with scissors if any of the wool remains so that it enters the tube and closes it like a button. Then lubricate the catheter with oil, or butter, or egg white, and seat the patient on a chair.
And cleanse his bladder and urethra with moist ointments or oil and lukewarm water. Then gently insert the catheter into the urethra until it reaches the root of the urethra, then bend the urethra upwards towards the navel, then push the catheter inside it.
Until, when the urethra is fully bent, then push the catheter inside until, when it reaches near the anus, incline the penis downwards with the catheter inside it, then push it until it reaches the bladder and the patient feels that it has reached something empty.
This is done in this manner because the passage through which the urine passes is tortuous. Then gently pull the thread with the wool, for the urine will follow the wool, then remove it and the urine will flow out.  And return the catheter.
Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.
Chapter Fifty-Nine.
How to inject the bladder with a syringe.
And the instruments suitable for this are made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long tube as fine as pus.  And you wish to instill waters and medicines into it; this is done with an instrument called a syringe, and this is its image:
It is made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long tube as fine as a hair, hollow throughout except for the tip, which is solid and has three holes, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And the hollow part in which the propellant is, should be only as much as it will fill without excess, so that when you draw something of the moisture with it, it is drawn, and when you push it, it is pushed to a distance, like the syringe that throws oil in sea battles.
Then if you wish to inject moisture into the bladder, you insert the tip of the syringe into the moisture and draw upwards on the piston; then the moisture is drawn into the body of the syringe.  Then insert its tip into the urethra as we described in the catheters, and then push the moisture with the piston.
Then that moisture reaches the bladder immediately, until the patient feels it. And this is the image of a delicate clyster also with which the bladder is injected:
It is made of silver or lead, its upper end resembling a small funnel, and below it a groove in which the ligature lies. Then take a camel's bladder and put into it the moisture which you wish to inject into the bladder, and then bind it tightly between the two parts with a doubled thread.
And warm that moisture a little over the fire, then insert the tip of the clyster into the urethra, then press your hand firmly on the bladder with the moisture until the patient feels that moisture has reached the bladder.
If you do not have a bladder, take a piece of thin leather and make a circle from it and pierce it all around. Then insert a strong thread into those holes as it is twisted, then gather the circle as you gather a tablecloth, after you put in it what you want of moistures, ointments and water.
Then tie it to the instrument and do with it as you did with the bladder, pressing your hand until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter Sixty.
On the extraction of a stone.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of stones and their treatment with medications, and mentioned the difference between stones generated in the kidneys and stones generated in the bladder, and the signs of all that, and mentioned the stone in which work with the hand and incision is done.
This is the stone generated specifically in the bladder and that which becomes lodged in the urethra, and I am describing that with clear explanation and brevity.
So I say that the stone generated in the bladder mostly affects boys, and among its signs is that urine exits from the bladder similar to water in its thinness.
And sand appears in it, and he scratches his penis and plays with it, and often it hangs down, then spreads, and the anus protrudes with it in many of them.  It is easy to remove in boys until they reach fourteen years, and difficult in old men; as for young men, it is intermediate between those.
And the one whose stone is larger, his treatment is easier, and the small one is the opposite.  So if we proceed to treatment, it is necessary first that the patient be given an enema to remove all the fecal matter in his intestines, for it may prevent the stone from being found upon examination.
Then the patient is taken by his feet and shaken and jolted downwards so that the stone descends to the neck of the bladder, or he jumps from a high place repeatedly. Then you seat him before you upright, with his hands under his thighs, so that the entire bladder is inclined downwards, then examine him.
And palpate him externally. If you feel the stone in the space, then immediately begin to incise it. If it does not fall under your touch at all, then it is necessary to lubricate the index finger with oil from the left hand if the patient is a boy, or the middle finger.
If he is a fully developed boy, then insert it into his rectum and search for the stone until, when it is under your finger, you move it little by little to the neck of the bladder. Then press on it with your finger and push it outwards towards the place you intend to incise.
And you order a servant to press the bladder with his hand, and you order another servant to stretch the labia upwards with his right hand, and with his other hand to stretch the skin beneath the labia away from the place where the incision will be. Then take the sharp scalpel, whose image is this:
And incise between the rectum and the labia, not in the middle, but towards the left buttock.  The incision should be on the stone itself, with your finger in the rectum, and you pressing it outwards obliquely, so that the incision is wide on the outside and narrow on the inside, to the extent that the stone can exit.
Not larger, for perhaps the finger in the rectum will press the stone during the incision, and it will come out without difficulty. And know that there may be stones with angles and edges, which makes their removal difficult for that reason.
And some are smooth, resembling an acorn and round, and easily removed.  As for those with angles and edges, increase the incision slightly. If it does not come out like this, then you should try to grasp it, either by seizing it with a firm forceps whose end is like a file.
To hold the stone firmly so that it does not escape, or to insert a delicate, curved instrument beneath it. If you cannot manage it, widen the incision slightly. If blood overwhelms you, stop it with alum.
If there is more than one, push the largest first towards the opening of the bladder, then incise upon it, then push the smaller ones afterwards.  Do likewise if there are more than two. If it is extremely large, it is ignorance to make a large incision upon it, for this exposes the patient to one of two things.
Either death, or a constant dribbling of urine, because the wound will not heal at all. But try to push it out, or try to break it with forceps until you remove it in pieces.
When you have finished your work, cauterize the wound with myrrh, aloes, and acacia, and bandage it, and place over it a cloth moistened with oil and wine, or rose oil and cold water to soothe the inflammation. Then let the patient lie on his back, and do not remove the bandage until the third day.
When it loosens, wash the area with plenty of water and oil, then treat it with palm-tree ointment and basil-ointment until it heals. If excessive inflammation, suppuration, and the like occur in the wound, such as blood clotting in the bladder and preventing urination—and this is known by the appearance of blood in the urine—
insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood, for if it remains it will lead to corruption and putrefaction of the bladder. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type of complication with its appropriate therapy until it heals.
In all treatments, it is necessary to bind and bring together the thighs to secure the medications applied to the area. If the stone is small and has lodged in the urethra, preventing the passage of urine, treat it as I have described before it requires incision.
Often I have dispensed with incision using this treatment, which I have tested; it involves taking a probe of steel, which has this shape:
The tip is triangular, sharp, and fixed in a shaft; then take a thread and tie the penis below the stone, so that it does not return to the bladder; then gently insert the probe into the urethra until the probe reaches the stone itself.
And turn the probe in the stone itself little by little, intending to pierce it until you pass through to the other side; then the urine will immediately flow. Then press on what remains of the stone from outside the penis; it will break up and be expelled with the urine, and the patient will recover.
If this treatment is not possible due to some impediment, tie a thread below the stone and another above it; then incise over the stone in the penis itself between the two threads and remove it.
Then untie the ligature and clean away the clotted blood in the wound.  The reason for tying the thread below the stone is to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other ligature above is so that when the thread is untied after the stone is removed, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound.
Therefore, when you tie the upper thread, you should raise the skin upwards so that it returns and covers the wound when you are finished, as we said.
Chapter sixty-one.
On the removal of stones from women.
Stones are rarely generated in women.  If a woman is afflicted with a stone, its treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons. One is that the woman may be a virgin; the other is that you will not find a woman who permits herself to a physician.
If she is chaste or related, and thirdly, you will not find a woman skilled in this craft, especially in manual work, and fourthly, the incision site for stones in women is distant from the location of the stone, requiring a deep incision.
And in that there is danger. If necessity demands it, then a skilled female physician should be sought; they are rarely found. If you cannot find one, seek a chaste and gentle male physician, or procure a skilled midwife experienced in women's matters, or a woman who knows something of this craft.
Then prepare her and instruct her to do everything you order, first examining for the stone. That is, see if the woman is a virgin. Then you should insert a finger into her rectum and search for the stone; if you find it and press it under your finger.
Then instruct her to incise it. If she is not a virgin but a widow, then instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone after placing her left hand on the bladder and pressing it firmly.
If she finds it, then she should trace it from the opening of the bladder downwards as far as she can reach, until she reaches the groin.  Then she should incise it opposite the middle of the vagina at the groin, from whichever side the stone is felt in that area.
And her finger should not leave the stone, pressing on it. Let the incision be small at first, then introduce the dilator into that small incision. If she feels the stone, enlarge the incision as much as is needed for the stone to come out. And know that there are many types of stones.
Some are small, others large; smooth, rough; long, round, and branched. Know their types so that you can determine what you need. If the bleeding is excessive, sprinkle powdered verdigris on the area and hold it for a while until the bleeding stops, then return to your work.
Until the stone comes out. And be sure to have with you the instruments mentioned for removing stones from men, to assist you in your work. If the bleeding is excessive and you determine from the throbbing of the blood that an artery has been severed, then apply styptics to the area.
And bind it tightly with bandages and leave it; do not revisit it, and leave the stone and do not remove it, for the patient might die. Then treat the wound. When the severity of the bleeding subsides after a few days and the area has become infected, return to your work until the stone is removed.
Chapter Sixty-two.
On incision of the water bladder.
Hydrocele is an accumulation of moisture in the tunica albuginea, which is under the skin of the scrotum surrounding the testicle.  It may be in a special membrane prepared by nature on one side of the testicle.
So that one thinks it is another testicle. It is between the skin of the scrotum and the tunica albuginea that we mentioned, but this is rare. This hydrocele is generated from a weakness that affects the testicles, so that this substance collects there.  It may also result from a blow to the testicles.
This moisture is of many colors; it may be yellowish, or bloody red, or black and purulent, or white and watery—which is most common. The signs by which it is recognized are:
Where the water collects, if it is in the tunica albuginea that we mentioned, the swelling is roundish and slightly elongated, like the shape of an egg, and the testicle does not appear because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
If the moisture is in a special membrane, then the swelling is round on one side of the testicle.
Therefore, a person imagines it to be another testicle. If the moisture is between the skin of the scrotum and the tunica albuginea, then it is palpable.  But if you want to know the color of the moisture, explore the swelling with the square probe whose image we have shown previously; whatever comes out after the probe, you judge it by what is inside.
If we proceed to treatment with cautery, it is necessary to order the patient to be bled if he is able and you see his body is full. Then he lies on his back on something slightly elevated, and you place many cloths under him, and you sit on his left.
And you order a servant to sit on his right, stretching his penis toward one side of the scrotum and toward the region of the groin. Then you take a wide scalpel and incise the skin of the scrotum lengthwise in the middle to near the pubis.
Make the incision straight, parallel to the line that divides the scrotum in half, until it reaches the tunica albuginea.  Then strip it back, being careful not to cut it.  Your stripping should be more from the side attached to the testicle, and strip as much as you can.
Then you widely incise the tunica albuginea filled with water and remove all the water. Then you separate the edges of the incision with hooks and stretch the tunica albuginea upwards, but do not touch the containing skin of the scrotum, and cut the tunica albuginea however you can cut it, either all at once or piece by piece.
Especially its thin side, for if you do not completely cut it, you cannot be sure the water will not return. If the testicle emerges from its covering during your work, then when you have finished cutting the tunica albuginea, replace it and then close the edges of the scrotum skin with sutures.
Then treat it like other wounds until it heals. If the testicle is already damaged by another disease, then you should tie off the vessels in the spermatic cord for fear of bleeding, then cut the testicle from the spermatic cord and remove the testicle, then treat it as we have described.
If the fluid is collected in both sides, know that they are two hydroceles. Incise the other side as you did the first, and if it is convenient to do it all at once, do so, then wipe away the blood.
And insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil or rose oil, and externally apply more wool soaked in a drink and oil, and spread it over the testicles and the flanks of the abdomen, and place folded cloths on top of it, which are the pads. And tie it on top with a bandage that has six ends, in this manner:
Then two cloths are taken, sewn together, and stuffed with fluffy wool in this manner. Make the ties with wet cotton or wool, and tighten them with the waistband of the trousers, and one end is taken across the buttocks to the back.
The second is taken towards the pubis, and the two ends to the thighs below, and the remaining two ends to the groins, and all the tightening is gathered in the waistband of the trousers. This hydrocele may also be treated by cauterization instead of incision with an iron.
This is to take a fine, knife-like cautery and with it incise the skin of the testicle while it is hot, as we have described, until the white peritoneum containing the water is exposed; then take another cautery of this shape:
It resembles the Greek eye, then sear that peritoneum with it while it is hot until all the moisture comes out. Then spread the peritoneum with hooks and peel off the containing layer with the cautery and cut it as much as you can, until you excise all of it, and protect the testicle so that the fire does not touch it.
Know that this procedure is safer and prevents more blood loss, and is better than the incision method. If during the incision or cauterization you encounter a hot swelling, bleeding, or something else, then you should treat whatever arises with the appropriate treatment, as previously described.
If the patient is cowardly and cannot endure this procedure because of its unpleasantness, then use cauterization as I describe, if it is proven to you by the evidence we have presented.
If the hydrocele is watery, then the patient should sit on a raised chair, then sear the lower part of the swelling with a wide scalpel, and make the cauterization along the length of the body, and make the opening wide so that all the water flows out.  Then place cotton on the area and bind it.
And leave it to drain the remaining water, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not come out during cauterization, and this is because the tunica albuginea obstructs the mouth of the wound, preventing the water from coming out,
then you should insert a goose feather into the wound, or the instrument used to extract water from abscesses, the image of which has been shown previously, or enlarge the opening of the wound slightly.
And know that the water may return and accumulate after six months or so. If it accumulates, cauterize it in the same way, and the patient should observe his days of rest.
Chapter sixty-three.
Concerning the incision on the fleshy scrotum and its treatment.
Know that an incision on this scrotum is fraught with danger, leading to death in most cases. Therefore, we advise leaving it alone and ensuring its safety. I will, however, mention its treatment and the types of swelling.
I say that many swellings may occur in the bodies which constitute the testicles, and this is due to many causes; either from an excessive, acrid humor which descends to the testicles, or from a blow. The swelling is the color of the body and contains no water.
The swelling may be soft, or it may be hardened, its color dark, and without sensation. It may be due to a complication of the arteries and their swelling, as previously mentioned, or from a swelling of the veins.  That which is from a swelling of the arteries is recognized by the dispersion of the swelling.
If you press it with your fingers, it does not disperse.  It should not be incised. As for that which is from a swelling of the veins, nothing of it disperses when pressed with the fingers. This type may be incised, as may other flesh.
It is to incise the skin of the testicle, then to extend the testicle upwards and to remove it from the white peritoneum. Free the spermatic cord from the vessels, ligate the vessels, and cut the spermatic cord after freeing it from every part of the testicle; if the testicle is adherent to that growing flesh.
Then it is necessary to remove the testicle and cut it. And if the adhesion is between some of the peritonea or between the vessels, then it is necessary to free all that adhesion and cut it with a circular cut.
If the growth of flesh is in the place of adhesion which is behind, then it is necessary to cut all of it and remove the testicle as we said. When your work is completed, dress the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and syrup, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Four.
On the treatment of hydrocele with a hernia.
A hernia is a twisted tumor, somewhat twisted, resembling a bunch of grapes, with relaxation of the intestines and difficulty for the patient in movement, exercise, and walking.  The treatment of this disease is very close to what has been mentioned before.
However, it is necessary to mention the procedure as the ancients did. I say that it is necessary to have the patient sit on a high chair, then push the intestinal contents downwards, then grasp the scrotum with your fingers along with the vessels that are close to the penis.
And let an assistant hold it and stretch it strongly, then you incise with a wide, sharp scalpel an oblique incision along the vessels until the vessels are exposed, then dissect from each side as I have described to you in the dissection of the arteries in the armpits, then insert a needle with a doubled thread.
And tie it at the first place where the hernia appeared and tie it also at the end, then incise it in the middle with a vertical incision along the length of the body and remove what has gathered in it of the foul, putrid fluids.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds that you want to heal for a period of time so that the vessels that nourish one of the intestines fall off, there is no harm in that. If the hernia affects all the vessels, it is necessary to remove one of the intestines with the vessels, lest the testicle be deprived of nourishment. before cutting the vessels, so that they wither and are of no use.
Chapter sixty-five.
In the treatment of intestinal hernia.
This hernia occurs from a fissure that appears in the peritoneum extending over the abdomen near the groins from the flank of the abdomen, and the intestine descends from that rupture into one of the testicles.
And this rupture is either from a fissure in the peritoneum or from its extension, and these two types occur from many causes, either a blow, or a jump, or a shout, or lifting something heavy, and so on.  Its sign, if it is from the extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs little by little over a long time.
It does not occur suddenly, and the swelling is even to the depth because the peritoneum constricts the intestine.  Its sign, if it is from a fissure in the peritoneum, is that it initially causes great pain suddenly, and the swelling is uneven, appearing under the skin nearby.
That is because the intestine exits and goes outside of the peritoneum.  The omentum may also exit with the intestine, and this hernia is called intestinal and omental. It may be with wind, and feces may flow into the intestine and be retained there, resulting in the death of the patient.
Because it causes severe pain and gurgling, especially if constricted. Treatment of these types of illness with iron is dangerous, so one should be cautious about engaging in it. The method of treatment is to instruct the patient to return the intestine into his abdomen by hand if it is possible to return it.
Then he lies on his back in your hands and raises his legs. Then you stretch the skin next to the groin upwards and incise the entire skin of the scrotum lengthwise. Then you insert hooks into the edges of the incision to the extent needed to open the incision with them.
The incision should be large enough for the testicle to emerge. Then you dissect the membranes under the scrotal skin until the firm white peritoneum is exposed on all sides. Then insert your index finger next to the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum under the testicular skin and the second peritoneum, and loosen the adhesion behind the testicle. Then, with the right hand, you pull inwards on the scrotal skin, while with the left hand you stretch the white peritoneum upwards and lift the testicle with the peritoneum towards the incision.
You instruct the assistant to pull the testicle upwards, and you completely loosen the adhesions from behind. You explore with your finger lest there be any twisted intestine in the firm white peritoneum; if you find any, push it down into the abdomen.
Then you take a needle with thick thread, twisted from ten threads, and insert it at the end of the peritoneum under the scrotal skin next to the incision. Then you cut the ends of the thread loop so that there are four threads. Then you place some of them on top of each other in a firm manner, and tie the peritoneum, which we said is under the scrotal skin, tightly on two sides. Then you also twist the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that nothing from the vessels that nourish it can reach it. Lest a hot swelling result from that, and you also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, less than two fingers away from it. After these two ligatures, you leave a length of peritoneum under the scrotal skin equal to the width of a finger.
Lest a hot swelling result from that, and you also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, less than two fingers away from it. After these two ligatures, you leave a length of peritoneum under the scrotal skin equal to the width of a finger. And you cut all the rest in a circular manner and remove the testicle with it. Then you make an incision at the bottom of the scrotal skin from which blood and pus will flow, as we described previously. Then use wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound, and use the ligature that we described.
And one may cauterize the white peritoneum which, as we said, is cut, cautiously, to prevent bleeding, for this often occurs. And you leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.  If it is slow to fall off, then loosen it with warm water and apply something that will make it sweat until it falls off.
Then you treat the wound with the other treatments for wounds until it heals.
Chapter sixty-six.
Concerning the windy hernia.  I have not seen anyone dare to treat this windy hernia with iron. The ancients mentioned that it should be done as we described in the hernia with a dālīyah, that is, that the vessels should be gently tied below after the incision upon it.
Then you incise in the middle and treat the tumor with what opens it until the vessels fall away, and treat the wound as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter sixty-seven.
Concerning the hernia which is in the groin.
A hernia may appear in the groin, as we said, so that the place swells but nothing of the intestine descends into the scrotum. And if it descends, it is slight and returns at all times.
But if time passes, the fissure in the peritoneum increases until the intestine and epiploon descend into the scrotum.  And this happens from the extension of the peritoneum which is in the groin, as we said. That is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and the place swells and protrudes. And its treatment is by cautery, as I previously described.
And it may be treated with an iron instrument in this manner: the patient lies on his back between your hands, then you incise the swollen, protruding hernia horizontally to the extent of three fingers, then you separate the peritoneum which is under the skin.
Until the white peritoneum which follows is exposed, then you take a roll and place it on the protruding part of the peritoneum and press it into the depth of the abdomen. Then you sew the two protruding parts of the peritoneum to the edge of the roll, and you adhere one to the other with the suture.
Then you withdraw the roll, and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle or anything else, as I instructed you in the treatment of intestinal hernia, then you treat it with the treatment of wounds.
If the threads are cut, I clean them and dress the wound until it heals, for the peritoneum contracts and the hernia does not protrude further.
Cauterization is better in this place because it is closer to safety.
Chapter sixty-eight.
On the relaxation of the skin of the scrotum.
The skin of the scrotum often relaxes in some people without the flesh inside relaxing, and its appearance is unsightly.  Whoever wishes to treat it should put the patient to sleep on his back.
And he cuts all the relaxed skin over the thick skin, then brings the two lips together; and if you wish, first sew the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, and make sure of the stitching, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it heals and the stitches fall out.
Chapter sixty-nine.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
Therefore, I should not have mentioned it in this book, but I mentioned it for two reasons: one, so that it may be in the knowledge of the physician if he is asked about it, and that he may know the treatment of one who has undergone it; and the other reason is that we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Such as lambs, goats, cats, and the like of animals.  So I say that castration is of two types, either by crushing or by incision and cutting. That which is by crushing, its method is that we seat the animal in hot water until its testicles relax and soften and hang down.
Then crush them with your hand until they dissolve and are not discernible upon touch. As for castration by incision and cutting, it is necessary that you hold the animal and squeeze the skin of its testicles with your left hand, then tie the cords and make one incision on each testicle.
Until, when the testicles appear, cut them off after you have skinned them, and do not leave on them anything of the membranes except the thin membrane which is on the vessels. This type of castration is better than that which is by crushing.
Because crushing may leave something of the testicles, so the animal desires mating. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
In the treatment of the hermaphrodite.
The hermaphrodite in men is of two types. One is that which appears in the area behind the scrotum or in the skin of the scrotum between the testicles, a shape like a woman's vulva, with hair; and urine may flow from that which is in the skin of the scrotum.
As for women, there is one type, and it is above the vulva on the pubis like small male genitalia, protruding outward; one of them is like a man's penis, and the two are like testicles.  And the treatment of the three types.
The two types in men and the one type in women: it is necessary to cut away that excess flesh until its trace is removed, then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it heals. As for the second type in men, from which urine flows, which is in the scrotum's skin, there is no action in it, nor is there any benefit from its removal.
Chapter seventy-one.
On the excision of the clitoris and the protruding flesh from the vulvas of women.
The clitoris may exceed the natural amount until it becomes large and its appearance ugly, and it may become large in some women until it is spread like that of men and becomes suitable for intercourse.
It is necessary that you grasp the excess of the clitoris with your hand or with a hook and cut it, and do not exaggerate in the cutting, especially in the depth of the origin, lest bleeding occurs.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds until it heals. As for the growing flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb until it fills it.
And sometimes it protrudes outwards like a tail. Therefore, some of the ancients call it the "tail" disease.  It should be cut as you cut the clitoris, and treated until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
In the treatment of prolapse.
Prolapse is when a woman's vulva is not perforated, or the perforation is small. This can be either natural, from birth, or accidental. The accidental kind results from a pre-existing condition, and may be due to excess, newly grown flesh.
or a thin or thick membrane. It may be located in the depth of the uterus, or in its sides, or above or below it.  It prevents intercourse, conception, and childbirth, and may even prevent menstruation.  Its identification is done by examination by a midwife.
If the obstruction is apparent and close, but if it is not apparent, she should examine it with her fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is a thin membrane and close to the labia, proceed to break it.  This is done by placing something like pledgets on the labia, then placing both thumbs together.
The woman lies on her back with her legs spread. Then, forcefully stretch the labia until the thin membrane is broken and the obstruction opens. Then take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the area. The woman should have intercourse every day so that the area does not close up again.
If the membrane is thick and dense, it should be incised with a wide scalpel resembling an Asian leaf. If the obstruction is from newly grown flesh, hook it with hooks and cut it. Have styptics on hand that do not sting, such as acacia,  cistus, and frankincense, mixed with egg white.
Then use a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not close quickly. Keep it in place for a few days and use a dry linen wick. Then treat it with the usual treatment for healing until it heals.  Other fleshy growths may appear in the uterus; these should be cut in the same manner.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor.  A cancerous tumor in the uterus should never be treated with iron.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red pustules that appear in the vulva of women.
As for hemorrhoids, they are a swelling of the mouths of the veins, so that much blood constantly flows from them. If hemorrhoids are neglected, they become warts.  Their types and signs have been mentioned in the classification, and we will mention them here.
What of them is treatable and what is not treatable. I say that hemorrhoids and warts, if they are deep within the uterus and do not appear to the touch, are not treatable with iron.  Those which are in the mouth of the uterus and are palpable are those which are treated.
It is necessary that the woman enter a cool room, then she should stretch the warts with a hook or a rough cloth, and cut them from their roots, then she should apply to them, when the bleeding occurs, one of the styptics that stops the blood without cauterization.
Such as acacia,  tragacanth, and frankincense and the like, then she should raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cold water. If the bleeding continues, then seat her in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, and gallnuts, and the like. Then take barley flour, knead it with honey and vinegar.
And apply it as a compress to her back. If the bleeding stops, otherwise, apply cupping glasses to the loins and breasts without restriction. Then take wool, dip it in the juice of  hemlock or the juice of  fenugreek or the juice of  ivy branches with a decoction of gallnuts, and apply it to the area.
If the swelling subsides, then treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for the red pustule, it resembles the heads of  lice, rough in appearance. It is necessary to cut what appears of it, according to what I mentioned concerning warts, the same, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
Concerning the abscess which occurs in the uterus.
Many types of tumors may occur in the uterus, such as cancer, the hardened tumor, ulcers, the polyp, the phagedenic ulcer, fistulas, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and the hot tumor. I have mentioned all of these diseases, their types, and their signs in the classification.
It is necessary that we mention in this chapter the hot tumor which occurs in the uterus, if it is from among the tumors which collect over time, how its treatment with iron is. It is necessary to observe; if the pain of the tumor in its beginning is sharp with throbbing, inflammation, and fever,
and you see it red when you touch it, do not hasten to treat it [with cautery] and treat it with what helps in suppuration until, when these symptoms subside, then it is necessary that the woman sit on a chair with two seats and lean on her back.
and she gathers her legs towards the lower abdomen and her thighs are spread apart and her arms are under her knees and you bind them with a suitable ligature.
Then the midwife sits on the right side and uses the instrument with which the mouth of the uterus is opened, the image of which comes later.  It is necessary, if you want to use this instrument, that you measure with a probe the depth of the woman's uterus, lest what enters from the instrument be more than the depth of the uterus.
This harms the patient. If the instrument is longer than the depth, then it is necessary to place pledgets on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the depth of the uterus, and it is necessary that the screw which moves in the instrument be from the upper side.
And you hold the instrument and the assistant turns the screw until the cervix is opened.  If the abscess appears and is touched by the hand and is soft and thin, then it is necessary to incise it at its head with a wide scalpel.  When all the matter has been evacuated,
it is necessary to place in the wound a soft tent soaked in rose oil or green oil containing some astringent. The tent should be outside the incision in the cervix, and clean wool soaked in water in which alchemilla has been boiled should be placed outside on the uterus and on the pubis.
Then treat it after two days with the ointment which is suitable for that until it heals.
And the uterus and wound may be washed by injecting with honey and water, then with water in which licorice root or long rhubarb has been boiled, then return to the ointment. If the abscess is hidden inside the uterus,
then it is necessary to abstain from treating it with iron, but it should be treated as we mentioned in the section.
Chapter seventy-five.
In teaching midwives how to treat living fetuses if they emerge in an unnatural way.
The midwife should first know the form of a natural birth.  Its signs are: if you see the woman straining downwards and yearning to breathe the air, and what she is experiencing from labor becomes easier for her.
And the emergence of the fetus is hastened. Know that this birth is in the natural way, and its emergence is head-first with the placenta either with it or hanging from its navel. If you see these signs, then you should press her belly so that the fetus comes out quickly.
For if it descends head-first, the placenta descends with it and cleanses well from those impurities. Anything other than this birth is unnatural and blameworthy, for the fetus may emerge feet-first, or its hands and feet may emerge before its head.
Or one hand or one foot, or its head and hand may emerge, or it may emerge folded, and perhaps it turns onto its back, and similar blameworthy forms.  Therefore, the midwife should be skillful and gentle with all these things and beware of mistakes and errors.
And I will explain each form and how to deal with it, so that you may learn and understand it. If the fetus emerges head-first, the natural emergence, and this is difficult for the woman and labor is hard for her, and you see that her strength has weakened, then seat her on a chair and instruct women to hold her steady.
And compress her womb with cooked fenugreek and moist ointments, then the midwife inserts a small scalpel between her fingers and cuts the placenta with it, or cuts it with her fingernail until the moisture within flows out, and she presses the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then she should inject the woman with fenugreek saliva mixed with shirāj ointment, then after the injection, instruct her to strain and make her sneeze with a sternutatory, holding her mouth and nose, for the fetus will emerge immediately. If the hands of the fetus emerge first, then they should be gently returned little by little.
If they do not come to enter, place the woman on a raised platform and raise her legs upwards, then shake the platform on the ground while the woman holds on so that she does not fall when it is shaken. If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them moderately, for then he will emerge.  The emergence of the fetus with its feet: If the fetus emerges with its feet, then they should be pushed upwards, then the fetus is turned little by little until it is straightened, then one of its feet is taken and extended little by little.
If they emerge to the thighs, instruct her to strain and make her sneeze with a sternutatory, for then it will emerge. If it does not emerge as we have described, otherwise, return the fetus little by little until it is brought to its natural form, for then it will emerge easily. If it is prevented by all that we have described.
Take some saliva of *khatmi*, some saliva of fenugreek, shirāj ointment, and dissolved gum, and pound all well together in a mortar, then apply it to the vulva of the woman and the lower part of her abdomen, then seat her in lukewarm water until it reaches the shrasīf.
