If you see that her lower parts have become soft, make a pessary for her from myrrh and she should endure it; and if the pessary remains in place for an hour, then seat her on a chair, then make her sneeze, and close her mouth and nose, and gently press down on her lower abdomen; then the fetus will emerge immediately. The emergence of the fetus on its knees and hands.
If it emerges in this manner, gradually insert your hands little by little; then the woman lies on her back and lowers her hips, while lying on a bed, and clasps her hands, then press gently on her abdomen little by little. Then remove the pillows from under her legs so that her lower parts are raised. If the fetus does not emerge, take both her legs and move them vigorously, then press gently above the symphysis pubis little by little until the fetus ascends, then the midwife inserts her hand and adjusts the fetus little by little.
And order the woman to strain until the fetus emerges. The emergence of the fetus transversely, one of its hands hanging down.  Gently replace its hand; and if you are unable to replace it, make the woman stand and walk; and if she is unable to walk, let her lie down.
on her bed and shake her legs vigorously; then use the poultice which I described from the ingredients. If labor pains seize her, seat her on a chair, then work to replace the hand and adjust the fetus to its natural position, and make the woman sneeze and order her to strain until it emerges.
The emergence of the fetus on its back, extending its hands, and its face towards its mother's back. The midwife should grasp the hand of the fetus, then turn it little by little and gently adjust it; and when it is adjusted, apply to the vulva what we described of ointments and poultices.
Then tell her to move little by little; and if she does that, seat her on a chair and tell her to lean to the right side; then tell her to strain and make her sneeze with a sternutatory; then it will emerge. The emergence of the fetus upright on its side.
If it descends to the mouth of the womb in this manner with the placenta, tear the placenta with your fingernail until it is emptied of moisture; and if it adheres to the face and neck of the fetus, cut it from the navel lest it be choked and die; then push the fetus inwards until it is as it should be.
And tell the woman to strain repeatedly, then it will emerge easily. The emergence of twins or many fetuses. Know that twins are frequently born, and three or four may be born and live, although this is rare; but as for five, it is something outside of nature, and they will not live at all.
And they are surrounded, even if numerous, by one placenta, and they are separated by a membrane-like barrier between them, attached to the navel of each one of them.  And they may emerge, like a single fetus, in a natural and easy manner, and they may emerge in a blameworthy manner as we described.
If some of them are difficult at birth and come to what we have mentioned of unnatural forms, then treat them as we have described. And let the midwife be gentle and do what she does with gentleness and slowness, and contrive for every form with whatever means she can devise that leads to safety.
Mention of what is conceived in the uterus of fetuses that are aborted. One, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten may be conceived in the uterus.  It has been confirmed to me that a woman aborted seven, and another fifteen, all of them conceived.
By the estimation of the All-Knowing, All-Mighty One. All of this should be in the knowledge of the midwife, for perhaps such a thing will befall her someday, and she may deal with it as is appropriate.
Chapter seventy-six.
On the removal of a dead fetus.
If you treat the dead fetus with what I mentioned in the section on treatment, and it does not come out, and you are compelled to work with your hand, then you should look: if the woman is healthy and strong, and there is nothing wrong with her, nothing from among the symptoms which one fears might cause her harm, then the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
And her head should be tilted downwards and her legs raised, and a servant should hold her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed, lest her body be pulled when the fetus is extracted.
Then moisten her vulva with moist ointments, along with mallow juice, fenugreek, and flaxseed. Then the midwife should anoint her hand with these ointments and juices, then gently insert it into the womb, then search with it for a place to hook the fetus, and the appropriate places for that.
See, if the fetus is positioned to descend head first, then hook it in its eyes, or in the nape of its neck, or in its mouth, or in its palate, or under its chin, or its collarbone, or in places near the ribs, or under the clavicles.
But if it descends feet first, then [hook it] in the pubis and the middle ribs.  And you should hold the hook with the right hand, and place its bend between the fingers of the left hand, and insert the hand gently and hook one of those places we mentioned.
Until you reach something empty, then insert another hook opposite it, or a third if needed, so that the traction is even and does not tilt to one side. Then pull with a steady pull, and the pull should not be straight only, but moved to each side.
So that its exit is facilitated, as is done with a molar when it is extracted.  In the meantime, the pull should be relaxed. If it gets stuck on one side, then the midwife should anoint some fingers with ointment and insert them from that side to rotate what is stuck. If part of the fetus emerges, then move the hooks to other places.
She [the midwife] raises it a little, doing this until the whole fetus comes out. If a hand comes out before anything else and cannot be returned because of its compression, then you should wrap a cloth around it and pull it until, when it has all come out, you cut it.
Either at the wrist joint or at the elbow.  And thus you should do with the other hand and with the feet. If the head of the fetus is large and experiences difficulty in exiting, or if there is collected water in its head, then you should insert a hooked scalpel between the fingers.
And split the head with it so that the water comes out, or puncture it with the instrument called the perforator, the image of which comes with the images of the other instruments in the chapter which follows this one.
Similarly, if the fetus is naturally large-headed, then you should split the skull or perforate it with the perforator as we said, then remove the bones with the forceps. If the head emerges and is compressed at the clavicle, then let it be split so that the moisture in the chest is released.
For the chest then becomes constricted. If the chest does not become constricted and it is not possible for it to come out, then cut as much as you can. If the lower abdomen is swollen or if there is a swelling in it, then you should puncture it so that the moisture flows from it.
If the fetus is in a breech presentation, then pulling it is easy and straightening it towards the mouth of the uterus is simple. If it is compressed at the abdomen or chest, then you should pull it with a cloth that you have wrapped around your hand, and split the abdomen or chest until what is in them flows out.
If all the members are extracted and the head is retracted and retained, let the midwife insert her left hand. If the mouth of the uterus is open, then let her insert her hand into the neck of the uterus and search for the head with it, and pull it with her fingers to the mouth of the uterus, then insert a hook or two hooks into it and pull it with them.
If the mouth of the uterus has become constricted due to a hot swelling affecting it, then you should not be forceful with it, but rather you should then use the pouring of moist, fatty things and use compresses, and sit in waters that relax and moisten.
But if the fetus is on its side, then if it is possible to straighten it, let him use what we mentioned for the living fetus. If that is not possible, then let him cut pieces and extract it.  And it should not be that anything of the placenta remains inside at all.  When you have finished your treatment, then use what we mentioned of the treatment for the hot swellings that appear in the uteruses.
If bleeding occurs, then let her descend into astringent waters and use all other treatments. And I have witnessed a woman who had conceived, and the fetus died in her womb, then she conceived again upon it.
Then the other fetus also died.  After a long time, a swelling appeared in her womb and it swelled until it opened and began to discharge pus. I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not close, so I placed on it some strong, drawing ointments.
Then a bone emerged from the place, then days passed and another bone emerged. I was amazed by this, since the abdomen is a place where there is no bone. I considered that it was from the bones of the dead fetus, so I examined the wound and extracted from it many bones from the bones of the head of the fetus.
I continued doing that until I extracted many bones from her, and the woman was in the best of conditions. And she lived like that for a time, discharging a little pus from the place. And I have only brought here this rare thing.
Because in it there is knowledge and help for what the skillful physician attempts with his hand in treatment.
Chapter seventy-seven.
In it are images of the instruments needed for extracting the fetus.
Image of a spiral instrument to open the mouth of the womb.
This is the binder with which books are bound; it has two spirals at the ends of the two pieces of wood, except that this spiral should be finer than the binder, and should be either of ebony or of boxwood, and the width of each piece of wood should be the thickness of two fingers.
And their width about a finger, and their length a span and a half, and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two guide-pieces of the same material, firmly attached to them; their length is half a span or a little more, and their width about two fingers or a little more, and these two guide-pieces...
are those which enter the mouth of the womb to open it when you turn the spirals. Image of another instrument for the same purpose, finer and lighter.
It is made of ebony or boxwood in the shape of hooks, except that it has two guide-pieces at its end, as you see; the length of each guide-piece is about a span, and their width is two fingers.  If you wish to open the womb with it,
then seat the woman on a bed with her legs hanging down, spread apart between her thighs; then insert these two guide-pieces, closely together, into the mouth of the womb, while you hold the end of the instrument below between her thighs; then open your hand with the instrument as you do with hooks, to whatever extent you desire to open the womb. until the midwife does what she wants. Image of another spiral mentioned by the ancients.
Image of the instrument which pushes the fetus.
Image of the forceps with which the head of the fetus is grasped.
It resembles scissors, it has teeth at the end as you see, and it may be made rectangular like hooks, in this image as you see, it has teeth like the teeth of a saw, to cut and tear with it.
Image of a pusher also.
Image of a hook.
This hook should have a little thickness so that it does not break when pulling the fetus with it. Image of a hook with two prongs.
Image of two wide scalpels for cutting the fetus.
All these instruments, the more numerous their types and the better prepared they are by the craftsman, the quicker his work and the higher his standing among people.  Therefore, do not despise any instrument that you have prepared, for there will certainly be need for it.
Chapter seventy-eight.
In the removal of the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, you should instruct the patient to hold herself, then make her sneeze with a sternutatory, and press your hand on her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out this way, well and good; otherwise, take a measure.
And pierce a hole in the lid, and place in it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, such as fumitory, rue, dill, chamomile, wormwood, marshmallow, and centaury—all these drugs or some of them. Immerse them in water and place them on the fire, then place a silver tube over the hole in the lid of the pot, and the other end in the mouth of the womb.
And hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the womb, then make her sneeze as we said.  Then the placenta will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in goose grease or mallow sap.
Then she inserts it into the vagina and searches for the placenta. If she finds it, she grasps it and pulls it out little by little. If it is attached deep in the womb, insert the hand as we have described, until you find the placenta, then pull it out little by little, carefully.
Lest the womb fall out with a strong pull, but rather it should be moved gently to the sides, right and left, then the amount of pulling is increased, then it will yield and be freed from adhesion.  If the mouth of the womb is constricted, we have described treatment with sneezing and the treatment with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out by all that we have described, avoid violence in pulling it again.  But it should be bound where it came out to the mouth of the womb to the woman's thigh, then inject it with the four-ingredient ointment, for it will cause it to rot after a few days.
And it will dissolve and come out, except that if it rots, a foul smell will rise from it to the stomach and head, and that will harm the patient.  Therefore, suitable fumigation should be used.
Therefore, some of the ancients tried fumigation with rue and dried figs.  The image of the instrument with which the woman is fumigated in the case of retained menses, placenta, and the like:
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or made of copper. The narrow end is placed in the vagina, and the wide end over the fire, with the incense placed on the coals.  It is held until that incense is gone, and then more is added.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
On the treatment of the non-perforated fistula.
Many boys are born with their anus imperforate; a thin membrane obstructs it.  It is necessary for the midwife to perforate this membrane with her finger; otherwise, she should puncture it with a sharp scalpel, taking care not to injure the muscle.
Then she should apply wool soaked in syrup and oil, and then treat it with ointments until it heals. If she fears it will close up, she should place a lead tube in the opening for many days, removing it whenever the child wishes to defecate.
The anus may also become obstructed by the healing of a wound or a tumor.  This adhesion should be incised, and then treated as previously described, with the lead tube.
Chapter Eighty.
On the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower parts.
Fistulas which occur in the lower parts are a complication and thickening which occurs near the anus, externally or internally, from one side or another. The fistula may be single or multiple.
If this complication becomes chronic, it opens and discharges a thin, white, watery fluid or pus. Some of these fistulas may communicate with the rectum and the intestine, while others do not.  Those which communicate may be recognized by the passage of feces and flatus when the patient defecates.
Worms may also emerge from them.  There may be fistulas which, if they are internal, communicate with the bladder and the urethra.  Some may communicate with the hip joint and the sacrum. A fistula communicating with the rectum is also distinguishable from one which does not.
Insert your index finger into the rectum and introduce a thin probe into the fistula, made of copper or iron, if the fistula is not tortuous. If it is tortuous, introduce a thin lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or hair with your finger. If you feel neither and nothing emerges from the opening—neither feces, nor flatus, nor worms, as we have said—know that it is not communicating, and proceed with treatment.
If the fistula communicates with the bladder or urethra, the sign is the passage of urine from it, and the failure of the site to heal with medication. If it communicates with the hip joint or sacrum, the sign is that the probe reaches there.
If it is not tortuous to the bone, and pain is present in that area, and pus constantly discharges from it, and treatment is ineffective and it does not heal with ointment—all these communicating fistulas are incurable.
Treatment is a vain effort for those ignorant physicians who attempt it.  As for those that are not communicating and not chronic, healing is hoped for with cautery, as I shall describe and as I have proven. The patient should lie on his back between your hands.
Raise his legs and incline his thighs towards his abdomen.  Then insert a copper or lead probe if the fistula is tortuous, until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it towards the anus, you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel with your finger that the probe has passed openly without feeling any septum or flesh between your finger and it, then know for certain that it is communicating; therefore, do not exert yourself, for there is no cure, as we have said.
It has been said that it heals in rare cases. Among the treatments from which benefit is hoped for is to heat a thin cautery iron according to the width of the fistula, as previously mentioned, and to insert it hot into the fistula until it reaches towards the anus, and then to repeat this two or three times.
Until you know that all that excess, matted flesh resembling a bird's quill has been burned. Then treat it with lint soaked in butter until the burned flesh comes out, then treat it with healing ointments, and if it heals.
Otherwise, it will never heal with any other treatment. But if you insert the probe and it does not reach your finger which is in the anus, and there is a thick partition of flesh or peritoneum between them, and you see the fistula near the surface of the skin, then incise the skin at the beginning of the fistula. And as you pass with the incision along with the probe which is in the fistula, until you reach with the incision where the end of the probe reaches, and the probe becomes free and falls out; then cleanse that matted flesh resembling a bird's quill, and leave nothing of it.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If the bleeding overwhelms you and prevents you from cutting that flesh, the best and quickest remedy is cauterization with fire or a caustic medicine, because cauterization with fire combines two good things: burning that excess flesh.
And cutting the blood and drying the moisture. Then treat it with lint soaked in butter or sulfur powder mixed with oil until the area suppurates and that burned flesh comes out in the pus. Then treat the wound with healing ointments that grow firm flesh.
These are the medicines which have astringent and drying properties; for when it is conjoined, then it is healed, and there is no fear of recurrence. If the fistula has reached the depth of the anus and is distant from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the anus and explore with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it a partition of peritoneum or flesh, and it is near to penetration, then the action in it is only by way of hope and expectation; that is, to use in it one of three methods: either to cauterize it as we have said.
Or to incise it until you reach its bottom, so that you can easily insert the lint and treat it from near. Do not overdo the incision, lest you cut the muscle surrounding the anus and cause the patient to involuntarily pass stool. Then treat it as we have mentioned; perhaps it will heal as we have said.
Or to penetrate that partition and perforate it after you have incised it near to the anus, as we have said, either with a probe or with another sharp-pointed instrument. Then cleanse all the adherent flesh in the incision as far as you can, thoroughly.
Then close the entire wound, together with the incision and the upper opening, until it is sealed, and the fistula remains open inside the anus; this will be easier for the patient.  The fistula may be perforated in this manner.
And if you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is on one side of the anus, towards the surface of the body with the skin and the circumference of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated tip, like a cobbler's needle, in this manner:
And insert into it a twisted thread of five threads or so. Then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its bottom, if it is perforated in the margin of the anus from inside, nearby.
Then bring the thread out through that opening by inserting your finger into the anus and bringing out the end of the thread. Bring both ends together and tighten them, and leave it for a day or two.  Whenever the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the ends of the thread is severed and falls away. Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the opening of the fistula is not perforated, perforate it however you can, except that it should not be too deep, for you should not do that because of the muscle, lest you cut it. Then treat it as we have mentioned until it heals.
The image of the hooked scalpel with which fistulas are incised: its curve is very sharp, and the other side is not sharp, lest you cut what is not needed, as you see.
Chapter eighty-one.
Concerning the opening of hemorrhoids from which blood flows, their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either they are inside the rectum, resembling red bladders or like grapes, and there are small and large ones, and blood constantly flows from them.
And they may be single or numerous, and they may be outside the rectum and at its edges, except that those which are outside the rectum are in most cases less moist, with a yellowish water or a little blood constantly flowing from them, and they are the color of the body.
The treatment of those that are inside the rectum is that you should order the patient to strain and bear down until the rectum comes out and the warts appear to you.  Then you quickly seize them with hooks or grasp them with your fingernail and cut them at their base.  If the hooks do not hold because of their moistness and laxity,
then rub them with a rough cloth or a piece of a rough sponge, or pull them with your fingers, then cut them. Then, after cutting, sprinkle on them some sharp powders to act as a cautery, or cauterize them as described in the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. If the rectum does not come out, inject the patient with an enema containing a little stimulant to wash what is in the rectum and make it come out quickly when the patient strains. As for the warts that are outside the rectum, their treatment is easy and simple.
That is, you take it with your fingernail or seize it with a hook and cut it, then treat it as we have mentioned until it heals. And whoever dislikes cutting with iron should use a thread in this way: take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the wart upwards and pass the needle through its base from the other side, and twist the ends of the thread below the needle while it is across, and pull the wart tightly, then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this with all the warts and leave one unpierced.
so that the excess blood flows from it, then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or kyrios oil on the rectum and tell the patient to be still, then leave him until they fall off. When the warts fall off, treat them with ointments and the rest of the treatment until they heal.
As for the treatment of fissures, they often occur from dryness of the stool and retention of the temperament. If it becomes chronic and medicine does not work, then it should be scraped with the blade of a scalpel or with your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper crust that prevents it from healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more forcefully than before until it bleeds well and is excoriated, then treat it, for it will heal.
Chapter eighty-two.
On the treatment of ingrown and non-ingrown nails, dry warts, and the ant.
A corn is a round nodule, the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail. It occurs on the whole body, especially on the soles of the feet and the toes, and causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to incise around the corn, grasp it with a hook or a forceps, and remove it from its root, then treat it; and if you wish, cauterize it as explained previously in its chapter. As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, matted, round protrusion on the surface of the body.
They often appear on the hands of children.  Its treatment is to stretch the wart and cut it, or pierce it with a silk thread or a hair until it falls off; and if you wish, cauterize it with fire or a caustic medicine, as previously explained.
Ingrown and non-ingrown warts may be removed with the instrument which I have mentioned in the treatment of the ant. As for the ant, it is also a small, matted, thick protrusion on the surface of the body, going very deep; and if cold affects it, a sensation similar to the sting of ants is felt in it.
It occurs on the whole body, but mostly on the hands.  Its treatment is to take a tube made of a strong goose feather or eagle feather, and place this tube on the ant until the circumference of the tube surrounds it on all sides; then rotate your hand with the tube until you incise around the ant.
Then place it deep in the flesh, and the ant will be removed from its root. And if you wish, you may do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner:
The top of the tube, towards the head, should be solid and wired so that the fingers may easily grasp and twist it. And if you wish, cauterize the ant as previously explained in the cauterization of warts in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
On the shapes of the instruments used for injections in rectal ailments, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, china, or cast or hammered copper.  These instruments may be made in small and large sizes, according to the users. Those used in the treatment of small children, whose rectums are narrow or sore, should be very gentle. Image of a large syringe:
Its upper funnel, to which the bladder is tied, is wide in this manner; it has a partition where the bladder is tied, as you see.  Its lower end, which enters the rectum, is solid, very smooth, and thin; on one side are two holes, and on the other, one hole.
The size of the hole should be the thickness of, or a little thicker than, a probe. The bladder, in which the medicines are placed, should be made from an animal's bladder or sheepskin, fashioned like a small pouch, its diameter being a span.
It is pierced with many holes in a circular pattern, the distance between each hole being the thickness of a finger. Then, a strong thread of twisted yarn of ten strands or so is passed through these holes and gathers the bladder like a pouch. Then, when the medicine is placed inside, it is tightly bound at the head of the syringe between two stones, and then injected. This is an image of the circle of the bladder and the hole:
The method of injection is to fill the pouch or bladder with warmed medicine, to a degree the patient can tolerate; be careful that it is neither too hot nor too cold.  Then, the patient lies on his back and raises his legs.
A cloth or sheet is placed beneath him. Then, the tip of the syringe is lubricated with oil, egg white, fenugreek saliva, or the like, and gently inserted into the rectum. Then, an assistant takes the bladder in both hands and squeezes it strongly until all the contents of the syringe are emptied into the bowel.
Then the syringe is removed, and the patient raises his legs against the wall. If the medicine stimulates evacuation, let him restrain himself as much as possible; if he sleeps the whole night with it, it will be more beneficial. If he is injected for diarrhea and evacuation is hastened, then repeat the injection until he is cured.
If there are painful hemorrhoids or tumors in the rectum, the syringe should be very gentle and smooth, smoother than those used for injecting children. This is an image of a gentle syringe with leather stretched over it:
Chapter Eighty-Four.
In the treatment of wounds.
I have mentioned in the classification the treatment of what resembles cauterizations, and I will now discuss their treatment by hand and by medication completely. I say that wounds differ according to the thing that causes the wound and according to the location where the wound occurs.
The things that cause wounds are many, such as the blow of a stone, or a cut from a sword or knife, or a stab from a spear or stick or arrow, and so on from many things.
As for wounds according to their location on the body, there is the wound that occurs on the head, or neck, or chest, or abdomen, or liver, and similar organs.
And I am describing the treatment of some wounds to make them a measure and rule for all wounds, and I begin with simple head wounds especially because the compound ones will be mentioned at the beginning of the third chapter of this book.
So I say that when a simple wound occurs in the head and there is no fracture of the bone, I look; and if it is from a blow of a stone or the like, and only the skin has been lacerated, and the wound is large, and I fear for the patient the occurrence of hot inflammation, then I hasten and open the cephalic vein in that place. And do not delay that, and let blood be drawn from him according to his strength if nothing prevents bloodletting, especially if the blood coming from the wound is little; and let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the wounded area.
And warn the patient against fullness, and strong drinks and foods; and if something prevents him from being bled, then let him reduce his food and not approach meats or drinks. And apply to the wound, if hot inflammation occurs in it, cotton soaked in rose oil alone or with a drink that has an astringent quality.
And if you are safe from hot inflammation, then apply to the wound, if it is fresh and the air has not altered it, the powder which I will describe to you shortly; but if the air has altered it, then apply some ointments to it.
Until pus forms, then treat it until it heals. And if the wound is large, and is from a sword cut or the like, and its edges do not come together by themselves, then bring them together by stitching, as I describe in the stitching of abdominal wounds.
If the skin exposes the bone and hangs, and its attachment is only by a small piece, then cut it off, then treat the wound with medicines that produce firm flesh to replace the skin, and order the patient to be nourished by foods that have firmness, like heads and brains and the like.
If decay occurs in the skin and it does not adhere to the bone, then cut away all the decayed part and throw it away, then treat it.  If in these wounds there is an artery or vein bleeding blood and the bleeding does not stop with medication, then search for the artery.
If you find it and it is not severed, then sever it with the scalpel and tie it. And if necessity demands, if what we have mentioned is not effective, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and superficial, then their treatment is easy and simple; it suffices to treat them by sprinkling this powder on them while they are bleeding.
before the air alters them. The preparation of the powder is that one part of frankincense, two parts of gum arabic, and three parts of slaked or unslaked lime are taken; all are crushed, sifted, and the wound is filled with it and firmly bandaged until it adheres well.
It will clot with the blood, then bandage it on top with a bandage and leave it; do not untie it as long as it does not swell, for the medicine will not separate from it until it heals.  The lime alone may do this if you do not have frankincense and gum arabic.
This action may also be done with cobwebs in many large wounds if its application and bandaging are skillfully done, and the wounds are fresh with blood, before the air alters them, but if the air has altered them somewhat, especially if it is in summer.
then there is no benefit in applying this powder; treat it with what we have mentioned: apply some ripening ointments or apply a poultice of barley flour made with water and honey until pus is produced, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
But if a fracture of the bone occurs with the wound and it is slight, draw it with a splint. I have described the treatment of head fractures in what follows, in the surgery of the neck. If the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment from a head wound if it is simple.
But if a nerve or artery of the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treating it with things that constrict strongly, like verdigris and vitriol and the like, because they harm the nerve and cause it to spasm.
And nothing cold should be placed on the wound at all, because the essence of the nerve is cold and its connection is to the brain, which is the noblest of organs.  What you treat it with should be soft medicines, like burnt alum washed with fresh water several times, moistened with oil or rose ointment and tutty.
If it is also washed with fresh water and other mineral stones in this manner, and moist ointments; but if the wound is large, use stitching or bind its edges with bandages, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has depth and a cavity forms in its lower part where pus collects, lance it in its lowest place. If an artery is severed in the wound and you see its blood flowing, ligate it or tie it off, or cauterize it if necessity demands it.
If the wound has severed some or all of the cartilages of the larynx, and the vessels remain intact, join the edges of the skin with sutures over the windpipe, but do not touch the windpipe itself. Rather, straighten it and return it to its natural shape, then bind it tightly and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, examine it; if there are bone splinters, extract all of them or as many as you can.  Those you cannot remove, leave them until the wound decays, heals, and their removal becomes easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle on it the powder we described. If a bone in the wound decays and becomes fistulous, treat it as described in its chapter, in the surgery of the chest and between the shoulders.
If it is a wound from a spear or knife and you see that it has depth, look: if air emerges from it when the patient breathes, know that it is a mortal wound. If it does not have depth and is fresh with its blood, do not initially apply powder to it, nor bind it tightly, lest the blood be trapped in its depths.
It will then recede to the heart and kill the patient. But put on it an attractive ointment and dress it twice a day. If you do not have ointment, put a used cotton ball in the mouth of the wound to absorb the moisture that comes out of it, and have the patient sleep on the wound.
so that whatever gathers there may flow out.  If three days or more have passed since the injury and the patient has not experienced convulsions, bad palpitations, or shortness of breath, and you see his other conditions are good, then know that the wound is safe, and treat it with cauterization and other treatments until it heals.
If it is difficult to see it and the pus flows continuously, know that it has become fistulous, and treat it according to its chapter.  The patient should lie on the wound so that whatever gathers there may flow out. If it is a wound of the chest or back, treat it with the stitching described earlier.
If it is large, cauterize it; if small, use powder. If it has affected the bone and broken off fragments, explore the wound and promptly remove those fragments if they are free. If they are not free, leave them until...
the wound becomes putrid, for then it will be easier to remove them. As for other wounds occurring in other parts of the body, their treatment is governed by what we have mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-five.
On abdominal wounds, the protrusion of the intestines, and their stitching.
The wound which occurs in the abdomen may be large, or it may be small or medium.  A large wound may allow the protrusion of one or several intestines, and returning them and stitching them is more difficult and strenuous.
A small wound may also be difficult in another way, requiring more strenuous effort to return the intestine.  If the intestine is not immediately returned to its place, it swells and thickens, making it difficult to replace.  For this reason, a medium-sized wound is preferable, because returning the intestine is not as difficult as with the other two types.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen is of four types: two are general, suitable for suturing the abdomen and for suturing other wounds of the body; the other two are specific, used for all wounds, but more especially for suturing the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and clarification. I say that if the wound is small and some of the intestine has protruded and its return is difficult, this is for one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the wound, as we said, or because the intestine has been swollen by the cold air.
If this is so, then it should be warmed by dipping a sponge or a damp cloth into lukewarm water alone, or water in which dill, rue, and spikenard have been boiled, and applying it to the intestine until the swelling resolves.
A styptic drink may also do this, and it is better than water alone in resolving the swelling.  If you see that the swelling has resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which alkanet or madder has been boiled, for it will facilitate its entry with the least effort.
If its return is still difficult after this treatment, then make a small incision in the wound with the instrument used to incise fistulas. The first treatment, if successful, is better than incision. Incision is only resorted to out of necessity and after other means have failed.  This is the image of the instrument:
One side is curved and limited, the other side is unlimited. The thin end is not as sharp as a scalpel, but is slightly blunt. It is an instrument resembling a small spatula, as you see. If the wound is widened and the intestine has entered, then it should be returned to its natural shape.
And its proper place, if you can manage it, is best. But if the wound is wide and is in the lower abdomen, then the patient should lie on his back with his legs higher than his head; if it is in the upper abdomen, then his head and chest should be higher than his lower body.
Similarly, if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always make the side with the injury higher than the other side. This should also be done in major and moderate surgeries.
As for minor wounds, position the patient as you are able. Then have a gentle assistant ready to hold the entire wound with both hands and bring the edges together, then reveal it to the person doing the stitching, piece by piece.
This is the description of a single common stitch: Take a needle, or several needles, according to the size of the wound. Then leave a space at the edge of the wound equal to the thickness of the little finger, and insert one needle without thread into the edges of the skin. along with the edges of the peritoneum under the skin from the inside, until you pass it through from that side.  You will have gathered the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, making four layers. Then, tie a doubled thread around the needle several times on both sides until the edges of the wound are tightly joined.
Then leave a space equal to the thickness of a finger, and insert another needle, then fasten it with the thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with as many needles as you need until you complete the entire circumference of the wound.  Let the needles be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin.
For very thin needles quickly cut the flesh, and thick needles are difficult to insert into the skin. Therefore, they should be of medium thickness.  And the needle should not be inserted into the edge of the skin too close to the wound.
lest the flesh quickly tear and the wound open before it heals.  Nor should you be too far apart with the stitches, lest the wound fail to unite. Then cut off the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the patient when he sleeps, and make for him pads of linen cloth on each side to hold the ends of the needles.
And leave them until you know that the wound has healed. This type of stitching with needles is most suitable for small wounds, because it may suffice to stitch them with one or two needles, or thereabouts.
As for the method of stitching for a common wound, it is that you bring together the four edges—that is, the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum—at once with a needle threaded with a twisted thread of moderate thickness, then when the needle has passed through these four edges, return the needle to the same side from which you began.
So that the thread lies interwoven from the top of the wound, so that the stitching is like the stitching of a purse with which goods are tied, and make the distance between each stitch the width of the little finger.  This stitching is known to all people.
With this stitching, I stitched a wound that appeared on a man in his abdomen. He had been wounded by a knife, and the wound was more than a span long.  About two spans of the middle intestine had protruded, and the wound was in the middle of the abdomen.
Then I replaced it after his intestines had remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours. The wound healed in about fifteen days, and I treated him until he was healed. He lived many years afterward, managing all his affairs, while the physicians had judged that he would not recover at all.
And what is remarkable is that I did not treat him with ointment, because I was in a place where there were no medicines.  I used to place worn cotton on the wound twice a day and treat it by washing it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the two types of special stitching, I mention it according to Galen's words: you begin the stitching from the skin and insert the needle from outside to inside.  When the needle has passed through the skin and the muscle going straight along the whole length of the abdomen.
I left the edge of the peritoneum on the side into which I inserted the needle, and I passed the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, into the other edge of the intestines. When I had passed it through, I passed it again through this same edge of the intestines from outside to inside.
And leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side, and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, and as you pass it through, pass it through the peritoneum to the edge of the intestine on its side until it passes completely through it.
Then begin again from this same side and sew it together with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side, and bring the needle out of the skin near it. Then return it into that skin and sew the edge of the peritoneum on the other side together with this edge of the intestine.
And bring it out of the skin on its side, and do that again and again, one after another, until you sew up the whole wound in that way.  In short, the description of this stitch is that you sew it like the stitching of furriers for fur.
That you sew the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin and leave it once from the other side until you finish. As for the second type of special stitching which Galen also mentioned, this is his exact wording.
Which is that you sew it in the manner that some physicians sew, by joining each part to its counterpart similar to it by nature, thus joining the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and the edge of the intestine to its other edge, which is as I describe to you.
It is necessary that you insert the needle into the edge of the intestine nearest to you from the outside, and pass it to the inside in it alone, and leave the edges of the peritoneum. Then return the needle and pass it from the outside to the inside in both edges of the peritoneum, then return it again.
And pass it from the inside to the outside in the edge of the other intestine which is on the opposite side. This type of stitch is better than the easy common stitch, which is the stitch previously mentioned. This is Galen's exact wording. Some have said that the suturing of the abdomen may be done with two needles. That is, you insert a single thread into them, and begin by inserting the needle from your side and passing it to the other side, and insert the other needle from the opposite side, then pass it from your side according to the stitching of the mattress stitch.
Know that if the wound is in the middle of the abdomen, then its suturing is more difficult than in other parts of the abdomen. As for the treatment of the wound, its treatment is governed by the rules of treatment of other wounds.  That is, if you find the wound fresh with its blood before the air alters it, and you have returned the intestine and stitched and tightened it, then apply to it the medicated lint.
If the wound has been altered by the air, then apply to it some of the ointments that you have prepared until it suppurates and the stitches fall out and the peritoneum and intestine adhere, then treat it like you treat other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medicines, then apply to it, from when it begins to suppurate, old cotton, and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it heals. For you do not need further treatment in most cases if the wounds are simple. If you fear that the wound might involve the principal organs in pain,
then it is necessary that you dip soft wool into moderately warm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and the armpit. If he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestines—and this often occurs—then inject him with a lukewarm astringent black drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and become a penetrating wound into its cavity. And know that what is thick of the intestines is easier to heal, and what is thin of them is more difficult to heal.
As for the intestine known as the jejunum, it does not readily heal from a wound at all, because of the multitude and size of its veins and the thinness of its substance and its proximity to the nature of the nerve. But if what protruded from the wound was the omentum, and you caught it fresh, then return it in the same way as you return the intestine.
If a period of time has passed and it has turned green or black, then it is necessary to tie it with a thread above the part that has blackened, lest bleeding occur, for the omentum contains veins and arteries, then cut below that ligature.
And leave the ends of the thread hanging from below the wound, outside of it, so that it is easy for you to remove and extract it when the omentum falls off and the wound suppurates.  Concerning wounds that occur in the intestine: if a small perforation occurs in the intestine,
it may heal in some people, because I saw a man who had been wounded in his abdomen by a spear thrust, and the wound was to the right of the stomach. The wound became chronic and formed a fistula from which feces and flatus emerged; and I treated it, although I did not hope for its healing. I continued to treat him until it healed and the place closed. When I saw that the place had closed, I feared for the patient that some bad accident might occur in his abdomen, but no bad accident occurred to him at all, and he remained in the best of health. He eats and drinks properly, engages in sexual intercourse, enters the bath, and exercises in his service. Some experienced people have said that if a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be sutured in this manner.
Large-headed ants are taken, then the edges of the wound are brought together, and one of these ants, with its mouth open, is placed on the edges of the wound. When it bites and tightens its mouth, its head is cut off; then it sticks and does not come undone. Then another ant is placed near the first.
This is done repeatedly with several ants, according to the size of the wound. Then it is bandaged and stitched, and those heads remain stuck in the intestine until the intestine heals and recovers. No harm at all occurs to the patient.  The intestine can also be stitched with fine thread.
Which is drawn from the intestine of the animal adhering to it after it has been passed through a needle. The end of this thread is taken from the intestine, then it is gently drawn out and a thin, twisted linen thread is tied to its end; then this thread is passed through the needle. And in it is the intestine's thread, with which the intestine is stitched, then it is returned to the abdomen. This type of suturing with ants and intestine is only by way of hope and expectation.
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, then there is no remedy and no healing at all.
Chapter Eighty-six.
In the treatment of catarrh and fistula.
Know that every wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and protracted and becomes an ulcer and does not heal, and constantly discharges pus that does not stop, is generally called, in whatever member it may be, a fistula, and we call it a catarrh, and a fistula.
It is, in reality, a hardened, matted, white mass, without pain, having a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some have called it a feather; and it is sometimes moist, constantly discharging pus, and sometimes the moisture may cease; and this moisture may be abundant or scanty, thick or thin.
And I have already stated in the classification, when mentioning wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal and in which flesh does not grow is due to one of nine causes; one of which is either paucity of blood in the body, or its poor quality, or because there is within it and on its edges hard flesh which prevents the growth of good flesh. Or because it is very painful and dirty; or because the ulcer itself is foul and the matter it discharges is of poor quality; or because the medicine is unsuitable for its treatment; or because of a corruption occurring in the region of the nature of an epidemic; or because of a peculiar quality in the region.
Or because it is very painful and dirty; or because the ulcer itself is foul and the matter it discharges is of poor quality; or because the medicine is unsuitable for its treatment; or because of a corruption occurring in the region of the nature of an epidemic; or because of a peculiar quality in the region. As was evident in the city of Saragossa, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is naturally slow; or because there is a single bone or several bones in it.  I have explained all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the classification.
It is necessary here that I inform you of the treatment of fistula and abscess which is done by hand surgery. Know that fistulas may occur in all parts of the body; some of them terminate in large veins and arteries or in a nerve.
Or to the peritoneum, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to a rib of the ribs, or to a vertebra of the vertebrae of the back, or to a joint of the compound joints, such as the hand and the foot; and some of them lead to the vicinity of a principal organ and so forth.
Now, those fistulas which are of this kind are among the difficult-to-cure diseases which do not accept treatment and working on them is difficult and ignorant. If there are fistulas which have not reached these places which I mentioned, and I hoped and expected their treatment,
And you wished to arrive at knowledge of that, then take a probe of copper or iron. If the fistula runs straight, examine it with this. If there is a bend in the fistula, examine it with a probe of thin lead, for lead is soft in its body.
It will thus be easy to enter and bend towards the bend. If the fistula has many openings, you cannot determine them with the probe. Inject one of its openings, then the moisture which you inject will proceed towards the other openings and flow.
Then thoroughly investigate in whichever way you can to know if there is a bone or nerve, or if the bottom of the fistula is far or near, or if it is a single fistula with many openings.  Ascertain all this to the extent of your ability by questioning the patient and noting the pain when you touch the place.
And similar signs and causes of the swelling. If you ascertain all this truthfully, then proceed to treatment with confidence. That is, you should consider whether...
The fistula is apparent and near, or in a safe place far from a joint or nerve or artery or vein or one of the places I mentioned to you, then incise the fistula as I described and remove from it the accretion and the corrupt flesh and the excess flesh.
And whatever grows in its opening of flesh or wart and the like, and treat it until it heals. If the fistula is deep and straight, then you should incise it deeply as much as possible, then cleanse it of all its corrupt flesh.
Then use a twisted thread soaked in strong medicines and insert it into the bottom of the fistula which you can reach with an instrument. Do this repeatedly until that strong medicine eats away all that remains of corruption in the bottom of the fistula.
Then treat it with ointments that grow healthy flesh until it heals. If it does not heal with this, cauterize it as previously described. If the cause of the fistula is bone and that is clear to you, then probe it and incise it as I told you, if nothing prevents you from.
A nerve, or a major organ, as we said. If the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then scrape it until its corruption is gone, then treat it with what will heal it until it heals. If it does not heal and the pus continues as it was doing.
Know that you have not reached complete removal of the corruption.  Expose all of it again and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it to the best of your ability, then dress it. If it heals, otherwise know that that corruption is in a deep recess. Expose all of it again.
And thoroughly scrape and cleanse it to the best of your ability. If the corrupted bone is small and you can pull it out, then pull it out with fine forceps suitable for that purpose.
If there are many bones, thoroughly extract them all and do not leave any behind to the best of your ability. If something of the ligaments obstructs you, and there is no danger in cutting them, then cut those ligaments and the flesh if it is there, and extract the bones.
If you cannot extract the bones immediately, apply something to the wound that will cause it to fester, and leave it for several days until it festers what is around those bones of flesh and ligaments. Keep the wound from healing the incision that you made during your work.
Rather, if you fear that, place upon it cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments. For when it festers those ligaments and loosens the bones, it will be easy to extract and pull them out.
If it is a single large bone, such as a bone of the leg or thigh bone, and so on, and only its surface is corrupted, then scrape it thoroughly until that blackness and corruption disappears, then dress the wound. If a large part of it is corrupted and the corruption has reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it.
And cut it all away to where the corruption ends. Then treat it until it heals. And I will tell you of a case of gangrene which occurred in a man's leg, to serve as an example and aid for your treatment. This man was young.
About thirty years old, he suffered pain in his leg due to something moving within his body until the materials reached the leg and it swelled greatly. There was no external cause. Then time passed, along with the mistakes of the doctors, until the swelling opened.
And much material flowed from it, and he was poorly treated until the leg became gangrenous and many openings appeared in it, all discharging pus and the body's humors. A group of physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no one among them skilled in surgery.
Until they consulted me. I saw his leg and the material flowing from those openings in great abundance, and the man's body was emaciated and his color yellow.  Then I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
And I found them leading to each other from all sides of the leg. So I began by incising one of those openings until I exposed some of the bone. And I found it corrupt, eaten away, blackened, putrefied, and perforated until the hole penetrated to the marrow.  Then I opened up what was revealed to me.
And I reached the corrupted bone. I believed that there was no corruption in the bone other than what I had cut and opened, and that I had excised it. Then I proceeded to dress the wound with cauterizing medications for a longer period, but it did not heal. Then I returned and exposed the bone again, beyond the first exposure.
And I found the corruption continuous with the bone. Then I opened up what was also revealed to me of that corruption. Then I attempted to dress it, but it did not heal nor unite. Then I examined it again, and I continued to cut away the bone piece by piece and attempt to heal it, but it would not heal, until I had cut away about a span of bone.
And I removed it with its marrow. Then I dressed it with medications, and it healed quickly and recovered.  This repetition in its operation and incision was necessary because of the weakness of the patient, his low tolerance, and my fear for his death, because he frequently experienced severe fainting from excessive vomiting.  He recovered completely.
And in the place of the bone, firm flesh grew, and his condition in his body improved, and his strength returned, and he managed his affairs, and in walking he did not suffer any harm whatsoever.  If a prominent bone in a part of the body is broken...
Then it should be opened in this manner: you take a ligature and tie it around the end of the prominent bone, and you instruct someone to pull it upwards, and you make another ligature of thicker wool than the first ligature.
Then you tie it around the flesh beneath the bone and pull its end so that the flesh is drawn downwards, and you expose the flesh from the place that you wish to open so that the saw does not injure the flesh, and you place a piece of wood or a board firmly beneath the bone from below.
Because if you do that, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the corrupt bone.  The sawing should be done a little above the place of corruption, lest there be corruption in the cavity of the bone which does not appear on its surface to the touch, so that you are obliged to saw it again.
If the bone is corrupt and not prominent, but its parts are joined together, and the corruption is in its middle or in some part of it, then expose the flesh from all its sides. Then place the piece of wood underneath, and then saw it from the first side where the corruption is.
When you have finished sawing it, saw it from the other side, and let the sawing be a little distance from the corruption, as we have said. If the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupt joint itself and saw the bone where it joins from the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy except excision. If the corruption is in the metacarpus of the hand or foot, the treatment is very difficult, but you should peel away the corruption however it appears to you, and excise it and cleanse it in any way you can.
And by whatever method it is straightened for you, provided a vein or nerve does not obstruct you. And know that the instruments and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, their arrangement, their thickness and thinness, their size and smallness, their hardness and porosity. Therefore, for each type of work, one should prepare an instrument similar to that work.
Know that the operations themselves may indicate to you the type of instrument you need, if you have long experience and knowledge of the arts of this profession, for that is from the mastery of the profession and witnessing many types of diseases. He may therefore devise for himself instruments similar to those for each disease, and I have depicted for you at the end of this chapter several instruments.
Making them examples to be followed and a measure by which you measure others, God willing. Image of a saw:

Chapter eighty-seven.
In the amputation of limbs and the sawing of bones.
The limbs may become putrid, either from an external cause or from an internal cause. If you treat that corruption with medicinal treatment and the treatment is not effective, and you see the corruption spreading in the limb, nothing restraining it,
it is necessary to amputate that member to where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may be saved thereby from death or from a great calamity which is greater than the loss of the member. And a sign of this happening to someone is that the member turns black, so that you think that fire has burned it.
Or it may fester after the blackness, until that festering spreads to what is next to that member and attacks the whole body. Then hasten to cut it off.  Likewise, if the cause of the corruption is the sting of some insects, such as a sea scorpion, or a snake, or a tarantula, and the like.
If the corruption or the sting is in the tip of a finger, amputate the finger and do not allow the corruption to spread to the hand.  Likewise, if it has taken hold in the hand, amputate it from the base of the forearm, and do not allow the corruption to spread until it takes hold in the forearm of the arm.
Likewise, if it has taken hold in the arm, amputate the arm at the elbow in the joint itself. If the corruption spreads and you see it has reached towards the shoulder, do not amputate the shoulder at all, for in that is the death of the patient.  Use other treatments as much as possible.
And likewise you do with the leg: if the corruption takes hold in the toe, amputate the toe at one of the joints; and if it takes hold in the instep, amputate the whole foot; and if it ascends to the knee, amputate the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no recourse but to leave it and leave the patient to death. The method of amputating or excising a member is to tie a ligature below the place you wish to cut off, and to tie another ligature above the place.
And one assistant pulls the lower ligature downwards, and another assistant pulls the upper ligature upwards, and you yourself dissect the flesh between the ligatures with a broad scalpel until all the flesh is exposed, then it is cut or excised. It is necessary that you place linen cloths on all sides.
Lest the saw touch the healthy part, which would cause the patient excessive pain and hot swelling. If bleeding occurs during your work, staunch the place quickly, or apply some styptic, then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the wounded member with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I will tell you about an example of a man who suffered this very condition in his leg, that is, a blackness appeared in his leg with a burning sensation like fire.
And this corruption first appeared in his toe until it took the whole leg. So the man, seeing the corruption spreading in his limb, with the intensity of pain and burning he felt, cut it off at the joint and healed.
Then, after a long time, the same corruption appeared in the index finger of his hand. He came to me, and I tried to restrain that corruption with the medicines I applied to the hand after cleansing his body, but the corruption did not abate and continued to spread to the other finger.
Until the corruption seized the hand, and he asked me to cut off his hand. I refused, hoping to restrain the corruption, and I also feared for his life if I cut off his hand, because the man's strength was failing.  When he despaired of me, he returned to his town.
Then I heard that he himself cut off his entire hand and healed.  I have recounted this story to be helpful in cases of this type of disease, and to serve as a guide to be followed and acted upon.
Chapter Eighty-eight.
On the treatment of fistulas and how to inject them with medicines.
If a swelling occurs in one of the fleshy organs, and the duration of the swelling is prolonged until it gathers matter, then bursts or is incised, and all the matter within it comes out, leaving the place empty like a vessel.
And the skin over it is like a rag, thinned, and its corruption has not affected the bone, nerve or ligament. Therefore, it deserves to be called a fistula, and not a sinus, unless its duration is prolonged until the corruption affects one of these organs.
Then it is called a sinus or fistula, and its treatment is to cut away all that skin completely, as it extends, especially if it has thinned and become like a rag, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere, and its corruption has not reached that extent, and there is a thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with injection. This is done by looking at the fistula; if it is large and the pus that flows from it is foul-smelling, then inject it with the Egyptian medicine.
which is vinegar, oil, honey, and verdigris in equal parts, mixed in a vessel and cooked over the fire until the medicine thickens and reaches the consistency of honey. Then take what you need and dilute it with water and honey, and inject the fistula with it, and bind its opening, and leave the medicine inside for about two hours.
Then remove it by pressing; do this for several days until the fistula is cleansed and the foul smell is gone.  It may also be injected with ash water if this medicine is not available. This is made by taking the ash of grapevine wood or oak wood and pouring water over it.
Then strain it and inject it until you are certain that the fistula has been washed. If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or ash water, then inject it with water and honey in which some powdered verdigris has been mixed.
Or inject it with honey and wine mixed together, for honey cleanses and washes, and wine closes the fistula, especially if the wine has astringent and drying properties. When you have done this several times and are sure that the fistula's corruption has gone, inject it with something that will cause flesh to grow in it.
Such as taking palm ointment and dissolving it in rose oil and astringent wine, or injecting it with some of the other medicines and ointments whose descriptions we have given in the chapter on ointments. If the opening of the fistula is narrow and does not allow for the injection tube,
widen it slightly with an iron instrument, or place in it a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or theriac ointment until it widens.  Similarly, if the opening is wide, bring its edges together with sutures, leaving only enough space for the injection tube and no more.
Similarly, if the opening from which pus flows is high up, incise it in the lowest part so that the pus flows downwards; for if the pus is trapped in the depth of the fistula, it prevents the growth of flesh.
If you cannot incise the fistula downwards as you wish, then position the limb so that the pus flows out easily, as best you can, so that none of the pus is retained at all.
As for the emollient medicines that are applied externally to the fistula and the method of applying them, take one of the emollient ointments appropriate to your purpose and spread it on a linen cloth; the cloth should be large enough to cover the entire fistula. Then, with scissors, make a hole opposite the opening of the fistula, slightly larger than the fistula opening.  Then spread some emollient ointment on another cloth, to the size of the hole, and place it there, somewhat away from the opening of the fistula, to facilitate the exit of pus. Do not remove the large cloth until later.
several days; as for the one on the opening of the fistula, remove it at all times so that what has collected there of pus may come out, and to see what flows from it of pus, whether it is much or little, mature or immature.
Also, examine the fistula itself: does the patient feel pain in it, or not, and is there swelling or not? If your treatment continues thus and you see that the matter that comes out is little at the opening of the fistula.
Know that the fistula has adhered or is close to adhering. Then, resolve it the next day or the third day. When you open the wound, change the cloths and renew the ointment. If, after a long time, thin pus comes out of the fistula, do not despair of its closure.
Its healing is near, for some people are slow in the growth of flesh in their wounds, and others the opposite. If, after many days, immature pus comes out of the fistula, know that the fistula has not closed. If the closure of the fistula is slow,
and its matter is prolonged, increase the drying medications, and let them be medications that are moist in their consistency and dry in their strength, like date-palm ointment if an excess of litharge has been added to it. Among the most effective things to treat it with is to take date-palm ointment.
and dissolve it with rose oil and sprinkle on it old wine, moderate in consistency, then knead it with it and use it.  He may do something similar if date-palm ointment is not available: honey, if you cook it until it thickens and use it; or take myrrh, aloes, and costus, crush everything, and sprinkle it on the honey while it is on the fire. Then apply it on a cloth and bind it to the fistula, or apply it with the honey that you cooked until it thickens, and sprinkle on it the medicaments, and place on it the cloths and bind it.
Long zedoary, the root of the asphodel,  the flour of the  cress and the centaury may be used in such a case, singly or together. Crush them, sift them, and sprinkle them on the honey described and use it. If the matter of the fistula is prolonged and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption in its depth.
And if it has affected the bones, or other hard nerves, or ligaments, treat it as a fistula, as previously described.
Chapter eighty-nine.
On the treatment of the felon, the diseased nail, the cutting of the excess finger, and the separation of adherent fingers.
A felon is an excessive amount of flesh that grows under the toenail or fingernail, and it may grow in the other fingers. If its duration is prolonged and its treatment is neglected, it swells into a hot tumor.
Or it becomes putrid and foul until it eats the root of the nail, and perhaps it spoils the whole nail, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone until it has a foul odor and the tip of the finger becomes broad, and its color becomes livid.  If you treat it with what we mentioned in the division and your treatment does not work,
it is necessary to cut with an iron instrument all the excess that grows from the nail, then cauterize the wound afterward; for cauterization is very beneficial in this. And as for if the bone is healthy and the nail is also healthy,
and the corner containing the nail had constricted the flesh inward and caused it to be pinched and injured, it is necessary to place a thin pledget under the corner of the nail which pinches the flesh and raise it upward and cut that flesh gently.
And you place on what remains of the flesh of the cauterizing corrosive medicines until it is all gone, then you treat it with ointments until it heals. And as for if the corruption has affected the bone, it is necessary to cut that bone and remove it, for it will not heal as long as there is corrupt bone at all.
If you see the corrosion and corruption spreading in the finger, cut it off at one of the joints as previously mentioned, then treat it until it heals. If the nail receives a blow or injury and severe pain occurs in it, it is necessary to bleed the patient first.
Then you incise the nail with a sharp scalpel, an oblique incision from above to below, and be careful not to reach with the incision to the flesh under the nail, for by that you cause the patient severe pain and it will be a cause for the growth of excess flesh in the place, then treat the place with that which calms pains.
As for the supernumerary finger which develops in some people's hands, perhaps it is entirely fleshy, and perhaps it has bones in some of it, and perhaps it has a nail in it, and the growth of some of them is at the base of the joint of some fingers.
Or its growth is in some of the joints of the finger, and that which grows in some of the joints of the finger does not move, and that which grows at the joint of the finger perhaps moves.  As for that which is only fleshy, its cutting is easy, and that is to cut it at its base with a wide scalpel.
As for that whose growth is at the base of the joint, its treatment is difficult, so avoid cutting it. As for that which grows in the finger at one of the joints, it is necessary to first cut its flesh in a circular cut to the bone, then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for that.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion which occurs to the fingers, some of them to some, that often occurs, and that is from that with which a person is born, and it is at the healing of a wound or a burn of fire and the like. Then it is necessary to incise that adhesion.
Until the fingers return to their natural form, then place between them a pledget or a piece of cloth soaked in rose oil, lest it quickly adhere, and separate them, or place a thin lead plate between them until the place heals as it should.
And likewise, if adhesion occurs to some of the fingers with the palm, then incise that adhesion according to what is suitable and improves the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On cutting off polyps.
Polyps are thick, twisted veins filled with dark, superfluous humors, occurring in most parts of the body, most frequently in the legs, especially in the legs of those who carry weights and burdens.  First, it is necessary to vigorously rub the body with black hellebore.
Then bleed the patient in the basilic vein. As for treating them with iron, there are two methods: one is to incise and remove the black blood; the other is to extract the entire vein.  As for incision, the leg should first be bathed in warm water.
until the thick, turbid blood is dissolved. Then the patient's leg is bound from above the thigh to below the knee with a bandage, and the vein is incised in one, two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then extract the black blood with your hand from below the leg upwards and from above downwards, until you have removed the amount you deem sufficient, or what the patient's strength can bear. Then bind it and instruct him to avoid foods that produce black bile, and repeat purging and bloodletting.
When the veins are full and this harms the patient.  As for extraction, this is how it is done: shave the patient's leg if there is a lot of hair, then take him to the bathhouse and bathe his leg in warm water until it reddens and sweats, or have him perform vigorous exercise.
If a bathhouse is not available, heat the limb. Then incise the skin over the vein lengthwise, either at its end near the knee or at its lower part near the heel. Then open the skin with hooks and peel the vein from each side until it is palpable.
When it appears, you will see it is dark red; once freed from the skin, it appears like a tendon. Then insert a probe under it until it rises and emerges from the skin, and suspend it with a smooth, blind hook. Then make another incision near that incision, about three fingers wide.
Then peel the skin from over the vein until it appears, then lift it with the probe as you did before and suspend it with another hook as you did initially. Then make another incision, or many incisions if necessary.
Then extract it and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel. Then pull and extract it until it emerges from the second incision. Then pull it to the incision above it and do this until you pull it from the third incision, the highest of all the incisions, until when all of it emerges, cut it.
If it does not yield to pulling and extraction, insert a needle with strong doubled thread, tie it, and pull. Insert the probe underneath and twist it with your hand to each side until it emerges. Take care that it does not break; if it breaks, it will be very difficult for you to extract it.
And some harm enters the patient from it.  When you have extracted it entirely, place wool soaked in a potion and rose oil or olive oil on the wounds and treat it until it heals. If the varix is convoluted, with twists and turns in various directions,
and not straight as we said, then it is necessary to make incisions on it at each part of its convolutions and points of twisting, then suspend it with hooks until you extract it completely.  Take care when incising it that you do not cut or injure the vein, for it will be difficult for you to extract it, so exert yourself carefully.
Image of the incision used to incise the varix:
Image of the blind hook:
It should not be hooked like other hooks, nor should it be sharp-pointed, lest it injure the vein. It should be thick, smooth, and curved, for if it is thin it will cut the vein with its thinness; rather it should have a considerable thickness, as we said.
Chapter Ninety-One.
On the removal of the urban varix.
This varix develops in the legs in hot countries like the Hejaz and the lands of the Arabs, and in hot, barren lands with little fertility.  It may also develop in other parts of the body besides the legs, and its development is due to an infection that occurs under the skin.
As happens with living creatures and worms inside the bodies, and gourd-like swellings and worms that develop between the skin and flesh, the sign of the beginning of this varix is that a severe inflammation occurs in the leg, then the area ulcerates, and then the varix begins to emerge from the site of that ulceration.
It is as if it were the root of a plant or animal.  When its tip appears, it should be wrapped with a small piece of lead weighing from one to two drachms, and tied, leaving the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever something emerges from it to the outside, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it becomes very long, cut some of it and wrap the remainder.
