Do not cut it from its root before it has all emerged, for if you cut it, it will contract and enter the flesh, causing a swelling and infection in the area and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it should be managed and pulled out little by little until it is completely removed and nothing of it remains in the body.  From this varix, in some people, something may emerge that is five or ten cubits in length.
It has been reported to me that twenty cubits emerged from a man. If it breaks off while you are treating it, insert a probe into its hole and gently probe it along the body until all its matter is emptied, then try to disinfect the area with medications for several days.
Then treat it with the treatment for tumors. This varix may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the leg or in the leg itself, resulting in many openings, and from each opening a branch emerges. Treat it as we mentioned in the classification and as previously described.
Chapter Ninety-Two.
Concerning the incision for worms generated under the armpit, called the cow's disease.
This disease is called in some of our countries the cow's disease because it frequently affects cows. It is a single small worm that is generated between the skin and the flesh, and moves throughout the body, ascending and descending.
It is perceived by touch as it moves from one member to another until it breaks through wherever it breaks through the skin, and emerges. Its formation is from the corruption of some humors, as happens with worms, snakes, and the gourd-like growth in the abdomen.
The harm to be expected from it is that if it moves in the body and ascends to the head and reaches the eye, it may open it and emerge, destroying the eye. This happens frequently. If you wish to treat it and remove it, that is done when it moves and becomes perceptible to the touch.
It is necessary to tie tightly above and below it with a strong ligature, then incise it and remove it. If it has sunk into the flesh and you do not find it, apply cautery with fire to the area until you burn it. The greatest harm to be expected from it is its damage to the eye, as we have said.
If you see it has reached the head near the eye, tie tightly below it on the forehead, then incise and remove it.  The patient should be treated with purgatives to cleanse the body of bad, putrid humors, and should abstain from foods that produce putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
Concerning the incision for the disease known as the gnawing ulcer.
This disease, which in our country is called *an-nafir*, is a pain that appears in some organs, then moves from one organ to another. I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in the countryside. She bared her arm and showed me a slight swelling in a vein of the forearm. After a short while, I saw that swelling moving along the ulna like a worm, ascending to her shoulder as quickly as possible, like mercury.
When it moved from one place to another, the pain disappeared from that place and settled in the shoulder. Then, after a while, it ran throughout the body until it reached the other arm.  Then she told me that it circulated through her whole body, as I witnessed.
I was amazed by the speed of its movement from one organ to another. I had never before seen this disease with my own eyes in this way, except that I had seen many people whose pain moved from one organ to another, but I had not seen it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
I could only explain it by the fact that the woman was a country dweller, with a dry body and exposed veins; therefore, that moving wind was perceptible.  It would not appear in the same way in people of comfort and with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it and the patient feels pain, and if it appears to you visibly as we have mentioned, then tie above and below it tightly with a bandage, and incise it so that the contained wind escapes, and cauterize the place.
If you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it by means of things that dispel and disperse the winds, such as the seeds of  *menten* and *sakbij* and similar medicines.
Chapter ninety-four.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the places in the body where they lodge. As for their differences according to their types, there are large and small ones, hollow glass ones and solid glass ones, and some have three points and others four.
Some have barbs and some have splinters. As for those according to the organs in which they lodge, they are of two kinds: either the organs are hollow principal organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like; if an arrow has lodged in one of these organs and you see the signs of death which I shall describe to you later, then you should avoid extracting that arrow from it, for death will overtake its owner in most cases. But if these bad signs do not appear and the arrow has not disappeared into the depth of the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  Among the signs [of injury to] the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane which is over the brain, then there will result from that a severe headache, stupor, and dizziness.
But if these bad signs do not appear and the arrow has not disappeared into the depth of the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  Among the signs [of injury to] the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane which is over the brain, then there will result from that a severe headache, stupor, and dizziness. and redness in the eyes and redness of the tongue, and convulsions, and confusion of mind, and vomiting; and perhaps blood will come out from the nostrils or ears; and perhaps speech will cease and the voice will be lost; and there will come from the place of the wound a white moisture resembling gruel, and there flows from it something like the water of flesh.
If these signs appear to you, then refrain from treating the patient and from extracting the arrow if it has not been extracted. As for the signs [of an arrow] if it strikes the heart and is near the left breast, and one feels it as if it has penetrated into something solid and not into something hollow;
and perhaps the arrow will have a movement resembling the movement of the pulse, and black blood will flow from the wound; and coldness of the extremities and cold sweat and fainting will follow; then know that death is inevitably approaching. And a sign of the arrow if it wounds the lung is the exit of frothy blood from the wound.
And the vessels which are near the neck will swell, and the color of the patient will change, and he will breathe with a high breath, and he will seek to inhale cold air. If the arrow strikes the diaphragm which is in the chest, then it will be near the small ribs, and the breathing will be great with severe pain.
And he will sigh and all the members of the shoulders will move. And if the arrow strikes the liver, severe pain will follow, and blood will come out from the wound which resembles the liver in its redness. And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then perhaps something undigested from the food will come out from the wound.
And its matter is apparent. And if the arrow strikes the abdomen and is fixed in it, and something of the stool or fat or intestine which has been perforated comes out from the wound, then there is no hope in its treatment or in extracting the arrow. If the arrow strikes the bladder and the urine comes out;
and something protrudes from it to the outside, and the pain becomes severe for the patient, then know that he is doomed. As for the other members, like the face, the eye, the throat, the shoulder, the arm, the vertebrae of the back, the clavicle, the thigh, the leg, and similar members, they are mostly safe.
When the arrow does not encounter an artery or nerve, and the arrow is not poisoned. And I tell you some of what I have witnessed concerning these arrows so that you may deduce from that your treatment. And that is that an arrow once struck a man in the corner of his eye at the root of the nose.
Then I extracted it for him from the other side under the lobe of the ear, and he recovered and nothing bad happened to his eye. And I extracted another arrow for a Jew which had struck him in the lobe of his eye under the lower eyelid, and the arrow had been hidden, and I grasped only its small end which is attached to the wood.
And it was a large arrow from the composite bows, square-headed, smooth, without barbs.  The Jew recovered, and no harm befell his eye. And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and the arrow was Arabic.
And it is the one with two barbs.  So I made an incision for him between the two jugular veins, and it had sunk into his throat. So I gently manipulated it until I extracted it, and the Christian was safe and recovered.  And I extracted another arrow for a man which had struck him in his abdomen, and we judged that he would die from it.
Then when thirty days or so had passed, and nothing had changed in his condition, I made an incision over the arrow and maneuvered it and extracted it, and he recovered and no harm befell him. And I saw a man whom an arrow had struck in his back, and the wound had closed over it.
Then after seven years the arrow emerged at the root of her thigh. And I saw a woman who had been struck in her abdomen by an arrow, and the wound had closed, and the arrow remained. Nothing changed in her condition, nor did she experience any harm from it in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man whom an arrow had struck in his face, and the wound closed, and he felt little pain from it. And there are many similar cases. And I extracted an arrow for a man from the Sultan's guard which had struck him in the middle of his nose, inclining slightly to the right.
And the entire arrow was hidden. I was called to treat him three days after the arrow struck. I found the wound of the arrow very narrow, so I explored it with a thin probe, but I did not feel it. He felt a stinging pain under his ear on the right side of the incision.
I hoped that this stinging pain was from the tip of the arrow, so I applied a dressing with drawing and ripening power, hoping that the area would swell and the sign of the arrow would appear, so I could make an incision. But nothing happened in the area to indicate that the arrow had reached there.
I continued the dressing for many days, but nothing happened. Then the wound closed, and the patient despaired of its removal for a time, until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and told me. I then applied a strong, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it, and I felt the thin tip of the arrow that adheres to the wood. Then I increased the opening of the wound with that strong medicine until the tip of the arrow appeared before our eyes.  And it took me about four months with this.
Then, when the wound widened and I was able to insert the forceps into it, I pulled and moved it, but it did not respond to removal.  I continued to gently manipulate it and devise methods with various instruments until one day I grasped it firmly with forceps, as its image is shown at the end of the chapter.
And the physicians judged that the cartilage of his nose would not heal, but I healed it, and the wound joined and the patient recovered completely, without any further harm. And I tell you the method of removing some arrows so that you may use this as a measure and guide for what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts and details of this craft cannot be grasped by description, nor encompassed by a book.  Rather, the skillful craftsman infers from the little to the much, and from what is present to what is absent, and devises a new action and a new instrument in the case of unusual occurrences.
If we turn to this craft, I say that arrows are removed from the members in which they are lodged in two ways: either by pulling them from the place where they entered, or from the opposite direction; and those that are removed from where they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding in a fleshy place, so it is pulled and removed. If it does not yield to removal at the time it is encountered, then you should leave it for some days until the flesh around it decays, so that it is easier to pull and remove it.  The same applies if it is lodged in bone.
And if it does not yield to removal, leave it also for some days and attempt to pull and move it every day, for it will come out.  If it does not yield to removal after some days, then you should bore around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine borer, until you widen the space for the arrow, then pull and remove it.
If the arrow lodged in the bone of the head has penetrated deeply into one of the ventricles of the brain, and some of those symptoms I have mentioned to you appear in the patient, then refrain from pulling the arrow and leave it until its condition improves after some days.
then proceed to pull out and remove the arrow. If it is very firmly lodged and does not yield to pulling, use the borers around the arrow as I have described to you, then treat the place until it heals.  But if the arrow has disappeared into a place in the body, and is hidden from sensation, then probe for it with a probe. If you feel it, pull it out with some instruments suitable for pulling it out. If you cannot reach it because of the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow, and there is neither bone, nor nerve, nor vein there,
then incise it until the wound is widened and you can reach the arrow so that you may remove it. If it has barbs, grasp them, and free the flesh caught in them from every side by every means you can. If you cannot free the flesh, break the barbs and twist them until you free them.
And when you try to remove the arrow from whatever place it may be, use the twisting of your hand with forceps in all directions until you free it. Be extremely gentle lest the arrow break and it becomes difficult for you to pull it out and remove it. If you cannot manage it at the time,
leave it for some days until the flesh around it putrefies, then return to it; it will then be easier. If bleeding occurs, use what we have mentioned in its chapter. Carefully avoid cutting a vein, nerve, or tendon, and use skill.
By every means you can, free the arrow; let this be done gently, slowly, and firmly, as I have described to you.  It is necessary that, when pulling out the arrow, you position the patient in the same way he was when the arrow struck; this is the most appropriate.
If you cannot do this, use whatever positions you can. As for an arrow that exits on the opposite side, either some of it will have emerged externally, or you will feel the tip of the arrow through the skin nearby, and see that it is protruding.
Make an incision over it, and let the incision be large enough for the forceps. Then pull it out, for it will be easy to remove. If it is stuck in a bone, twist your hand in a circular motion until the arrow affects the bone and makes a space for itself, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for some days, then return to it until it comes out. If the arrow shaft is still present, push it with it. If the shaft has fallen off and you want to use pushing, insert the hollow instrument so that its cavity enters the tail of the arrow, then push it with it.
If the arrow is hollow, push it with an instrument that enters that cavity; the arrow will then easily come out. If the arrow is poisoned, it is necessary that you cut away all the flesh where the poison has penetrated, if you are able to do so, then treat it with what is suitable for that.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, or in the abdomen, or in the bladder, or in the flank, and it is near enough to be felt with a probe, and you can make an incision over it, then make an incision, being careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then suture the wound if it needs stitching, and treat it until it heals.  Image of the forceps with which arrows are extracted:
Their ends are like the beak of a bird; they are made like a file, so that when they grasp the arrow or anything else, they do not let go.  Various sizes, large, small and medium, are made, each according to the size of the arrow, the width of the wound, and its narrowness.
Image of the hollow pusher:
Image of the solid pusher:
This has a solid end like an awl, to make it easier to insert into the hollow arrow and push it.
Chapter ninety-five.
On phlebotomy.
The veins usually opened in the body number thirty; of these, sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the “whispering” veins, and the two arteries in the temples which are visible, and the two veins at the inner canthus of the eyes, known as the “seeing” veins.
And the vein erect in the middle of the forehead, and the vein at the tip of the nose, and the two jugular veins in the neck, and the two veins in the upper lip, and the two veins in the lower lip—these veins are known as the “four,” and the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins opened in the arm and hand, they are five: one is the cephalic, which is on the outer side and the common people call it the vein of the head; and the median, which is the middle vein and is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, and the common people call it the vein of the body.
And the basilic, which is situated on the inner side and is also called the axillary, and the common people call it the vein of the abdomen; and the vein of the forearm, which is situated on the ulna, to which it adheres, and which appears clearly above the thumb, and the azygos.
And this is the vein which is between the little finger and the ring finger; it has two branches. And in the leg and foot there are three veins: one is that which is below the hollow of the knee on the outer side; the second is the saphenous, and its place is at the heel on the inner side; and the sciatic vein, and its place is at the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three similar veins. As for the two veins behind the ears, the benefit of opening them is for chronic colds, migraine, and ringworm, and chronic bad sores of the head, and the manner of opening them is as I describe.
And that is to shave the head of the patient and rub the back of his head in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth, rubbing well; then the patient's neck is constricted with a turban until the two veins and their position appear behind the ears in the two low places of the head; then you examine them with your finger, and wherever you feel their pulsation under your finger there you mark with ink.
Then you take a lancet, which is known as the *nashshal*, and you insert it under the vein in the skin until the lancet reaches the bone; then you raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein together with the skin, a cleanly severed cut, and the length of the cut is the amount of two fingers joined together.
Or something similar, and you let out the amount of blood that you want, then you bind them with bandages and leave them until they heal.  And it has been explained earlier in the book the cutting and cauterization of them. As for the two arteries which are in the temples, the benefit of opening them is for chronic migraine, severe headache, persistent ophthalmia, and the flow of sharp humors directed towards the eyes.
And the manner of opening them is as I describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the veins appear clearly to the touch, and their pulsation becomes evident under your finger; then you mark with ink. Then you lift the skin from above the vein upwards with your index finger.
And you insert the lancet, the *nashshal*, from below and raise the vein upwards and sever it as you did with the other two veins, and you let out blood according to your need, then you loosen the constriction of the patient's neck and place your finger on the vein for a while.
Then you place cotton and a bandage on it and bind it tightly from above and leave it until it heals.  Their mention, cutting, and cauterization have been explained earlier in the book. As for opening the vein of the forehead, its benefit, after opening the vein of the head, is for chronic facial ailments such as ringworm, ulcers, and erysipelas.
And the manner of opening it, as I describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the vein appears, then you take the instrument called the axe, and this is its image:
You place the projecting spike which is at the head of the axe on the vein itself and strike from above with a comb or something similar, and you let the blood flow to the amount you desire, then you loosen the constriction of the patient's neck and bind it until it heals.
It may also be opened with a wide lancet, but the lancet should not have a sharp point like other lancets, rather it should have a slightly broad point, and you open it obliquely because the bone is near, so the lancet may break if it is thin.
As for the two veins which are at the temples, their benefit is for ailments of the eye such as ringworm, inflammation, and cataracts, and diseases of the face. As for the manner of opening them, it is that the patient constricts his neck with a turban, then you open them while you are standing over his head, and let the opening be slightly obliquely lengthwise with a small, slightly broad lancet.
For the location has no flesh; if the lancet is thin-pointed, it may break. Then you let out the amount of blood you need, and you place cotton on them and bind them for one night, then you unbind them. As for opening the vein of the nose, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
And severe headache and diseases of the face like the red erysipelas which appears in the nose, especially if it is chronic.  As for the manner of opening it, the patient constricts his neck, then you hold his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long lancet.
And you pierce it in the middle of the nasal bone itself, between the nasal septum, straightly, because the vein does not appear to the touch there; then the blood will immediately appear.  And you should move your hand with the lancet a little and let out the amount of blood you need, then bind it for a night, for it clots quickly.
As for the jugular veins, the benefit of opening them is for shortness of breath, the beginning of leprosy, and melancholic diseases which appear on the surface of the skin, such as black vitiligo, scabies, bad sores, and cankers. As for the manner of opening them, the patient constricts below them in his neck with a bandage, and the surgeon stands at the head of the patient.
And the patient sits on a chair, then you open the vein lengthwise with a somewhat wide incision, then you let out a moderate amount of blood, or according to what you see as necessary for that. Then you do likewise with the other vein, then you untie the bandage.
And you bind the veins moderately so that the patient does not suffocate, and you leave him until the next day, for the wound will heal. As for the veins of the temple, the benefit of opening them, after opening the veins of the head, is that it benefits from thrush in the mouth, corruption of the gums, bad sores, cracking of the lips, and bad sores.
which are in the nose and around it.  As for the manner of opening them, you seat the patient before you and bind his neck with a turban, then you turn back his lips and look at the two veins, one of which you see to the right of the lip and the other to the left of it, and you discern them by their blackness.
And that is also because around them are small, black veins; you cut them with a cutting incision. If it is difficult for you and you do not know what they are, aim to cut the largest and most obvious of them.  Likewise, do with the two veins which are in the lower lip.
As for the two veins which are under the tongue, the benefit of opening them, after opening the veins of the head, is for the inflammations which occur in the throat from a disease of the uvula and diseases of the mouth.  As for the manner of opening them, you seat the patient before you facing the sun, and you raise his tongue and look under the tongue, on one side you will see a vein and on the other side a vein, and their color tends toward blackness.
Then you open them, and be careful not to go too far in cutting them, for beneath them are arteries, and perhaps a flow of blood from those arteries may result. As for the three veins which are opened in the elbow, they are those which custom has established for opening in all people, and their opening is done in two ways.
Either by piercing with a wide, rounded or olive-shaped lancet to the depth, or by cutting with a knife-shaped lancet, which is the *nashal* [and this is its image], image of the wide, rounded lancet:
It is wide, as you see, so that it is suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, apparent, thick veins, which contain thick, turbid blood. And this is the image of the olive-shaped lancet:
And this lancet is less wide and thinner at the tip, suitable for opening thin veins which contain thin, yellowish blood. And this is the image of the *nashal* lancet:
This is the *nashal*, which is suitable for cutting, and there are types of it, wide and narrow, according to the width and narrowness of the veins also. And by this one may infer others, and it is well-known among craftsmen.
As for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of opening it is that it draws blood from the illnesses which are under the throat and neck, from what is near the chest and abdomen. And it is necessary for the phlebotomist, when opening it, to beware of it and be cautious, for beneath it is an artery. If he errs and goes too far in piercing with the lancet, he will cut that artery.
Then bleeding will occur. Therefore, it should not be opened with a lancet for piercing, but it should be opened by cutting with the *nashal*. If the basilic vein does not appear clearly, then it should be avoided and one should move to another, or seek one of its branches.
Or you may open in its place the vein of the arm, for it is clear, and cut it with the *nashal* lancet, as we said. If you wish to open it itself, then before tightening the arm, you should feel the place until you identify the place of the pulse, then mark it with ink.
Then you tie the arm and cut the vein with a sharp cut with the *nashal* lancet, as we said, and be careful that the stroke falls far from the place of the artery. And when you see, upon tightening the ligature, a swelling in the place which you marked with ink, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood, upon opening the vein, spurt like the urine of a boy, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is from the artery.  Then immediately place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger. If the bleeding stops—and it often stops—then tighten the arm.
And leave it, and warn the patient against neglecting it, and let him be on his back and not move for several days until it heals.  If the bleeding does not stop and it overcomes you, and you do not have medicine at hand, then amputate the artery if it appears to you, for its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop.
Or take a pistachio shell and split it, and take one half and bind it tightly on the vein with a ligature and pledgets until the next day. If the bleeding stops, well and good; otherwise, treat it with what was previously mentioned of placing styptics, and stopping its bleeding is not difficult in most cases.
Because of the smallness of the wound and the tightness of the ligature on the arm, know it. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of opening it is that it draws blood from the top of the head and the lower body, because it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, as we said.
It is necessary for the one who opens it to be lying on his back when it is opened, for underneath it is a nerve. If he goes too deep with the lancet and injures the nerve, numbness occurs in it which is difficult to cure, and perhaps it will not heal at all.  And this nerve is often palpable.
If it is hidden in some people and is thin and not visible, then you should make your incision with a lancet and avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise.  As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of opening it is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for diseases of the eyes, and it is preferable in this vein in particular, if you wish to open it, to use a lancet with an olive-shaped point or a wide, flat lancet, because it is the safest of all veins, because there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
However, when opening it, you should avoid the head of the muscle with the lancet and look for the soft place. It does not harm if it is not opened on the first attempt to be opened again several times, except that it may swell in some people if it is not opened on the first attempt.
But that swelling does not harm it at all. As for the method of opening, its complications, and what should precede its treatment, the first thing is that you should know that opening is used either only for maintaining health.
Or it is used in diseases. That which is used to maintain health and longevity and to prevent the occurrence of diseases is that the opening should be in one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the cephalic, the basilic, and the median, and that the opening should be at the beginning of spring when signs of plethora appear.
And the opening should be on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours of daylight have passed. As for the opening that is used in diseases, it does not have a limited time, but whenever necessity and need require it, day or night.
And at every hour and at every time. Children should not be opened until they reach fourteen years of age, and the elderly who have passed sixty years should not be opened. If someone intends to open for any reason whatsoever.
He should cleanse his intestines before opening with a soft enema if there is much retained feces, lest putrid waste be drawn into the veins from the intestines upon opening, harming the principal organs.  And do not open the feverish, the drunk, or the intoxicated until that passes from them.
And let him also beware of opening after dysentery, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive coitus, exertion, exercise, sleeplessness, fasting, and everything that weakens the strength, whether physical or mental. Then look to the thinning of the humors beforehand if the blood is thick.
With foods, drinks, and medicines if he is able to do so. Then let him enter the bath if nothing prevents him, or let him do some exercise so that the blood is thinned, and let the opening be in the middle of the day, as we have said. And let him intend to empty his chest that day of all bad mental states.
Such as worries, anger, and fear, and all physical conditions like excessive exertion and strain, coitus, and the like. And let him prepare for his session the things that people are accustomed to using, such as various perfumes and fragrant herbs, amusements, and the like, each person according to what he can.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on a cushion higher than the cushion on which the patient sits. Then let him extend his arm and let the phlebotomist rub it with his hand twice or three times. Then let him tighten the bandage with the fillet and twist it twice or three times. Let the tightening be moderate.
For if the tightening is not moderate, either by excessive tightening, it prevents the flow of blood, or if it is relaxed, it also prevents the flow of blood. Then after the tightening, it is necessary that the patient rub both his hands together.
Until the veins swell and become perceptible to the touch. Then let the phlebotomist wipe the lancet with a little old oil, especially. Then let him place the index finger of his left hand on the vein itself, a little below the place which he wants to open, lest the vein evade and avoid the incision. For some veins you find are like a tendon; they evade when phlebotomized, and some are full of wind. When you place the lancet on them, they sink under the lancet, deceiving the phlebotomist; and he does not open the vein, or if he opens it, it is only a narrow opening.
Therefore, the phlebotomist should be certain and deliberate in all these matters. Then let him insert the lancet. If the vein is opened at that time, well and good; otherwise, let him try again a little below or above that place quickly if the place is not swollen.
If the patient swells or is distressed, leave him for a day or two, and do not tighten the ligature, for it may bring on a hot swelling, and do not let him enter the bath. Then he may repeat the phlebotomy if he wishes.  If the lancet is inserted and the opening is small, and the flow of blood is thin,
and you fear that the desired amount of blood will not come out, then return the lancet to the same opening gently, keeping it straight, and increase the opening a little. Do this quickly before the place swells, for in many people the place may swell with a small opening.
But if you see that it has swollen, do not repeat it at all, for it will not help you. Put something of oil dregs on it, for it facilitates the flow of blood, and it is better in this case than oil itself and other ointments as well.
Therefore, use oil dregs in all your phlebotomies when the flow of blood is difficult.  The theriac and the schechnaia may also do this, if one of them is applied to the place, for the blood becomes thin and dissolves if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of phlebotomy—and this often happens, especially to one who has been bled only that once, or if the opening of the vein was small—then quickly apply a sponge soaked in slightly warmed salt water, and bind it for an hour, for it will resolve.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood has completely left the vein itself or another vein. If something black or green remains in the place after a few days, it does no harm. If you wish, apply something of aloes and myrrh dissolved.
Or something of the juice of the  *fudanj* and the like. Often a swelling and a boil occur when bleeding from the basilica vein; place your hand upon it. If you find that it is soft when you press upon it, then it is a bad boil, so beware of putting anything on it of what we have mentioned.
For perhaps arterial blood will flow from it; but bandage it with something astringent to constrict the place, then treat it with the other treatments until it heals. And it is necessary to extract from each person a quantity of blood according to his strength and the color that predominates in the blood.
For if the blood is black, let it flow until it becomes red; and similarly, if you see it thick, let it flow until it becomes thin; and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is gone. And it is necessary for one who is full-bodied and strong and needs to have blood extracted at once,
to widen the opening of the vein and let the lancet be wide. And for one who is weak, the opposite. And it is necessary to extract it in stages, and let the opening be narrow.  And the best instrument to use in opening a vein is one that is sharp, well-sharpened, incised not punctured.
And this type of bloodletting is safe from bleeding and from cutting a nerve; and it is better and safer than bloodletting across or lengthwise. And if one is accustomed to fainting at bloodletting, then it is necessary to feed him before bloodletting something of bread soaked in pomegranate water or oxymel.
If he is feverish and blood is extracted from him in three or four times, then if he is of a cold temperament, let him take before bloodletting bread soaked in hydromel or in spiced honey-wine or in fragrant basil-wine.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting and its cause is the great outflow of blood, then it is necessary to give him meat-broth and thin basil-wine and use perfuming with galingale, and rub his chest with it, and use all that we mentioned in the section on fainting.
which results from vomiting. And as for one who wants to relieve his arm and to let his blood flow a second time, it is necessary for one whose bloodletting was for copious evacuation and whose strength is weak, that the blood be let little by little according to his strength on successive days.
And as for one who wants to relieve his arm and to let his blood flow a second time and whose body is strong, let him do that after seven or nine hours from his first bloodletting. And as for one who wants to draw the blood from his body to the opposite side from that to which it inclined, it is necessary to relieve him on the second or third day.
And as for one in whose body the blood is abundant, has become hot and sharp, and has caused fever, it is necessary to extract the blood from him in one go and to extract a large quantity and to widen the opening until fainting occurs, after you have observed all the conditions of bloodletting. And that you place your hand on his pulse during the flow of blood lest death occur instead of fainting, for that often occurs if the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligence occurs. And it is not necessary, if you want to loosen the arm and let the blood flow a second time,
if the mouth of the vein has closed and the exit of the blood is difficult, that you prick it forcefully or twist it strongly, for that is very bad; rather, either you leave it until you bleed him a second time, or you remove with the blade of the lancet what has congealed of the blood in the mouth of the vein.
Or you apply to it something of salt dissolved in water, or you apply to it something of theriac faruq or shukhzanaya and prick it delicately until the blood comes out; and if the vein has swollen, leave it.
And do not touch it until the swelling subsides; and if necessity demands a second bloodletting and there is no alternative, either you bleed him above that place, or you bleed him in the other arm or in another vein.
And as for phlebotomy of the basilic vein, it is bled instead of the cephalic and basilic if they are not found or are hidden, for it is composed of them; and its method of phlebotomy is that the patient puts his hand in hot water until the forearm reddens and the vein appears clearly, then it is tied above it with a ligature slightly, a moderate tying.
Then you bleed the vein at a slight angle, neither across nor lengthwise, and let the opening be wide, and let your bleeding be a little above the joint of the hand; and if the exit of the blood is difficult, return the hand to the vessel with hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until it reaches your need; and if you are in the days of summer, you may dispense with returning the hand to the hot water, and the most that makes the blood flow in hot water is in the time of winter; and phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all the veins. Because there is no underlying vein or nerve beneath it; and as for phlebotomy of the median vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments; and its method of phlebotomy is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after you put it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is no underlying vein or nerve beneath it; and as for phlebotomy of the median vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments; and its method of phlebotomy is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after you put it in hot water until the vein swells. And it becomes very apparent to the touch, then you bleed it at a slight angle, and even if you sever it completely, that will not harm him at all; and be careful not to thrust your hand deeply with the lancet, for beneath it is the nerve of the fingers and the place is bare of flesh; then return the hand to the hot water.
And leave it to flow; for if you do not return it to hot water, the blood will congeal in the mouth of the vein and be prevented from flowing; and when you have drawn out the amount of blood needed, place on the vein fat and salt lest it close quickly.
And likewise you should do with every narrow branch; and as for the benefit of phlebotomy from the left hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the spleen; and likewise you do in its phlebotomy as you did in the second, the same; and as for phlebotomy of the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases in the lower part of the body, such as uterine ailments.
And suppression of menstruation and kidney diseases and chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs and similar diseases; and its method of phlebotomy is that he puts his leg in hot water and rubs it until the veins swell, then you tie above the joint of the leg with a ligature, and the vein is located at the heel, on the outside, toward the big toe.
And many branches extend from it on the surface of the foot; therefore bleed it in the widest branch of it, or at the heel where it joins—this is best and safest; and if you bleed it on the surface of the foot, then be careful of the nerves which are beneath it on the surface of the foot.
And make your incision as if you intend to amputate it, and let the lancet be sharp; and if the exit of the blood is difficult, then let him return his leg to hot water and leave the blood flowing until it is emptied; and if the phlebotomist misses the vein on the first attempt,
let him repeat the phlebotomy a little higher; for the place is safe, and there is no fear of harm from it if you are careful of the nerve as we said; and likewise you do with the saphenous vein of the other leg, the same; and as for the sciatic vein, its place is, as we said, at the heel from the outer side.
And its benefit is for pain in the hip if that is from excessive hot blood; and its method of phlebotomy is that you put the patient in the bath and hasten, and bind his leg from the hip to above the heel by four fingers' breadth with a thin, long bandage, for it will not appear except in this way.
Then, when it appears, bleed it in whatever way is most convenient for you, either by making an oblique incision, which is best, or by amputating it or making a longitudinal incision; for its position is safe, and it is in most people extremely hidden; and if you do not find it,
and it does not appear at all to the touch, then bleed some of its branches, which are those that appear on the back of the foot towards the little finger and ring finger, and be careful of the nerves, and let out the amount of blood that you desire.
Then loosen the bandage and place cotton on the place of the phlebotomy and bandage the place, for it will quickly heal.
Chapter ninety-six.
Concerning cupping and its method of use.
Cupping vessels may be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass, and cupping is of two kinds: one is cupping with scarification and removal of blood, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping without scarification is of two kinds.
And the cupping of the sides of the neck on both sides, and the cupping of the chin, which is under the lower jaw, and the cupping of the shoulders, and the cupping of the coccyx at the base of the spine, and the cupping of the forearms, which are the middles of the arms, and the cupping of the legs, and the cupping of the heels.
Cupping draws blood from the small veins spread throughout the flesh, and for this reason it does not weaken the body as much as bloodletting, and cupping, whether with fire or without, should not be used in any illness that is caused by fullness until the whole body is purged.
If there is a need for cupping because of illness or habit, we use it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end, in the middle, and at any time whatsoever.
And that is because some people, when their blood increases to the point that they need to have it removed by cupping, feel heaviness and headache in their heads; and some feel fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck; and some feel itching in their face and sides, darkness and pain in their eyes; and some itch at the place of their cupping.
And some laugh a lot, and some find the taste of blood in their mouths, their gums swell, and they spit blood; and some sleep a lot; and some see in their sleep blood, redness, slaughter, and wounds, and things like that. So when we see any of that,
especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we order cupping at that time after two or three hours of the day have passed. As for the benefit of cupping the temples, it benefits from the heaviness in the head and water that flows into the eyes.
But it should be after the evacuation of the whole body. And this cupping may be a substitute for bloodletting of the head. And it is warned against using it for someone who is cold-headed or who has a cold, for it harms him greatly.
Therefore, the elderly and those with cold diseases in their heads should not use it, and whoever persists in it will generate forgetfulness in him.
Therefore, the cupper should be ordered to lower his hand with the cupping glass a little lower, for fear of generating forgetfulness. As for cupping the nape of the neck, it is a substitute for bloodletting of the internal jugular vein and the basilic vein.
Therefore, it benefits from asthma, shortness of breath, rupture of the respiratory organ, cough, and fullness.  The cupping of the nape of the neck should be raised a little, for if it is lowered, it will produce weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it benefits from pains occurring in the head, ophthalmia, migraine, angina, and toothache. It is a substitute for bloodletting of the basilic vein. And the cupper should be ordered not to deepen his hand with the lancet, lest he cut an artery and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it benefits from thrush in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and similar diseases in the mouth. It may take the place of bloodletting the veins in the lips. As for cupping the shoulders, it benefits from palpitations which result from fullness and heat.
As for cupping the insides of the forearms, it benefits from what benefits bloodletting of the three veins: the basilic, the internal jugular, and the cephalic; because it attracts blood from all those small veins in the flesh, and attracts those small veins from other thicker veins, until the attraction reaches the three thick veins.
The cupper should be ordered not to prolong the incision, because the place is devoid of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries. As for the single cupping applied to the coccyx, it benefits from hemorrhoids and sores of the lower part.
The cupper should be ordered that the cupping glass be large and of copper, because the place needs strong suction, and a glass cupping glass might break and make a large incision. As for cupping the legs, it visibly reduces fullness.
Because it attracts blood from the whole body, and benefits from chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder; it promotes menstruation, and benefits from boils and abscesses; and it takes the place of bloodletting the saphenous veins and the veins of the heel, except that it weakens the body greatly, and fainting occurs in most people. The cupping of the veins of the heel has benefits similar to the cupping of the legs.
The method of applying cupping glasses is that the cupping glass is first placed empty and suctioned moderately. Do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but place them quickly and remove them quickly so that the humors may flow to the place evenly. Continue to repeat this until you see the place has reddened and opened.
When the redness of blood appears, then make an incision and repeat the suction slowly. Then look at the condition of the bodies. If a person has loose flesh with porous pores, then it is necessary to incise only once, lest the place ulcerate.
And order the cupper to widen the incision and deepen it slightly, and to moderate the suction gently and with light movement. If the blood is thick, then it should be incised twice; in the first time, to open a way for the thin blood and its watery part, and in the second time, to completely extract the thick blood.
If the blood is very turbid, then repeat the incision a third time to achieve the goal. In general, if we want to extract a little blood, we are satisfied with one incision; if we want to extract a lot of blood, we make more incisions, especially if we judge that the blood is thick.
Then it is necessary to make a deep incision, and the moderate limit in the incision is only the depth of the skin. What should be used of ointments when placing cupping glasses, and of waters, and what the cupped person should avoid. As for someone whose skin is thick, hard, dry, and whose pores are tight,
then it is necessary to anoint the places of the cupping glasses with opening, softening, and resolving ointments. As for if it is in the summer time, then such as the oil of the quince, or the oil of violets, or the oil of sweet almonds, or the oil of pumpkin seeds.
And as for if it is in the winter, then such as the oil of daffodils, or the oil of iris, or the oil of chamomile, or the lily and similar. If the waste matter is thick and cold, then let the ointment be the oil of marjoram, or the oil of the  wild rue, or the oil of  milk, or the oil of rue, and similar.
If the cupped person has wide pores and soft flesh, then it is necessary to abstain from ointment. These should wash their cuppings after cupping with rose water, or with cold water, or with fox grape water, or with gourd water, or with chickweed water, and similar. And as for someone whose blood is very moist,
he should wash his cuppings with vinegar, or with water of myrtle and sumac, and similar. And as for someone whose waste matter is thick, he should wash his cuppings with old wine, or with marjoram water, or a decoction of rue, or chamomile, and similar.  And it is necessary to avoid cupping in the bathhouse and after the bathhouse.
Rather, it is necessary to use it after leaving the bathhouse by an hour or two hours. And no one should sleep after cupping. What it is necessary to manage for the cupped and the phlebotomized before cupping and after it.
It is necessary to look first. If the cupped or phlebotomized person is choleric and what predominates in his blood is sharpness and inflammation, then it is necessary that he take refrigerants like pomegranate and dandelion with vinegar, and lettuce, and oxymel, and sherbet, and similar, and he should make his foods of squabs and lamb meats, stews, and sour things, and similar.
And whoever his temperament is cold, then it is necessary that he be given honeyed drinks and dates syrup, or seed oxymel, and he should consume moderately perfumed wine, that which is between old and new, and he is ordered to little food, and he should make his food of squabs and thrushes and birds, and young pigeons, stews. And it is necessary that the drink on the day of cupping and phlebotomy be more than the food.
And it may be necessary to give some people at some times some of the theriac of al-Faruq, or musk medicine, or silphium, before cupping and before phlebotomy, or after it, to strengthen the principal organs and to thin the blood. And it is not necessary to give it to those who are feverish.
As for the cuppings which are without a condition, they are the cuppings which are placed on the liver and spleen and breasts and on the abdomen and navel and the place of the kidneys and the hip joint, for these organs do not tolerate a condition on them, but rather what is intended by them is either drawing blood from one organ to another.
Like our placing the cupping glass on the breasts in a case of epistaxis, or we use it to relieve an organ of a cold humor which has gathered in the organ, like our placing the cupping glass on the abdomen and navel, for it loosens the organ and warms it and removes the pain by resolving that humor.
And it may be placed on the kidneys if there appears in them an obstruction or a stone, for by the strength of its drawing it may perhaps open the obstruction or remove the stone from its place. And likewise it acts if it is placed on the liver and spleen in case of a humor which disturbs them. And these cuppings may be used empty, only by suction.
And it may be used with fire, and it may be used filled with lukewarm water in cases of sciatica, and that is to fill the cupping glass, and let it be large, with hot water alone, or with water in which some herbs suitable for that have been cooked.
Then it is placed full on the place and held and removed and repeated several times according to need. And this is the image of the cupping glass which is used with fire:
Its mouth should be the width of two open fingers, as we have pictured, and its depth about half a span, and there should be in its side, about its middle, a small hole about as large as a needle can enter; it is made of Chinese copper or yellow copper, thick-rimmed.
Smooth, level, polished, lest it injure the member when placed upon it; and there should be in its middle a crosswise groove of copper or iron where the lighted candle is placed.  And this cupping glass may be made larger than we have described, or smaller, according to the diseases.
And the instrument used for this: for the cupping glasses of children and thin people are not the same as those of men and stout-bodied people.  As for the method of placing this cupping glass with fire on the member, it is to bring a lighted wick of tightly-twisted linen, or a small candle of pitch, and to place it on the middle of the strengthened bar which is in the middle of the cupping glass.
So that the ascent of the fire will be upwards towards the bottom of the cupping glass, lest the body of the patient be burnt; then it is placed on the member, and the finger on the hole which we mentioned, until, when we have held the cupping glass as long as we need, the finger is removed.
And the vapor will come out through that hole, and the cupping glass will adhere to the place. Then bring the wick in the same way, and replace it if you need to. As for the cupping glass which is used in the treatment of sciatica with water, it has neither a strengthened bar nor a hole.
It is only used by being filled with water and placed on the member.  And this is its form:
And this cupping glass, the larger it is to hold much water, the better it is.
Chapter ninety-seven.
On the suspension of the leech.
Leeches are used mostly in cases where cupping glasses cannot be applied, either because of the small size of the member, like the lip and gum and so on, or because the member is devoid of flesh, like the finger and nose and so on.
And the method of using them is that you choose leeches which are in fresh, pure water, free from impurities, then leave them for a day and a night in fresh water until they are hungry and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body is first purged by bloodletting or cupping, then the affected member is wiped.
Until it reddens, then they are applied; and when they are full, they fall off. And if it is possible to cup the place, it is more effective; otherwise, wash the place with vinegar, then with much water, and rub and press. If the bleeding continues after the leech falls off, and this is a trickle, then wet a linen cloth in cold water.
And place it on top until the trickle stops. If the bleeding is profuse, sprinkle on it powdered vitriol or alum or similar astringents until the bleeding stops, or apply halves of peeled walnuts to the place.
And leave it until the walnut adheres to the place, for then the blood will stop. And it is preferable, if it is necessary to reapply leeches, not to apply those same leeches if others are available. If the leeches refuse to adhere, let the place be wiped with fresh blood.
Or pierce a needle into the place until some blood comes out, then apply them; for when they sense some blood, they adhere to the spot. And if you want them to fall off, sprinkle on them some aloes, or salt, or ashes; then they will fall off immediately.
Chapter Three.
On setting (bones).
This chapter also deals with what is needed in the art of medicine, namely the setting of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know, my sons, that this chapter is claimed by the ignorant among physicians and the common people, and by those who have never examined the books of the ancients on it, nor read a single letter from them.
For this reason, this art of knowledge has become extinct in our country, and I have never encountered anyone at all who is skilled in it. And I have only acquired what I have acquired from my long reading of the books of the ancients and my eagerness to understand them, until I extracted knowledge of it from them, then I adhered to experience and practice for my whole life.
And I have depicted for you in this chapter everything that my knowledge encompasses. And my experience has passed over it after I have brought it closer to you and freed it from the ramifications of prolixity, and I have shortened it to the utmost, and clarified it to the utmost, and I have depicted for you in it many images of the instruments that are used in it.
For it is from an increase in explanation, as I did in the preceding two chapters, and there is no strength except in God, the Most High, the Greatest.
The first chapter.
In it are summaries and compendia concerning the matter of fractures of bones, and it is necessary to present them before we begin to mention the broken and dislocated members one by one.  Therefore, it is necessary that we mention at the beginning of this chapter summaries of discourse and sections. It compels you first to understand them and stand upon their reality, you and others who are eager to learn this noble craft.
So I say that whenever a fracture, dislocation, wound, or fall occurs to someone, it is necessary to hasten first to bleed him or purge him, or both. Unless something prevents it, such as weakness of strength, or the one to whom this occurred is a child or a very old man, or the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then restrict his diet to cold vegetables and the meat of birds and gazelles, and forbid him drink, strong meats, and overfilling with food, and every food that fills the veins with blood, until the swelling is safe and the flow of material to the place is not expected.
Then let the patient return to his first regimen, which is his custom.  When the broken bone begins to knit, then the patient should eat foods that are very nourishing, strong, and thick, containing viscosity, such as porridges, rice, heads, and brains, and cow's paunches. And eggs, soft fish, and thick drinks, and the like, for by this regimen the consolidation of the fracture will be quicker and better. And know that broken bones, if they are in strong men and old men, cannot ever unite and join together in their first nature.
Because of the dryness of their bones and their hardness. And it may unite and join what was of bones extremely soft, like the bones of very small children. But nature produces on the broken bone from all its sides something resembling glue, thick, which adheres to it and tightens it until some of it adheres to some of it.
And binds some of it to some of it until it comes to the utmost strength and solidity as it was at first, until nothing hinders it from its actions.
For this reason, it is necessary to give the patient foods which contain solidity, viscosity, and thickness, as we said. And know that the fracture may differ in its types according to the difference of the members, for the fracture of the bone of the leg is different from the fracture of the bone of the head, and the fracture of the bone of the chest is different from the fracture of the bone of the back.
And so are all the other members, each differing from the other. And we shall come to the mention of each type of fracture explained in its chapter in detail apart from the others.  And the type of fracture of the bone may also differ in itself.
For it may be its fracture a shattering without fragments occurring in it, and its fracture may be along the length of the bone, and its fracture may have fragments and protrusions, separated and unseparated, and the fracture may be with a wound and perforation in the skin, and the fracture may be a slight crack.
And for each type there is a special method in its setting, as will be mentioned in detail in its place. And of what makes known a fracture of the bone is its crookedness and swelling and its appearance to the sense, and its roughness when you probe it with your hand.
Then when there is not in the place an apparent crookedness, nor roughness, nor do you feel upon touching the bone a disturbance, nor does the patient feel much pain, then there is not a fracture, but it may be a sprain or a slight fracture or a slight crack, so it does not behoove you to move it by extension and probing at all.
Rather apply to it of the medicaments the mention of which will come after a while, that which suits the place, then bind it gently. And know that if the bone is shattered and broken in two without fragments occurring in it, except that each part has moved away from its fellow.
Then it behooves you to hasten at once to its straightening and setting before a hot swelling occurs to it.  If a hot swelling occurs to it, then leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides, then set it in whatever way you are able of gentleness and skill.
And know that its setting and straightening is easier than a bone in which fragments have occurred. Then it is bound as will be mentioned. If there are fragments in the bone, then it is necessary to extend the broken limb from both sides, whether hand or foot.
Either with your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with rope and hand. And let your placing of the limb be on a level place according to its natural shape, until when the broken parts of the bone are extended, then return those fragments to their places in whatever way you are able of skill and gentleness.
And strive with all your effort that you do not cause the patient pain or suffering by your action, and strive with all your effort to join one of the bones to its fellow in the best manner. And it is necessary at that time that you touch and feel them with your hand; if you see there something contrary, correct and straighten it to the extent of your ability.
And beware of excessive extension and strong probing, as many ignorant people do, and they often cause by their action a hot swelling or stiffness in the limb, as I have witnessed repeatedly from their actions.
Then, after the setting, perfection, and binding, keep that limb still and quiet, and warn the patient not to move it while awake or asleep, and when he turns and fidgets, and when he has a bowel movement, and in all his movements to the best of his ability, and let him seek to have the position of the limb be a position that ensures safety from pain.
And that is because whenever he feels pain or suffering in the position of the limb, he should move it to another position in which he does not feel pain, and he should strive that that position is level and straight.
Lest crookedness occur in the limb if it is set, and as for the manner of binding the broken limb, it is as I describe to you. Know that broken limbs differ in their smallness and largeness and shapes.  And those of them that are small, like the arm, finger, forearm, and the like.
Then it is necessary that the bandages be soft and gentle; and those that are large, like the thigh, back, and chest, then it is necessary that the bandages be broad and firm, because a wide bandage is necessary for a large limb and binds it from every side with an equal binding so that no flaw interferes.
Then, after you have finished the setting, you begin to apply the appropriate plaster. With a soft, gentle pull on the site of the fracture, then you begin to wrap the bandage on the site of the fracture itself, three or four turns, according to what the limb requires, and you pull the bandage slightly with your hand, then you take it to the upper side of the fracture site.
With less tightening than you did to the fractured area, then you gradually spread the wrapping away from the fracture site and loosen the tightening little by little until you take something suitable from the correct position, then you take another bandage and wrap it also on the fractured area.
turns, then you take the wrapping to the lower side of the fracture, and let your action in tightening and loosening the wrapping be as we mentioned in the first upper wrapping, then you place between the bandages from the soft padding or cloths what evens out the crookedness of the fracture.
If there is crookedness, otherwise do not put anything in it, then wrap another bandage around it, then set the firm splints on these bandages immediately, if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, then apply what alleviates that swelling and removes the inflation and leave it for some days, then tighten the splints on it then, and let the splints be from broad, carved, skillfully prepared halves of reeds, or let the splints be from sieve wood.
which are made from pine or palm leaves or willow or boxwood and the like of what is available from that, and let the width of each splint be in this image and this shape precisely.
Except that the splint which is placed on the fracture itself should be a little thicker and broader than the other splints, and as for the length of the splint, it is made according to what suits the broken limb, in its size and smallness. Image of the splint:
Then tighten the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening precisely, then tie from above with firm threads according to what we mentioned of the tightening, which is that your tightening on the place of the fracture should be more. And the farther it is from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And the threads should be moderate in thickness and thinness, and let them be of wet linen especially, because if the threads are thick, as I have seen done by the ignorant, who make their threads from twisted linen strips, this is a great mistake.
Because the tightening with them falls outside of moderation, and thin threads are also unsuitable because you do not achieve with the tightening what you want, and there should not be less than a finger between the splint and the splint.
If the patient is harmed by the ends of the splints after tightening in the sound places, then place under them soft tow or fluffy wool so that nothing harms him from that. As for if the fracture is with a wound and a rupture occurs in the skin, we will mention that separately.
Know that not every broken limb should be tightened with splints from the first day. That is, if the limb is large, the splints should not be placed on it except after five or seven days or more, according to your assurance that hot swelling will not occur.
And this is a description of the bandages with which the ancients used to bind fractures, dislocations, and sprains. A description of a common, brief bandage that binds fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, because it is something that is not overcome by heat or cold.
And it is that you take from the dust of the mill, which is the essence of the flour that adheres to the walls of the mill during the movement of the mill, then you knead it as it is, without sifting it, with egg white, and make its kneading neither thick nor thin, then you apply it.
The description of another bandage that binds fractures, dislocations, and sprains. Take of mastic, leadwort, acacia, resin, gum tragacanth, and sugar, ten drachmas of each; and of myrrh and aloes, five drachmas of each; and of lote tree, twenty drachmas; and of Armenian or Roman clay, twenty drachmas. Pound everything together and mix it with lote tree water or with egg white if the patient's temperament is hot, then use this bandage, for it is good in composition, quickly binds broken bones, and is suitable for most people because of its moderation.
The description of another bandage which is also beneficial for fractures and sprains. Take gum tragacanth, mastic, and white lead, ten drachmas of each; myrrh and aloes, five drachmas of each; acacia, six drachmas; Armenian clay, twenty drachmas. Pound everything finely, sift it, and knead it with water or egg white and use it.
The description of a bandage for joints and bones that have shifted from their positions, and it soothes the pain occurring in them, and for bone cracks and fractures. Take carded wool and dip it in vinegar and cooked oil, and place it on the affected area. This bandage does not have the power to set.
But it is excellent in alleviating hot swelling and relieving pain especially. The description of another bandage that sets broken bones. Take the leaves of the deaf fig and the leaves of wild poppy, and pound them together and bandage with them while moist. The description of another brief bandage used when a bone fracture is setting.
And you want to resolve the remainder of the swelling, take of the root of white lead, chamomile, violet flowers, and starch, one part of each; pound everything together and knead it with egg white if the member is not inflamed; but if it is inflamed, then knead it with coriander water or with water and use it.
The description of another bandage stronger in resolution than the first, used when a hard swelling occurs when the bone is setting. Take of the root of white lead, flaxseed, fenugreek, rosemary, balm mint, violet flowers, and chamomile, one part of each.
Pound everything together and knead it with vinegar or with fresh water or with egg white, all according to the heat of the member and the stillness of its inflammation. As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that it should be placed on the broken member when it is set.
Except for the cerate made from wax and oil only, and he described it as being moderate between thickness and thinness. As for Galen, he saw that things that contain drying agents with something of heat, such as myrrh, aloes, and frankincense, and the like that we have mentioned, should be placed on the broken member when it is set.
The description of a bandage that is useful for weakness and pain. Take comfrey, fumitory, human hair, plucked, or bird feathers, and leadwort, and salt, equal parts. Pound, sift, and knead, and bandage with it. As for the amount that the bandage should remain...
Then loosen it.  You should observe: if no pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone from its place occurs in the afflicted area, do not loosen it for many days. If, however, severe itching or troubling pain or swelling occurs in the area...
then hasten to loosen it immediately and do not delay at all, and remove the bandage from it. Then take a soft cloth or a wet sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it until the itching and pain subside. Then leave the member to rest for an hour.
Then apply carded wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose oil, and bind it on for a night until you are safe from hot swelling, the swelling of the member subsides, and its pain goes away. Then return it to gentle tightening and a light bandage, and do not tighten it as tightly as the first time.
And treat it gently until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and puffiness, and all the symptoms have gone completely, and you need a bandage and tightening, then prepare it as you did initially, equally. If nothing of what we have mentioned occurs in the member...
then do not loosen it except after three, four, five, or seven days, and it may be left for twenty days; all this depends on what appears to you of the condition of the member, as we said, until the fracture sets and the flesh begins to join together on it.
Then also increase the tightening more than your first tightening, and also increase the nourishment of the patient beyond what was previously mentioned. If you see that the place of the fracture has dried up and become thinner than it should be, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.
Then bathe it with lukewarm water each time you loosen it, and let this be every three days, and lessen the tightening a little. For by this action nourishment will flow to the member, and it will heal quickly. As for what ignorant bone-setters do, of breaking the member again…
if it is not set properly at first, and is set crookedly, then it is a mistake in their action and a great deception. And if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and acted upon it, and I have seen no trace of it from any of them at all. and the correct thing is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
On accidental fractures in the head.
The types of accidental fractures in the head are many, their forms varied, and their causes diverse.  Some fractures are caused by a sword blow, and it may either completely sever the bone until it reaches the membrane beneath the bone. Just as the adze does in wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called adze-like. Or, the sword may cut some of the bone and sever only its surface, without the cut penetrating to the end; this type of fracture is called a complete avulsion, and the wound of these two fractures may be either large or small.
And there are fractures which are comminuted or contused; their cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall onto a stone or something similar. This fracture may be penetrating, nearly reaching the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be on the surface of the bone.
And the wound of these two fractures is also either large or small.  And there is a fracture that is hidden in the bone, as thin as a hair; it is a slight crack.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called hairline.  And there is a fracture caused by a fall or a blow with a stone or the like, which drives a part of the bone inwards, causing a depression in the area, just as happens to a copper pot when struck, causing a part of it to be driven inwards.
This most often occurs in the moist bones of the head, like the heads of children.  All these types of fractures may have loose or non-loose fragments. We shall describe the treatment of all of them in their proper place, and we shall recognize all these types of fracture by examining them.
And examining them with probes and removing the corrupt flesh from them. As for the hairline type, it is known by examining the bone, and rubbing and staining it with ink; then the fracture appears black. As for the treatment of the fracture, first look at the patient's symptoms.
If you see among his symptoms that which clearly indicates danger, such as vomiting bile, swelling, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, sunken and reddened eyes, and similar symptoms, then do not approach the patient or treat him, for death is inevitable in most cases with these symptoms.
If you see symptoms that do not frighten you and you hope for his recovery, then begin his treatment.  And if the wounded man comes to you immediately after his injury, and this is in the winter months,
you should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case. And if it is in the summer months, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane under the bone is corrupted and those symptoms we described appear.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain and there is shattering and bruising, then you should cut away the shattered and bruised part as I describe to you: that is, shave the head of the wounded patient.
And uncover the bone in whatever way is possible for you and according to the shape of the wound and what is easiest for the patient. If, when examining the bone, you find bleeding or hot swelling, then treat that as is appropriate.
That is, pack the area with cloths soaked in a syrup and rose oil, and clean the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding stops. Then begin to rasp away the bone and remove it; this can be done in one of two ways.
The first way is to cut the bone with a delicate saw with a narrow blade.  This is its image:
Then use another saw, slightly wider than the first. This is its image:
Then also use another saw, wider still than the second.  It is necessary that you have several different saws, some wider than others, and some shorter than others, and that they be extremely sharp at their edges, and let them be of Indian steel or good steel.
And use gentleness in striking the saw, lest you shake the head and injure it. If the bone is strong and hard, then it is necessary to perforate around it before using the saws with the drills which they call non-immersive drills; and they are called non-immersive drills.
Because they do not go beyond the limit of the skull bone to what is beyond it, for the drill has a round edge below its sharp head, similar to a ring or small circle, preventing it from sinking and going beyond the thickness of the bone.
And it is necessary for you to take many of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that you have for every skull a drill whose sharp end is, in length and shortness, suitable for the thickness of that skull.  And this is an image of three types. Of the drills: large, medium, and small.
As for the method of perforation around the fractured bone, it is that you place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then you move the drill to another place, and you make the distance between each hole the thickness of an awl or similar.
Then you cut between each two holes with the cutters, and you do this with the utmost gentleness you can, as we said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with some other instrument prepared for that.
Such as the rasps and delicate forceps. And it is necessary that you be very careful that the drill or cutter does not touch anything of the peritoneum.  If the bone is removed and is free from the peritoneum, if it was attached to it, and you have extracted it, then it is necessary to cleanse it.
And smooth the roughness remaining in the bone with another instrument similar to the cutter, except that it should be finer and more delicate than the other cutters. If any small bones or splinters remain, take them gently with the instruments you have.
Then you treat the wound afterwards with lint and ointments which will be mentioned. As for the other method of operation, it is a very easy method, far from danger, mentioned and highly praised by Galen. And this is what he says. First you should begin by exposing the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and obvious, until, when you have exposed that part, you place the tip of this lenticular cutter underneath it, and this is its image:
The lenticular part of it should be smooth so that it does not cut anything, and the sharp part of it is on its sides extending lengthwise, as you see, so that the lenticular part rests against the peritoneum and the face of the sharp cutter against the bone; then strike the cutter from one side.
