With a small hammer until you cut all the bone gently as it rotates, and you are safe from the membrane, so that nothing frightening happens to it at all, even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he were sleepy.
If any part of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some parts of the bone, scrape it off with the tip of the lenticular cutter itself and carefully free it from it, for it will be freed from it without harm or fear.
But if the fracture of the bone did not penetrate to the membrane, and what was broken from the bone was its surface, and roughness and small fragments remained in it, then you should remove that roughness and extract those fragments with fine forceps, a number of which have been made of different sizes so that you may use in each place that which is most suitable and appropriate according to what the work leads you to.
And the broken bone itself and its shape, and you should use first in your scraping of the bone the widest of those forceps, then use after it a finer one, and continue to do that successively until you come to use the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for the other small hairline cracks and slight fractures, you should use for each one a treatment according to what leads to its healing, which is something that is not hidden from anyone who has the least experience in this art and has studied what we have written and explained concerning large fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you remove the bone, you should take a linen cloth the size of the wound and dip it in rose oil and place it on the mouth of the wound, then take another folded or triangular cloth. And dip it in syrup and rose oil and place it on the first cloth and do this as gently as you can so as not to burden the peritoneum, then use a wide bandage on top and do not tighten it except to the extent that it only holds the wound.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are certain the hot swelling has subsided, then unwrap it and use afterwards medicines which have a drying quality, such as the roots of iris, and powdered cyclamen, and powdered spikenard, and ziraund and the like; and that is that you make from these medicines a powder.
And sprinkle it on the wound as it is dry; and in general, use in that a medicine which cleanses and does not sting, and strive with all your effort during your treatment of the wound that it be clean, without grease or dirt, and do not let pus collect in it at all.
Because if pus collects on the membrane of the brain it corrupts and spoils it, and from that happens to the patient a great calamity; and it may happen in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if one is negligent in its treatment.
Blackness on its surface; if the symptoms we mentioned appear to the patient, then know that he is inevitably doomed.  And if the blackness only occurred from a medicine which was applied, and it was in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness.
It is necessary that you take one part honey and three parts rose oil, and mix them well, and smear them on a cloth, then place it on the peritoneum, then treat it with the various treatments which are necessary until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the setting of a broken nose.
Know that there is no fracture of the nose except of both its upper parts, or one of them, because they are bones, for the lower part of it is cartilaginous and does not fracture, but rather it is subject to contusion, crookedness, and flattening.
If one of its parts is fractured, then it is necessary that you insert the little finger into the nostril and set that fracture from the inside with your index finger and thumb from the outside until you return the nose to its natural shape.  Let this be done gently by you, and be careful that your action does not cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and the finger cannot reach it, then it should be set with the end of a probe of slight thickness. If the fracture is on both sides, then do likewise, and let him hasten to set it on the first day of the fracture.
If possible, otherwise after seven or ten days, when the hot swelling has subsided, then insert into the nostril a pledget of linen cloth if the fracture is on one side, or insert two pledgets if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the pledgets be of a thickness to fill the nostril.  Some of the early bone-setters mentioned that the pledgets should be moistened with fat and changed every day, but I do not think this is the case; rather, the pledgets should be moistened with egg white mixed with powdered millstone.
Then leave the pledgets until the bone sets and the cartilage hardens.  Goose quills, after having been wrapped with soft cloths, may be inserted into the nose at the location of the pledgets; this will make the retention of the nasal fracture firmer and will prevent the patient from being unable to breathe.
This is not necessary; if you wish, you may do it, and if you wish, you may make the pledgets. If a hot swelling should appear in the nose during your work, apply a compress to the nose with  kyroton or with cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil or something of diachylon ointment.
If no hot swelling appears, it is necessary to dress it externally with fine semolina and powdered myrrh mixed with egg white, then place a soft bandage over it, and do not bind the nose at all, if the nasal bones are fractured in small pieces.
Or if it is fragmented, it is necessary to incise it and extract the fragments with the appropriate instrument, then suture the incision and treat it with suitable ointments that heal and cicatrize.  If a wound occurs inside the nose, it is necessary to treat it with pledgets and use lead tubes. until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On the setting of the lower jaw if it is fractured.
If the lower jaw is fractured and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, if the fracture is only external and it is not fractured into two pieces and is not depressed inwards, then its identification is easy.
It is necessary, if the fracture is in the right side, that you insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth, and similarly if the fracture is in the left side, you insert the index finger of the right hand and gently lift the edge of the fracture from the inside to the outside.
And with your other hand from outside the bone, you control it to straighten it. If the fracture of the jaw has been broken into two, then it is necessary to use traction from both sides in a straight line until you are able to straighten it. If it has occurred in the teeth, a protrusion.
Or if they are separated, then tie what you hope will remain with a gold, silver or silk thread, then place on the fractured jaw a splint, then place over it a folded cloth, and place over the cloth a firm large splint or a piece of leather the length of the jaw. Then tie it from above according to what is convenient for you to tie and matches its closure so that it does not become undone, and you order the patient to rest and be still, and you make his food soft soups. If you think that something has changed in its appearance in any way, then hasten to untie it on the third day.
Then tie it from above according to what is convenient for you to tie and matches its closure so that it does not become undone, and you order the patient to rest and be still, and you make his food soft soups. If you think that something has changed in its appearance in any way, then hasten to untie it on the third day. Then you repair what you have bandaged with mill dust mixed with egg white or with semolina flour after removing the splint from it, and you place a soft pad over the bandage. As long as that bandage adheres to it and the condition of the bone does not change, leave it.
Do not untie it until it heals and the fracture is strong, for often this fracture becomes strong in three weeks. If a hot swelling appears during that time, use what we have repeatedly mentioned to soothe it until that swelling goes away.
And as for if the fracture is with a wound, you look; if a splinter or splinters have separated from the bone, be gentle in removing those splinters with whatever instrument is suitable for you to remove them.  If the mouth of the wound is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as much as you need.
Then, when you have removed those splinters and nothing remains of them, stitch the mouth of the wound if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for that. And stitch the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the setting of the clavicle if it is fractured.
The clavicle is most often fractured in front, at the end of the shoulder, and its fracture is of three types: either it is broken into two without splinters, which is the easiest to set;
or splinters occur in the fracture, which is more difficult to set; or the fracture is accompanied by a wound.  The procedure, if the fracture is without a wound, is that you prepare two assistants, one to hold the arm adjacent to the fractured clavicle, and the other to extend the neck towards the other side.
Then you set the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, and there is no prominence or irregularity. If you need more extension, you should place a ball of rags or wool under the patient's armpit.
The size of the ball should suit your needs. You extend and raise the clavicle and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is set as it should be. If you are unable to pull the end of the clavicle outwards because it has gone inwards, then the patient should lie on his back.
A moderately sized pillow is placed under his shoulder, and the assistant presses his shoulder downwards until the clavicle that is deep rises upwards. Then set the fracture and align it with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the clavicle has broken off and is moving, you should incise over it and remove that splinter gently. If the splinter is embedded in the bone, try to cut it with one of the instruments prepared for this purpose. After placing under the clavicle the instrument that protects the peritoneum, which is an instrument of wood or iron, and this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without a bowl. Its width should be according to the size of the bone, large or small, and its length should also be according to what you can manage for the work. Let it be double-ended, as you see, one end wider and the other narrower.
If the wound opening that you made when removing the bone splinter is wide and you are not concerned about hot inflammation, then join the edges of the wound with sutures. If the opening is small, or you fear hot inflammation, then treat the wound with lint and pledgets according to the size of the incision.
If hot inflammation develops, moisten the lint in rose oil and vinegar or wine and apply it. As for setting a bone that is not fractured or incised, this is done by applying to the bone a bandage made from mill dust with egg white. And you place a soft pad over it, then you place a ball under his armpit if he needs it. Then you take a bandage that is very long, its width being a cubit or so, then apply a folded pledget over the pad and bandage, then take a splint of thin wood. Let its width be three fingers and its length likewise. Then wrap it in a cloth, then wrap that cloth with the splint in the part of the bandage that lies on the fracture site, then tighten the bandage on the fracture as you wrap it. And pass it over his neck and under his sound armpit and under his injured armpit, and pass it repeatedly on each side, and however you see that the tightening secures the fracture firmly, which is not hidden from you.
And the aim of all this is that the splint does not move from the fractured bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day; whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the splint has moved, correct it and tighten the bandage. Then let the patient sleep on his back, and place a small pillow under his armpit when he sleeps at night.
So that his arm is raised from his side, and the clavicle fracture is raised with the elevation of the shoulder, or tie his arm to his neck. And do not untie the bandage unless an incident occurs in the area, such as itching or swelling, until twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a reason for it. Then return to the binding and leave it until the clavicle fracture heals and consolidates, and it strengthens and becomes firmer; this takes about twenty-eight days. And in some people, it may be less than that.
Chapter Six.
Concerning the setting of a fractured humerus. The humerus rarely fractures in its wide part; rather, it fractures at its extremities.  When a part of it is fractured, or it is fractured in its middle, this is known by touch.  So, according to the shape of the fracture, treat accordingly.
Set it and return it to its natural shape by any means you can. Then apply to the area mill dust with egg white and soft tow. Place over this a compress of folded cloth, and then apply a broad splint of wood.
Thin enough to cover the whole shoulder or slightly wider. If, under the splint, there is a prominence in any part of the shoulder, smooth that prominence with soft tow so that the splint lies evenly, then bind it tightly from above with a long bandage.
Bind it firmly and make sure the splint does not move from its place. Check the bandage every day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it moves from its place. Let the patient lie on his sound side. The shoulder heals in twenty [days].
days or twenty-five days. When this time is complete, remove the bandage, and you are safe, for it is among the bones which one does not fear will be crushed or broken.  If a splinter of bone protrudes and is irritating under the skin, incise over it and remove it. And do what was mentioned in the fracture of the clavicle, to calm the hot swelling if anything of that happens.
Chapter Seven.
On the setting of a fracture of the chest.
The chest may be fractured in its middle, and this rarely happens; but its extremities are more commonly fractured and shattered.  Among its symptoms, if its middle is fractured, is that it inclines downwards, and there is intense pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing.
And perhaps blood is discharged, and the depression in the fractured bone is apparent and not hidden from touch.  Its treatment is that the patient lies on his back, and a pillow is placed between his shoulders; then his shoulders are compressed, and the ribs are brought together by hands from the sides, and gentleness is used in smoothing it in every way possible.
And continue until the shape of the bone is restored as it should be; then apply a bandage and a splint, and place over it a splint of thin wood from willow or elm, or something similar that is light, after wrapping it in a cloth. Then gently bind it to the fractured bone lest it shift; and pass the bandage around to the back several times and tighten it firmly; then examine the bandage at all times, and whenever it loosens, tighten it.
And if necessity dictates its removal because of suppuration that appears in the area, or pain, or swelling, then hasten to remove it, take off the bandage, and treat whatever has appeared, according to its treatment; then reapply the bandage if you see fit. And his face, and bind him firmly until he heals.
Chapter Eight.
Concerning the treatment of fractured ribs. Know that fractures of the ribs occur in the thick parts that are next to the back, and their ends in front;  they are subject to injury because they are cartilaginous, and this is easily recognized by palpation with the fingers.
And its treatment is that you set the fracture with your fingers in the accessible way until the shape is properly aligned, then you bandage and bind the fractured bone with a splint if it needs it. If the rib fracture is angled inwards, then the patient experiences severe pain.
And it [the fractured rib] pushes inward, like the inward displacement that occurs in those with a punctured lung, because the bone pushes against the diaphragm; and it also causes shortness of breath, coughing and the expulsion of much blood. This is difficult to treat, and the ancients devised many stratagems for it.
And some of them said that it is necessary to give the patient foods that produce flatulence and wind so that the abdomen becomes distended and expands, and the fracture is pushed outwards.  We dislike this, lest it confirm the occurrence of hot inflammation if it has not yet occurred; and if it has occurred, then it increases and confirms it.
And some said that a cupping glass should be placed on the area, then strongly sucked; and this is more similar to reason, except that it is feared that the cupping glass will draw humors to the area because of its weakness. And some said that the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
And make a pad between the ribs until it is full, so that the bandage is even when you wrap it around; then treat the patient with the treatment for a punctured lung, in terms of diet and medicine. If the patient is overwhelmed by a severe condition that he cannot bear, and the bone pushed against the diaphragm in a harmful way, and we feared for the patient; therefore, it is necessary to make an incision in the area and expose the fractured rib, then place under it the instrument that protects the peritoneum, which has been previously described, and gently break the bone and remove it.
Then we bring together the edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot inflammation appears in the meantime, then act quickly: wet pads in rose oil and place them on the area, and treat the patient internally with things that calm the inflammation as well.
And he should lie on the side on which sleep comes easiest to him until he recovers.
Chapter Nine.
On the setting of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if a fracture affects them—and this rarely happens; more often they suffer contusion—and similarly the vertebrae of the back as well, if this happens to someone and you want to know whether he will recover or not, look: if you see that his hands have become relaxed, numb, and dead, and he is unable to move them, extend them, or flex them, and if you pinch them or prick them with a needle he does not feel it and finds no pain in them, then know that in most instances he will not recover; he is dying.
But if he moves them and feels the pinch and prick, then know that the marrow of the bone is safe and the patient will recover with treatment. If the vertebrae of the back are similarly affected and you want to know whether he will recover or not,
look at his legs: if you see that they have become relaxed and what happened in the hands has happened in them, then if he lies on his back, wind and excrement come out involuntarily, and if he lies on his stomach, urine comes out involuntarily, and if he lies on his back and wants to urinate, he is unable to do so, then know that he is dying; do not bother with his treatment. If none of this happens to him, the matter is less serious. The treatment of what has happened is to try to alleviate the hot swelling.
by placing on the affected vertebra rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks; apply this three times a day until the hot swelling subsides. Then apply one of the strengthening, drying compresses to the area and bind it tightly, and instruct him to remain still and quiet.
And let him sleep only on the side where he feels no pain until he recovers. If a splinter or something else has become detached from the bone at the time of the injury, then it is necessary to incise the skin and remove that bone. Then, if the wound is large, bring the edges together with sutures, then treat with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx, which is the tailbone, is broken, then it is necessary to insert the thumb of the left hand into the rectum.
and set the broken bone with the other hand as far as possible and as the setting allows, then apply the compress and splint if necessary, and then bind it.
If you feel a broken splinter in it, then incise it and remove it, and treat the wound with the previously mentioned treatment until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a fractured hip.
Hip bones rarely fracture; but if they do fracture, it is usually in such a way that their extremities are shattered, they split lengthwise, and they are displaced inwards.  The patient experiences pain in the affected area, weakness, and numbness in the leg on the side of the fracture.
Its treatment is that you pass your hand over it until you ascertain the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only in the extremities, then set that fracture as best you can, until it resembles its natural form.
If the fracture is lengthwise or if it has been displaced inwards, lay the patient on his stomach until you are ready to set the fracture.  When you have set it, apply a bandage, then place a splint of wood or leather over it, and bind it tightly.
Do not fear that the fracture will shift or the splint will come loose.  Fill in any hollows with padding so that the binding is even. Instruct the patient to lie on his back or on his sound side. If he develops a hot inflammation, ...
...cease manipulation and treatment until the hot inflammation subsides. Apply whatever remedies will alleviate it, as described above. Then return to setting and binding it as is proper. If there are fragments or shattered pieces of bone, they should not be removed.
They should not be touched but rather smoothed from the outside, as we have said, and the binding should be left in place until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the treatment of a fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is that which is between the elbow and the shoulder. If it is fractured, treat it in one of two ways. One is to take a smooth, curved piece of wood of moderate thickness, in this manner:
And you tie two bandages to its ends, then it is hung from a high place, and the patient sits on a chair, then his broken arm is placed on the wood until his armpit is touching the middle of the curve of the wood, then something heavy is hung from above it.
Or a servant stretches it downwards, then the physician sets the fracture with his two hands until he returns the fracture to what is appropriate. The other method is that the patient lies on his back and his hand is hung from his neck with a bandage.
Then you order two servants, one to hold above the fracture with his hands and the other to hold below it, and each stretches to his side. And if you wish the stretching to be stronger, then tie below the fracture with a bandage and above it with a bandage, and each of the servants stretches to his side.
If the fracture is near the end of the shoulder, then the middle of the bandage should be under the armpit and the other under the fracture towards the elbow.  Likewise, if the fracture is near the elbow, then the bandage should be in that place and on the elbow itself.
Then set the fracture gently without violence, until the fracture is set as it should be and unites well. Then bandage it if no hot swelling appears. If a hot swelling appears, then leave bandaging until the seventh day and apply a moistened, soft cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then bandage it. The method of bandaging is to apply the compress to the fracture, then apply a bandage of new cloth over the compress, then bring the forearm against the upper arm itself and place his open hand on his shoulder.
And apply the cloth and bandage to the upper arm and forearm so that the forearm acts as a splint, if nothing prevents you from doing so and nothing changes in the broken bone. If you fear that something will change, then use splints.
And it is that you place on the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints, and let there be the width of a finger between each splint, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture, with an addition of three fingers on each side.
Then tighten the splints with the tightening I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And that your tightening on the place of the fracture be stronger.
And the further from the fracture, the less the tightening. If you see that the placing of the splints and the tightening are as we said when you set the limb at once, then do so.
And if you fear a hot swelling, leave off the tightening and the splints until the seventh day, as we said. Then examine the bandage every three days, lest there occur at the place chafing or swelling, or the nourishment be prevented from reaching the limb because of excessive tightening.  Correct all this as we mentioned.
But if you are confident that nothing of that sort will happen, do not loosen the bandage except after many days. And let the patient lie on his back, with his hand on his stomach, and a cushion filled with wool, of moderate size, be placed under the arm.
And examine at every time, of night or day, lest the shape of the broken limb be disarranged, or the bandage become slack.  Correct all this with your effort, and let the patient's diet be according to what we have already stated; that is, that the food should be light at first, until the bone begins to consolidate.
Then it is necessary to make his food more substantial. For it is the nature of the arm and leg to consolidate in forty days. Then it is necessary to loosen [the bandage], and he should use the bath and be treated with the ointments suitable for this. If the fracture is severe and comminuted,
do not loosen the bandage from him until fifty days or two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On the setting of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called the ulna; one is small, and it is that which is next to the thumb, and the other is large, and it is situated beneath the small one from below.  Therefore, perhaps the larger ulna is fractured alone, or the smaller one alone, and perhaps both are fractured together.
When the smaller upper ulna is fractured, then its setting is easy and its healing is quicker. When the lower ulna is fractured, its fracture is bad and its healing difficult. And the worst is if both bones are fractured. If the bone which is fractured is the smaller upper ulna,
it is necessary for the physician, when setting it, to make its extension easy and gentle until he straightens it. If the large ulna is that which is fractured, then it is necessary to make its extension stronger. And if both ulnas are fractured, then it is necessary to make the extension very much stronger.
And it is necessary that the shape of the hand, when it is set and extended, be extended upon a cushion, and the thumb of the hand should be higher than all the fingers, and the little finger should be lower than the other fingers, and the patient should be sitting squarely upon himself.
And let the cushion be opposite him in height, lest the patient suffer difficulty.
Then a servant extends the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant extends from above likewise. Then the physician straightens the bone until he returns it to the best shape he can. If there are fragments in the fracture of the bone, then they are removed.
Return each fragment to its place to the best of your ability. If a fragment appears that is separated and is piercing the skin, and there is no hope of setting it, then incise upon it and extract it in the manner which we have mentioned previously. If the fracture is with a wound, then a chapter has been devoted to it.
Then take the treatment of that from there. If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of your setting, then apply a cloth with prepared kypros, rose oil, and white wax. Let it be moderate between thickness and thinness, and bind the cloth upon it gently until the swelling subsides.
Then remove the kypros and apply the prepared bandage of mill dust with egg white. Then apply the splints. Let the splint which is placed on the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger. And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most instances.
Whether the fracture was in one forearm bone or in both forearm bones together, then make your binding on the place of the fracture stronger and tighter. And as you proceed with the binding upwards or downwards, make the binding somewhat looser, as was mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, and not very hard. And let the thread with which it is bound be of special linen, moderate between thinness and thickness, as we have described. And examine the limb and bandage after a few days.
If something happens that needs to be corrected, such as itching appearing in the limb, then it should be bathed with warm water until that itching subsides. And the limb should be left unbound for a night so that it may rest, then the binding should be resumed.
If the binding has loosened and the bone has shifted or something like that, then repair all of that to the best of your ability. And look also, if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive binding, then it should be loosened a little and left for some days until nourishment reaches it, then it should be bound.
If nothing of what we have mentioned occurs to the patient, then it should not be unbound except after twenty days or so. Then, suspend the patient's hand to his neck, and let his arm be straight.  He should carefully avoid restless movements, and he should sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture of the arm heals in thirty days, or in thirty-two, and perhaps it heals in twenty-eight days; all that depends on the conditions of the temperaments and conditions of strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the setting of a fracture of the hand and fingers.
Fractures rarely occur in the metacarpus and phalanges of the fingers; rather, contusions occur frequently.  So when a fracture or contusion occurs in the hand…
The patient should sit cross-legged, with a chair directly in front, level with him.
Then he should place his hand on it outstretched. Then the bonesmith should extend the broken bones, and the physician should straighten them until they are well-set. Then a bandage and splint should be applied, if there is no hot swelling, and then a splint should be applied over this, according to the location.
And it has been included in a soft rag.  If the fracture is towards the lower part, towards the palm of the hand, then make a kind of ball from a rag and instruct the patient to grasp it with his broken hand, then tighten it with a long rag.
Let the splint be of leather, soft enough to conform to the skin with the flexion of the whole hand, and tighten it as needed. If the fracture is outward, then the splint should be placed above and another splint below in the hand so that the hand is open and erect.
Then apply the bandage as the hand rotates and interweave it between the fingers. If the fracture is in one of the finger bones, if it is the thumb, straighten it as needed, then bind it with the hand. And if you wish, make a small, upright splint to support the fracture.
And it should not move. If the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle, index, little, or ring finger, then straighten them and bind them with the adjacent healthy finger, or bind them all together, which is better, or place a small, upright splint on them.
As we said for the thumb, and examine during and after your setting for hot inflammation; counteract it appropriately should anything of that occur, as described repeatedly.
Chapter Fourteen.
On setting a fracture of the thigh.
The thigh bone frequently breaks and is perceptible to the touch because it moves forward and backward.  Its setting is by tightening a bandage above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, the patient lying on his stomach.
Then extend each bandage evenly to its side; this if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. But if it is near the head of the thigh, then it is necessary to tighten a soft bandage, either of wool or similar.
At the head of the thigh towards the groin so that the extension is upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture. Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, let the bandage be near the knee so that the extension is downwards. Then the physician straightens the bone with both hands until he restores it to its natural form.
And the bone unites well. Then it is necessary to maintain the bandage and tightening, unless a hot inflammation occurs in the limb. If inflammation occurs, leave it for a few days until the hot inflammation subsides, then return to its treatment.
As for tightening it, it is necessary to wrap a firm, wide turban twice or three times around the fracture, leaving the excess. Then twist the leg until the heel is at the origin of the buttock, and insert a long thread between the thigh and leg near the knee from below, the ends of the thread being above on both sides.
Then wrap the excess of the turban around the leg and thigh. Then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture itself, and place one splint on the leg bone. Then fill the space between the thigh and leg with soft rags so that the tightening is even.
Then begin strong tightening from the middle at the site of the fracture, three or four turns.  The farther the bandage is from the site of the fracture, the less strong, softer, and looser should be your tightening.
Then take the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie with it the splints above. Then pass the ends of the thread downwards until you reach the heel of the foot, and also tighten with them the ends of the splints on the other side.
So that the bandage does not move from its place. Then leave the tightening as it is, as long as no pain, swelling, or puffiness, etc., occurs in the limb. If any of this occurs, immediately untie it and correct whatever occurred, in the manner we have repeatedly mentioned.
If there is a protruding bone splinter, it should be straightened if possible. Otherwise, make an incision over it, remove it, and treat the wound as previously described until it heals.  This fracture of the thigh may also be set without adding the leg with splints.
As we mentioned in the case of the humerus and forearm, except that setting it this way does not result in lameness for the patient. However, if it is set alone, without including the leg, the patient will inevitably limp.
Know that the thigh heals in fifty days, or a little more or less, all this depending on the differences in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the fracture of the kneecap.
Fracture of the kneecap is rare, but it is frequently contused. If it is fractured, it is either a crack or a shattering into pieces, and this occurs with or without a wound.
You will ascertain all this by touch, and you will set it by straightening the separated parts with your fingers until they are joined and united, as far as you can manage with gentleness and precision; then apply a bandage and a circular splint, if necessary.
Then tighten the appropriate bandage above, and treat all the conditions we described in other fractures, such as hot swelling and the like, by counteracting each symptom with its appropriate treatment until healing occurs.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the setting of fractures of the leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the fibula;  they are subject to the same types of fracture as the bones of the arm. Therefore, its setting is the same as that of the arm, and the procedure is identical. If both bones are fractured, the leg is displaced in all directions; if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg is displaced forward; and if the thicker bone is fractured, and this occurs lower down, then this is something that is not hidden from you.
Therefore, use extension and reduction, and apply splints accordingly, except that if the fracture of the leg is a severe one with many fragments, the extension should be less and gentler, and you should take great care in setting it.
And in the case of the leg, there is something in addition to what is done for the arm:  when you have applied the splints and completed all your work, take two small pieces of pine wood, such as is used in planing rooms, and which is placed between the cracks in the planks, or [take pieces] from date palm leaflets or something similar.
Choose those of moderate thickness, not thin ones, and let their length be that of the leg from the knee downwards; then wrap each one twice lengthwise with a cloth, and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the other side.
Let them extend from the knee to the lower part of the foot; then tie the two small pieces of wood in three places, at the ends and in the middle. By this means, the leg is prevented from tilting to the right or left, and is held firmly and well. A wooden gutter along the length of the leg may also be used.
It is placed in it to keep it from moving, and this is especially necessary in fractures with a wound. Then examine the leg every day, and give it the greatest care with regard to swelling, inflammation, or anything else. If anything of this sort occurs, counteract it as necessary until it heals. The bone of the leg knits in about thirty days or so.
Chapter Seventeen.
On fractures of the bones of the leg and toes.
As for the heel, fracture never occurs there; as for the bones of the leg, fracture may occur to them; and as for the toes also, fracture rarely occurs to them; rather, contusion occurs to them in most instances.
If a fracture occurs to the bones of the leg and you see that those bones have overlapped, then let the patient place his foot on the ground upright, as if walking; then you stand and place your foot on what is elevated of those bones, then tread on it and straighten it.
until they return to their places; then apply a bandage and splint from above, and place a small board with two flat ends under the sole of the foot; then bind the foot firmly after wrapping it with cloths and everything else you need.
Then, if three or four days pass, untie the bandage, for you will find the bones straight, whether they were fractured or dislocated; the procedure is as you see. As for if some of the toes are fractured, set them and straighten them according to what I described to you in setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the fractured toe along the length of the toe, and let it be a little wider than it; then place under the foot the board that I described to you and bind it firmly; if what is fractured of the bones is two or three or more.
Then make for each toe a splint of its size, inserted into a soft cloth, and bind the board to the bottom of the foot, and let each end of the board rest outside the sole of the foot to secure it well.
And you should not forget to attend to all that we mentioned to you in other fractures from the symptoms that we mentioned, and treat each symptom as it should be.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of the woman's pubic bone and the man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, seat her cross-legged, then incline her slightly backward, and let her be held from behind, then let the midwife pack her vulva with cotton until it fills it and becomes like a ball in her vulva.
Then the woman is shaken and her loins are raised little by little; then that cotton will come out until it is at the opening of the vulva like a ball, and the fracture of the bone will return. Then a pad is placed on her back. When she wants to urinate, the cotton is gently removed so that she can urinate.
And it is returned in the manner in which it was first inserted, then she returns to her original position. She does this for seven days or so, then it will heal. And if you wish, you may take a sheep's bladder, tie a tube to its mouth, and insert the whole bladder into her vulva.
Then blow forcefully into the tube until the bladder inflates inside the vulva; then the fracture will return. Then pack it with cotton and remain as described until it heals. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, this is used in its setting.
And its setting is as we described for the hip bone.  The correct procedure for these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, especially for someone with some experience and who understands this book well, is not hidden from you, for the fracture itself will guide you in most cases.
Understand the correct way to set and bind it. As for a man's penis if it is fractured, take a goose's gullet, insert the penis into it, then place a bandage of cloth over it and bind it, and leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the setting of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
Whoever suffers a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large, like the thigh bone or the humerus or similar, should immediately bleed him if the conditions for bleeding, as we have previously explained, allow.
If the wound is bleeding, you should immediately stop it by applying powdered vitriol if nothing else is available. Then set the fracture on that same day and do not delay it.
If no hot swelling occurs, then if hot swelling occurs, delay setting it until the ninth day, until the hot swelling subsides, and do not touch it at all on the third and fourth days, for bad symptoms will result.
If the fractured bone is protruding through the skin and exposed, you should try to gently reposition and straighten it with your hands, using a slight pull. If you cannot reposition and straighten it with your hands, reposition it with this instrument, which is an iron instrument about seven or eight fingers long. And its width should be according to the wound.
Therefore, the physician should have three or four of them, according to what he needs for treatment in each type of fracture, and let them be rounded with slight thickness, lest they bend when pressed during use.
And let the end be sharp, with a curve at its tip, and let the upper part be thicker, and from its middle to its lower part, much thinner.  This is its image:
And it is called in Greek *peron*, meaning a small lever.  The sharp, curved end should be placed on the end of the protruding bone, and it should be pushed with it gently until the bone returns and is somewhat straightened, then straighten the ends of the fracture against each other.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grasp it well, then the end of that bone should be cut until the instrument can grasp it. If you are not able to reduce the bone as we described at all, then cut it with a similar cut from those we mentioned.
Or saw it with one of the saws, however you can, then remove any roughness and thin scales remaining on the bone. If, after reduction, the patient feels severe, painful throbbing, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
If you are able to return it to its natural place, then do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient. When you have completed setting the bone, dip a cloth in a black astringent drink, especially if it is summer, and do not apply any ointment or anything greasy to the wound.
Lest putrefaction and corruption occur in it. Then, when you have finished setting the bone, use splints and leave the wound uncovered by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors, the size of the wound.
And beware, most carefully, of tightening the wound with the fracture, for many ignorant physicians have done this, causing their patients either death or gangrene or tetanus.  Let your binding be soft and loose, unlike the binding of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or a large wound, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, and he feels troubling pain in the place, then you should not apply splints; instead, make bandages of firm cloths in the place of the splints, and bind it with them.
If after a day or two you see that pus is beginning to form in the wound, remove the cloth that you placed on it with the drink, then use lint and ointments with which we are accustomed to treat wounds, such as the tetrapharmacon and the like, and you should loosen the bandage.
And examine the wound every day, morning and evening, until it heals and closes.  The limb should be positioned so that the pus flows down easily. If many days pass and the wound does not heal and the pus does not stop, then know that there are small fragments of bone.
Then you should explore the wound with a probe; whatever of those fragments is loose, remove and extract it; and whatever is not loose, and is piercing the limb and causing pain, then aim to cut and extract it in any way you can.
If the wound suffers from inflammation, or gangrene, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction, then you should treat each symptom with its corresponding treatment, as previously described in its chapter.
What you should pay attention to and keep in mind is that if a large bone is broken and protrudes from a limb, such as the thigh bone, the humerus, and similar large limbs, do not attempt to pull or extract it. For death often results from such an action; rather, leave it until it putrefies, for perhaps it will fall out on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place for treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
In the treatment of complications that occur after some fractures.
This complication often occurs after the healing of a fracture, especially those near the joints, making the limb unsightly and perhaps preventing it from its natural function.
Examine it; if the complication is fresh, use astringent drugs such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, gum mastic, acacia, and the like. Take some or all of these and mix them with an astringent syrup, egg white, or vinegar.
Apply this to the complication with a bandage and bind it tightly. Leave the bandage undisturbed for many days, then unbind it and reapply another until the complication disappears, God willing, or fasten a tight lead plate to it.
Lead has the property of removing all that is superfluous in the limbs. If the complication has hardened and become severe, and necessity dictates its removal, then incise it from above and cut away the excess growth, or shave it off with some instrument until it is gone, and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On the treatment of a fracture if it heals and the limb remains thin and unlike its original nature.
If a bone fracture heals and the limb remains thin and weak, this is due to many causes. One cause is excessive loosening and tightening of the bandages, or excessive tightening of the bandages preventing nourishment from reaching the limb, or excessive and repeated rubbing, or excessive movement at the wrong time, or a deficiency of blood and weakness in the patient's body.
The treatment of this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body so that his blood increases, and to use baths and to make him happy and joyful and similar things, then to apply pitch to the limb so that the pitch draws much nourishment to it, or to repeatedly rub it with lukewarm water. until nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural shape.
Chapter Twenty-two.
In the treatment of fractured bones which have been set crookedly and prevented from functioning properly.
If, after a limb has healed, a crookedness or a projection of the fractured bone, or a complication appears, and the appearance of the limb is thereby disfigured. Unless the limb is prevented from its natural function, then one should not accept the opinion of those who claim that the limb should be broken from the head, as many ignorant physicians and bone-setters in our country have done.
And this action is utterly reprehensible, leading to great harm, the least of which is disability; but if the crookedness and complication are recent, then it should be bathed in water in which emollient herbs such as mallow leaves and root, and vervain, and the like have been boiled.
And it should be bandaged with emollient bandages such as well-made diachylon, or the sap of mallow root should be taken and beaten with chicken fat and butter, and bandaged with it; or ripe figs should be taken and pounded with pigeon dung and similar medicines which are called inhibitors of consolidation.
And sometimes the complication will be resolved by constant gentle massage with the hands, and the movement of the limb in every direction at all times should be used; but if the crookedness is old and severe and has become hardened, and treatment with iron is necessary.
Then it should be incised above and the connection of the bone released, and the excess of the complication or bone should be cut with delicate cuts, and gentleness should be used with effort and care; then the wound should be treated as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
A dislocation is the displacement of a joint from its place, which hinders movement, disfigures the limb, and causes the patient severe pain and suffering.  If a dislocation occurs to someone, then he should immediately proceed to reduce it.
He should not delay at all, for if he delays, the place will swell and become inflamed, making it difficult to reduce the dislocation. Therefore, it should not be delayed, nor moved, nor stretched while it is swollen, because it often causes the patient spasms and harmful pains.
But if that happens, he should immediately proceed to bleed the patient, then leave him until the swelling subsides somewhat, then bathe the limb with warm water and oil, then gently reduce it, and treat each limb as mentioned in its place.
And I have also arranged the chapters on dislocations according to what preceded in fractures, from the upper part of the body to the lower.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws rarely become dislocated, and their dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced from their positions, so they relax slightly, or they are completely dislocated so that they relax completely. towards the chest, so that the saliva of the patient flows and he cannot retain it, nor can he close his jaw, and his tongue stumbles in speech. But if the dislocation is slight, it often returns of its own accord very easily.
But if the dislocation is complete, it should be reduced quickly and not delayed at all.  The attendant should hold the head of the patient, and the physician should insert the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside his mouth, if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
or he should insert both thumbs if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, and the rest of his fingers from outside to adjust it, and he should tell the patient to relax his jaw and allow it to go to every direction, and the physician should adjust the jaw and push the jaw until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, then use a warm water and oil compress until their reduction is easy, and do not delay their reduction at all, as we said. When they have returned and become straight, and the mouth of the patient is closed and they are not relaxed, then apply to them bandages of linen with a plaster made of wax, oil, and rosewater.
Then bind it gently with a loose bandage, and let the patient sleep on his back with his head raised between two pillows so that it is not moved to the right or left, and he should not strain to chew anything, but his food should be liquid and soft.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw is set, let him eat whatever he wishes, and let him use it gently and not strain to open his mouth when eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is set and healed.
If the reduction of the jaws is difficult, if they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their places, then fevers and constant headaches often result from this, and perhaps the patient's belly will become distended, and perhaps he will vomit pure bile. If you see this, then know that it is corrupted.
And many die from this within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On the reduction of the clavicle and the tip of the shoulder.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate from the inner side because of its connection to the chest.  It may dislocate outwards, and this is evident to the touch. Its reduction is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms.
Then press the place with your hand firmly, and it will return. Then apply a bandage and pads and bind it. As for its end which is next to the shoulder and connected to it, it rarely dislocates; but if it dislocates at any time, then it should be reduced and set as we have mentioned and as is possible for you.
Then apply a bandage and pads and bind it, and instruct the patient to observe rest and quiet until he recovers.  With this same treatment, the tip of the shoulder is reduced if it also leaves its place.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In the reduction of the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: one is that it dislocates towards the armpit downwards; the second is that it dislocates towards the chest; and it may also dislocate above the shoulder, but this is rare.
It does not dislocate to the rear because of the shoulder blade, nor does it dislocate to the front because of the nerve. It most often dislocates and goes down towards the armpit, especially in those whose flesh is thin, because it comes out quickly in them and goes back in quickly.
But as for those whose flesh is abundant, it is otherwise; that is to say, it comes out with difficulty and goes back in with difficulty.  And it may happen to some people that a blow or a fall causes the shoulder to swell with a hot swelling, so that one thinks it has dislocated; it is necessary, therefore, to examine this until you ascertain its truth.
Then you proceed to its treatment.  The dislocation is recognized if it is downwards towards the armpit, for if you compare the dislocated shoulder with the sound shoulder, you find a clear difference between them, and you find the head of the shoulder to be displaced, and under the armpit, on touching, the head of the shoulder is like an egg.
And the patient is unable to raise his hand to his ear, nor to move it in all movements. Similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, you find this apparent to the touch, not hidden.
And this dislocation is easily reduced if it is recent or if the patient is a child.  Its reduction is that a servant raises his hand upwards, then you place your thumbs under his armpit and raise the joint forcibly upwards to its place, while the servant raises his hand and extends it upwards.
Then he lets it down; then it returns quickly. If it does not return by what we have mentioned, and the dislocation has been some days, then the patient should bathe in hot water, and use things which relax and soften, such as cooking marshmallow root, fenugreek, and rue in water and applying it.
Then the patient lies on his back, and you place under his armpit a ball of wool, which is moderate between soft and hard. Then the physician places his heel on the ball, and holds the patient's hand with his hand, and extends it downwards.
And he pushes the ball with his foot; then it returns to its place. And if you wish to reduce it in this way, you bring a man taller than the patient and station him on the side, and he puts his shoulder under the patient's armpit and raises his armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand downwards towards his belly. If the patient is light, then something else should be hung on him to weigh him down; then the jaw returns immediately. And it may also be reduced in another way.
And that is to fix in the ground a long piece of wood, the head of which is round like the pestle of a mortar, neither thick nor thin. Then it is placed under the patient's armpit, after you have placed a soft cloth on the head of the wood, and the patient stands along the length of the wood.
Then you extend his hand downwards from the other side, and he also extends his body from the other side forcefully; then the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult to reduce it by all that we have mentioned, then use this treatment: you take a piece of wood about two cubits long. And its width is about four fingers and its thickness about two fingers; it has a round head to make it easier to enter deep into the armpit in this manner:
Then you tie a soft cloth on the round head, lest the wood hurt the patient. Then it is placed under the armpit, and the whole hand and arm are extended on the wood downwards, and the wood is tied to the upper arm, forearm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a crossbeam. The body is suspended by the crossbeam and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side. Then the joint enters immediately.  If it enters in any way possible, then a moderately sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then the prepared bandage of mill dust, frankincense, and egg white is applied to the whole shoulder as it extends upwards, then the ball is firmly tied under the armpit, and the bandage is wound over it from above, and his hand is suspended to his neck.
He is left without moving his hand for seven days, and the patient's food should be light so that the limb may strengthen and heal more quickly. Then, after seven or five days, it is untied and exercised; if it is firm and does not weaken, then it is healed.
If the joint dislocates repeatedly due to dampness or other cause, cauterization with three cauteries should be used as previously described in the chapter on cauterization. If all this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days and the joint is not stable,
the bandage and binding are repeated several times, but it does not stabilize, and it falls and weakens, and you cannot raise it upwards. Know then that the nerve in the head of the humerus is severed, stretched or weakened. Know then that the joint will never stabilize in its place.
As for a dislocation towards the chest and breast and backwards, it is reduced by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns, and the rest of the binding and treatment are used until it heals. If after healing there is stiffness in the limb and slowness of movement,
the patient should use the bath repeatedly until that stiffness softens and returns to its original nature.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
In the treatment of elbow dislocation.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and likewise returns with difficulty also.  It dislocates in all directions, especially forwards or backwards. Its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is visible and palpable, whichever way it is dislocated.
And if you compare the dislocated elbow with the sound one, this will be clearly evident to you by the deformity of the joint; and he cannot bend the arm nor touch his shoulder with it.  You should hasten and reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot inflammation occurs.
For if a hot inflammation occurs, its reduction is difficult, and perhaps it will not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is backwards; for this is the worst of all types of dislocation, and the most painful, and often death ensues.
And its reduction, if it is possible to be reduced, is that an assistant extends his hand with both hands and his arm extended, and the physician's hands are above and below the elbow, and he pushes the joint with the thumbs of both hands, or with the base of his palms, until it returns to its place.
And as for if the dislocation is forward, it may be reduced by bending the arm sharply until the base of the hand strikes the corresponding shoulder; and if the dislocation does not return, then use very strong traction, which is that two assistants extend the arm.
And two assistants also hold the patient so that he does not move during the traction; then the arm is rotated in every direction after wrapping a folded cloth or a wide bandage around his hand. And if the physician touches the joint with his hands, he should lubricate them with ointment so that it will help in easily sliding the joint.
Then he pushes the joint strongly until it returns; and after its return, it is necessary to apply a bandage containing astringents and desiccant with egg white, and it is bound tightly, and the arm is hung from the patient's neck, and it is left for several days.
Then it is untied; and if the joint is fixed in its place, then untie the bandage from it and leave it. And if you see that the joint is not strengthened well, then reapply the bandage and leave it also for several days until it is strengthened, then untie it. And if a swelling occurs after the return of the joint and its untying,
and difficulty in movement occurs, then use moistening in the bath and gentle massage and rubbing until it softens; or place a fat ram's caul on the joint, then bind it and leave it on for a day and night, then remove it and put him in the bath.  And when he sweats, massage the joint moderately.
Then reapply the caul once, twice, and three times with the bath until it softens. And if you wish to apply a moist, thin ox caul with fat and bind it on, do that several times, for it will soften and return to its former state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
In the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist often dislocates, and its reduction is easier than that of other joints, except that it is necessary to hasten its reduction at the time it dislocates, before the place becomes swollen or a hot swelling appears.
To reduce it, place the patient's wrist on a board and have an assistant extend his hand. The physician should place his hand on the prominence of the joint and push it until it returns. However, he should look to see if the dislocation has gone towards the palm of the hand.
If the dislocation is prominent towards the back of the hand, let him place the palm of his hand on the board during extension and reduction. If the dislocation is prominent toward the back of the hand, let him place the back of his hand on the board so that the physician's hand falls on the prominence of the joint itself. If it returns immediately, well and good; otherwise, bind it with a bandage that soothes the swelling.
And leave it; do not manipulate it again, for it will not tolerate it, and you will not be able to reduce it after a few days have passed, except that the joint will remain crooked, and it will not harm the patient except if the hand becomes weak and unable to grasp anything.
Then you will know that a nerve has been severed or bruised; there is no remedy for this except cauterization, which may or may not help.  When the wrist is reduced, apply the bandage we described, then tighten it and leave it for five days.
Then unbind it and exercise the hand. If its movement is difficult and there is any stiffness, soften it with warm water and massage repeatedly until it softens.
Chapter twenty-nine.
In the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may be dislocated in any direction. If a finger is dislocated towards the back or palm of the hand, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns. Then bind the tip of the finger and suspend it towards the direction in which it was dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it is straight that day. If it is night, bind it in the same way; continue to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
Do the same if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; bind it towards that same direction and do as you did before until it heals.  Do the same if it is dislocated in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
In the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
If a vertebra of the vertebrae of the back or neck is dislocated, a complete dislocation, or many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, then there is no treatment, for death quickly comes to the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's stool is passed involuntarily; he cannot restrain it, and often some of his limbs become relaxed, either his legs or his arms, or one of them.  But if one vertebra is displaced from its place, it often returns.
And its displacement is in four directions; that which is displaced backward is called a hump. As for its treatment, you should look; if the hump has occurred from youth, then there is no treatment for it, nor is there any cure at all. But that which has occurred from a fall or a blow, and the like.
The ancients mentioned it with various treatments in lengthy descriptions, most of which is not beneficial. I have shortened this, so that a little of it suffices for much of what they brought, by approaching the meaning and explaining it, and I have depicted the instrument differently from what they explained and described.
So I say that the hump which occurs in the front of the chest has no remedy and no cure for it; and the same for those on both sides also. Only that which occurs in the back is treated, specifically with what I describe, which is that you extend the patient on his face.
on a level bench near a wall, and spread a wet mat underneath so that his chest is not harmed. Then place an upright wooden post fixed in a hole in the ground at one end of the bench towards his head, and another wooden post towards his feet at the other end of the bench.
And a servant holds the post, and let it not be secured in the hole, and another servant holds the other in that manner. Then wrap around the patient's chest and under his armpits with a soft, tight bandage, and extend the end of the bandage to the post at his head and tie it there.
Then tighten with another bandage above his hips and above his knees and at his ankles. Then gather all the bandages and tie them to the other post at his feet. Then each servant pulls his post with the bandage, and the two posts do not move from their fixed positions.
Except that they are not fixed, as we said. And the physician places his hands on the hump with force until it returns, or he places a board on it, then leans on the board with his feet until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three cubits.
And dig into the wall, which we said should be near the patient, a place into which the end of the board is inserted. Then place the middle of the board on the hump, and the physician places his feet on the other end and pulls strongly until he compresses the hump.
And it returns to its place. And if you wish to make it with a screw that is twisted by hand, it is that you plant in the ground at the head of the patient at the end of the shop two posts, their length being a cubit, and at his feet at the end of the shop also two posts.
Let the distance between each post be a span. And a hole is made in each post through which the screw passes. And all the wood is firmly fixed in the ground so that it does not move at all. And insert a round rod, which is the screw in which the bandage is twisted, into the holes of the two posts.
And at its end is a hole in which a rod a span long is fixed with what is twisted. And in the other two posts likewise. Then tighten the bandage which you tightened on the chest of the patient in the screw which is at his head, and the bandages which you tightened on his legs in the screw which is at his feet.
Then a servant stands at each screw, twisting his hand with the handle with which he twists the screw, and the physician levels the hump as we have previously explained. And this is an image of the screw, the shop, and the patient:
Then, after the vertebra returns and the place is level, it should be necessary to apply a dry bandage with egg white, then a splint, then place on top of the bandage a splint of a board whose width is three fingers or thereabouts, and its length is as much as takes the place of the hump.
And on some sound beads, then it is bound with the appropriate bandage, and the patient uses light food until he recovers. If some protrusion remains in the place at the end of healing, then it is necessary to use medicines that relax and soften, along with the use of the board we described for a long time.
And a lead plate may be used in that. And a protrusion may appear at the end of the vertebrae of the back, and it is thought to be a dislocation, and that is an extra bone that has protruded. Do not treat it with this treatment, for perhaps death will occur.
Chapter thirty-one.
In the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and they are not subject to what happens to the other joints of slight displacement and depression, and the hip joint is dislocated in four ways.
That is, it is dislocated inwards, outwards, forwards, and backwards, and most often it is dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards. And the sign of its dislocation inwards is that if you compare the sound leg of the patient with the affected leg, it will be longer.
And the knee will be more prominent than the sound one, and the patient will not be able to bend his leg at the groin, and the area next to the groin will be clearly swollen because the head of the femur has gone there.
And the sign of that which is dislocated outwards is that its symptoms are the opposite of these symptoms. And the sign of that which is dislocated forwards is that he extends his leg completely, but he cannot bend it without pain being in the knee, and if he wants to walk, he cannot do so forwards.
And his urine is retained, and his groin is swollen, and when walking, his footfall is on the heel. And the sign of that which is dislocated backwards is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the groin, and his leg is also shorter than the other.
And the groin is relaxed, and the head of the femur is clearly at the position of the loin. As for the reduction of the types of this dislocation, you should look, for if the dislocation is old, has become chronic with its sufferer, and its reduction has not been attempted, and it has remained in its state, then there is absolutely no treatment for it.
It should not be exposed to it. As for that which is recently dislocated, and whatever type it is of the four aspects of dislocation, then hasten to twist the joint, and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it right and left, perhaps...
it returned and did not need any other treatment. If it does not return like this, then it is necessary to prepare a strong, capable assistant who will extend his leg from below, either with his hands or with a bandage tied around his leg above the knee, and another assistant who will extend it from above.
by inserting his hands under his armpits, then tightening a soft bandage around the top of the thigh, and another third assistant holding the end of the bandage, and his extension is either from the front, from the groin towards the clavicle, or from behind towards the back.
And their extension is all at once, until the patient is raised with his body from the ground and remains suspended. For this type of extension is a common type for the four types. If the dislocation returns as we have said, otherwise, each type needs what I mention of specific treatment.
As for its specific reduction, if the dislocation is inwards, then the patient should lie on his sound side, then a bandage is applied to the top of the thigh between the head of the thigh and the area under the groin.
Then the bandage is extended upwards from the groin towards the upper body towards the clavicle, then another strong, capable assistant takes it with his arms and embraces the thick part of the affected thigh and extends it outwards with a strong pull, then it returns to its place.
And this type is easier than all the other types of treatment by which this member is reduced. If it is difficult for you and it does not respond to this type of treatment at all, then the patient's legs should be tied together with a strong, soft bandage on the heels and on the knees.
And the distance between each and its fellow is four fingers, and the affected leg is extended more than the other by two fingers. Then the patient is suspended by the head from a wooden beam that is in the house, and his distance from the ground is two arms' lengths.
Then you order a strong boy to embrace the head of the thigh, and another boy hangs onto the patient, and the other boy embracing the thigh pushes strongly, then the joint returns to its place quickly.  As for its specific reduction if the dislocation is outwards…
The patient should lie on the bench as we described for the hunchback, and the bandage is tightened on his affected leg in particular and on his chest. Then the two pieces of wood are placed, one at his feet and the other at his head. Then an extra piece of wood is placed in the middle of the bench, very firmly secured.
Wet rags are wrapped around it so that it does not harm the patient. The wood is placed between his thighs so that he does not slip downwards when pulled. Then each servant pulls towards his side, and the physician sets the hip with his hands. If it responds to reduction…
Otherwise, place the board on him and press it down as we mentioned for the hunchback, except that the patient should lie on his sound side. And if the dislocation is forwards, then the patient's leg should be extended once he is in this same position on the bench.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected hip, then presses it with his other hand, and with that he makes the pressure extended downwards towards the knee. If the dislocation is backwards, then the patient should not be extended downwards.
He is raised off the ground, but should be placed on something firm.  As should also be the case if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we mentioned of his lying on the bench on his face, and the bandages tightened.
As we said before, and the pressure with the board should also be used on the place to which the joint has gone. When the hip joint has returned completely to its proper place—and the sign of its return will not be hidden from you— you extend the patient's legs.
If you see them even, and the patient bends and extends the leg without difficulty, then know that the member has returned to its proper place. Then, bring the thighs together, apply the bandage, and bind it with a turban firmly so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain still for three or four days. Then untie the bandage, and compare the leg with the other. If you see them equal in size, then know that the fracture has healed, and allow the patient to walk.  If you see any relaxation in it,
then return and bandage and tighten it according to your first tightening, and leave it also for three days. Then untie it, and let him walk slowly on it for days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-two.
In the treatment of fracture of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways: inwards, outwards, and downwards—that is, backwards; it is never dislocated forwards.  The sign of its dislocation is that you order the patient to bring his leg to his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, then know that the knee is dislocated. The method of setting all types of its dislocation is that you seat the patient sitting with his legs extended if he is able to do so, and a servant sits behind him to hold his waist and twist him slightly backwards, then you sit on his thighs.
And you place your back against his face and put his leg between your legs, then you grasp his knee with your hands and interlock them between your fingers on his knee, then you press the sides of his knee strongly with your hands, and another servant extends his leg until the knee returns to its place.
The sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh softly without constraint. Then bandage it and fasten the leg to the thigh, then bind them both with a bandage for three or four days, then release them.  He should only do a little walking for a few days until it strengthens.
If you are unable to return it by what we have described, then use strong traction with the bandages which I have previously described in the treatment of the hip until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the treatment of a dislocated heel.
The heel may be displaced slightly, or it may be completely dislocated.  Its dislocation is either inwards or outwards, and the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen and prominent on the side to which it is dislocated.
As for the treatment of its slight displacement, it is easy to reduce it; this is done by gently extending it with the hands and straightening it until it returns.  As for its treatment if it is completely dislocated, then the patient should sit down, and a strong servant should hold him from behind his back at his waist.
Then you grasp his foot with your right hand from above, and with your left hand from below the foot at the place of the heel; then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left twice; then extend it with your left hand and push the front of the foot with your right towards the leg without violence.
Do this twice as we have described; then push the front of the foot towards the leg on the third time while you are pulling at the heel. If it returns in one or two times in this way and you see the foot is straight, otherwise repeat the procedure, for it will return.
If it refuses to return by what we have described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground, and fix a strong wedge in the ground between his thighs, having wrapped a cloth around it so that it does not hurt the patient; then a servant should hold his thigh.
Then another servant extends the leg, either with his hands or with a rope tied around the neck of the leg; then each servant extends in the opposite direction to his companion, and the wedge remains between the patient's thighs, holding him so that his body does not be pulled downwards during the extension; then the physician straightens the joint with his hands.
And another servant holds the healthy leg below, for the joint returns quickly; when the joint has returned and you have ascertained the correctness of its return, apply the compress and bandage, and tighten it with firm bandages, and bind the foot with a bandage below; and you should guard against the nerve which is above the heel from behind.
lest the bandage be tight upon it and hurt him; then leave it two or three days; if the bandage loosens, tighten it, then loosen it on the third or fourth day; and the patient must abstain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this time, you cannot be sure that the joint will not be dislocated again and become spoiled, and will not accept treatment afterwards; if a hot swelling appears, you should use for its alleviation what we have previously described in another place in the treatment and cleansing, until it disappears.
Chapter thirty-four.
In the treatment of a dislocated toe.
You should set what is dislocated with a slight extension without force, and this is not difficult, but easy; if the dislocation is in some of the joints of the instep, the patient should place his foot on a level surface of the ground.
Or on a board while he is standing as if walking, then you stand and place your foot on the dislocated joint, then press down on it forcefully until it returns and you see that it is straight and there is no prominence in the place.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot that takes the whole foot, having two ends, then bind it tightly and firmly for three days, then unbind it and keep him from walking for many days until it strengthens and you are safe from recurrence, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On the types of dislocation which occurs with a wound, or with a fracture, or with both together.
When something of that happens and you delay its treatment and setting, it often ends in death.
Therefore, no one should undertake the treatment of such a thing except one who is skillful in the craft, long experienced, gentle, compassionate, careful, not rash or impetuous, and that he should use in the beginning only those medicines which soothe hot swellings and leave the patient alone.
As for the power of God, except what you hope for its safety from harm with the mildness of the disease and some hope appears to you in it, then return it from your hand at the first, before the hot swelling happens.
If the limb returns to what you want, then use the treatment which soothes hot swellings and treat the wound with what is suitable for it from drying ointments; if the dislocation is with a fracture and there are detached splinters in the bone, then remove them. And follow in that what we mentioned in the simple diseases, what preceded in their places.
And strive your best and avoid entering the path of risk, as I have advised you before, for that is more lasting for your reputation and safer for your honor, God willing.
